# Cartography K

Thank you to Anshul Ganta, Ayan Mahajan, Craig O'Sullivan, Gavin
Schmidt, Rishad Vaghaiwalla, Sam McLaughlin, Seth Small, and Sopha
Lutska, for your work on this file.

Special thanks to Sydney Pasquinelli for directing, editing, and shaping
this K and our research.

Some generic cards have been taken from other Michigan files (The CCFMLP
Colonialism K and the K Lab Necropolitics K) just to make it easier for
people to make speeches without having to open a bunch of docs, so shout
out to those labs for their cards.

## 1NCs

### 1NC\-\--short

#### The AFF is inscribed within a Eurocentric notion of "the Arctic" -- their entitlement to explore and develop the arctic relies upon a colonial cartography that was designed by and for colonial powers.

**Ashcroft et al. '25.** Bill Ashcroft et al. PhD, Emeritus Professor in
the School of English, Media and Performing Arts. A founding exponent of
post-colonial theory, co-author of The Empire Writes Back, author and
co-author of twenty one books and 200 chapters and papers. Received the
Fellow of the Australian Academy of the Humanities award, the Global
Award of Academic Excellence award, the Australian Research Council --
Australian Professorial Fellowship award, the Distinguished researcher
award University of Hong Kong award, the ITET Fellowship award, the
Distinguished Visiting Professor, Beijing Normal University award, the
Distinguished Visiting Fellowship, University of Adelaide award, the
McRae Russell Prize for work of Australian criticism (Shortlist) award,
the Crawford Prize (PhD Thesis), the Colin Roderick Prize for Australian
Literature (MA Thesis); "Post-Colonial Studies: The Key Concepts (4th
ed.)" p. 49-51, Routledge, 2025; \\\\\\ SDM

CARTOGRAPHY (MAPS AND MAPPING) Both literally and metaphorically, [maps
and mapping are dominant practices of colonial and post-colonial
cultures]{.underline}. [Colonization itself is often consequent on a
voyage of 'discovery', a bringing into being of 'undiscovered'
lands]{.underline}. The process of [discovery is reinforced by the
construction of maps,]{.underline} [whose existence is a means of
textualizing the spatial reality of the other,]{.underline} naming or
,in almost all cases, [renaming spaces in a symbolic and literal act of
mastery and control]{.underline}. In all cases [the lands so colonized
are **literally reinscribed**, written over,]{.underline} as [the names
and languages of the indigenes are replaced]{.underline} by new names,
[or are **corrupted** into new and Europeanized forms by the
cartographer]{.underline} and explorer. [This was a long-standing
feature of colonialism]{.underline} and not restricted to distant
unknown lands. Irish playwright Brian Friel graphically illustrates this
in Translations, which deals with [the mapping and renaming of the West
of Ireland by the English]{.underline} army in the nineteenth century,
[a process in which the native Irish (Gaelic) culture is literally
overwritten by English imperialism.]{.underline} [Maps also **inscribe
their ideology** on territory in numerous way]{.underline}s other than
place-names. [The blank spaces of early maps signify a literal **terra
nullius**, an open and inviting (**virginal**) space into which the
European imagination can project itself and]{.underline} into which [the
European (usually male) explorer must **penetrate**]{.underline}. Such
[blank spaces invite other cultural superscriptions, such as the
elaborately drawn monsters and **sub-human wild-men** (savages) of most
early maps]{.underline}. Imaginative transferences are frequent. Thus
[early maps of Australia]{.underline} (Terra Incognita or Terra
Incognita Australis as it was known in the seventeenth century when the
first European maps of the northern coast-line were drawn) [show an
interior populated by elephants and pygmies]{.underline}. Similarly [the
so-called cannibals of the Indies]{.underline} (a term derived from the
corruption of the Spanish name for the Carib peoples) [appear frequently
on maps of the interior of Africa]{.underline}. Indeed[,]{.underline} by
the early twentieth century, the term is almost synonymous with Africans
in popular literature and humour, as witness the numerous cartoons that
represent cannibals as black people with bones through their noses
roasting missionaries in a pot. [The fictionality of the narratives of
such processes of 'discovery', which the process of mapping objectifies,
is emphasized by the role of the native guide in such explorations, who
leads the explorer]{.underline} to the interior. [The prior knowledge of
the land that this dramatizes, and which **cannot be wholly silenced**
in the written accounts of these explorations, **is ignored, and
literally silenced** by the act of mapping]{.underline}, [since the
indigenous people **have no voice** or even presence that can be heard
in the new discourse of scientific measurement and written texts that
cartography implies.]{.underline} [They figure, if at all, only as
illustration -- savages, cannibals or monsters. The allegorization of
space (and its hierarchization) is also defined by maps, first by the
decision as to which projection to favour,since this privileges some
land-spaces over others]{.underline}; or [by the decision to orient all
maps on a North--South axis]{.underline}; or [by the creation of a body
of special maps that divide the land into such 'objective' categories as
climatic regions, population densities and, not least, natural
resources.]{.underline} [Maps can also serve as (allegorical) **tools of
exploitation**]{.underline}.In his analysis of Mercator's Atlas, José
Rabasa (1993) demonstrates how [the projection map became a major **tool
of Euro-centrism**, defining European latitudes as pivotal reference
points for the conception of a **world hierarchy**,]{.underline} and
[embodying as geographical fact European attitudes about]{.underline}
the nature of [the world]{.underline}. In effect, [the European map
created]{.underline} what has remained [the contemporary geographical
world reality.]{.underline} [The definition of cultures as tropical in a
world where temperate is the norm]{.underline}, [or]{.underline} as
[densely populated or thinly populated]{.underline},[or]{.underline} as
[resource rich or poor]{.underline}, [are essential discriminations upon
which]{.underline} a practice of [exploitation or **control** can be
erected]{.underline}. That [these techniques also feature in such modern
controlling **discourses as 'development'**]{.underline} studies
[illustrates how persistently the sciences of cartography and mapping
have contributed to the establishment and reestablishment of the
**various world orders**]{.underline} of the last few centuries.
[Although ethnography]{.underline} (the supposedly objective scientific
description of 'primitive'peoples and cultures) [has frequently borne
the brunt of anti-colonial attacks]{.underline} as being the principal
intellectual discourse of colonization, [it is worth noting that
geography and geographers,and the science of **cartography, played at
least as important a role in** underpinning the objects and values of
**the colonial enterprise**]{.underline}. The Royal Geographical Society
was a prime mover in the imperial conquests of the 'undiscovered'
regions of the world, and it is significant that as Kipling's Kim
illustrates (the cover of the Head of the Secret Service in that text,
Creighton Sahib, is that of the Director of the General Survey of
India), [**the colonial mapper** and surveyor **was** frequently **the
most ubiquitous figure of imperial control**.]{.underline}

#### Cartography [destroys]{.underline} the territory is seeks to [dominate]{.underline}

Azza **Harras 25**, PhD in Postcolonial Studies, Assistant Professor in
the Department of English, Culture, and Communication at the Royal
Military College of Canada, "Varieties of imperial imaginary of the
Arctic and the Congo in Lady Franklin of Russell Square and Heart of
Darkness," p. 257-371, Journal of Postcolonial Writing (a peer-reviewed
academic journal published by Routledge), v. 61, published 3/3/2025
\\\\\\ SDM

> [The territory no longer precedes the map, **nor does it survive
> it**]{.underline}. It is nevertheless [the map that precedes the
> territory]{.underline} -- precession of simulacra -- that engenders
> the territory. (Baudrillard Citation1995, 1)

[Baudrillard's theory]{.underline} of the precession of simulacra that
engenders the territory [highlights Said's notions of geography as a
colonial construct.]{.underline} [Cartography, one of the key concepts
in postcolonial studies, constitutes a significant part of Arctic and
African colonial narratives. Imperial powers have shown great interest
in cartography and charting maps as **manifestations of domination** and
material gain]{.underline}. The [imperial enterprise portrays maps as
parchments with vacant spaces awaiting a **glorious imperial
flag**]{.underline} to give them meaning, identity, and culture.
Canadian and British fiction frequently features maps with several
goals. According to Graham Huggan (Citation1994), featuring maps in
Canadian literature shows the close relationship between historical
development and geographical discovery and allows writers to come to
terms with and celebrate the rediscovery of their country. Maps, seen by
Huggan as a product and a process, because [the map "does not restrict
itself to the decoding of the model, but also involves itself in the
further recoding of the modelling system"]{.underline} (4), are
reflected in Elce and Conrad's texts in nearly identical ways. In one of
the letters imagined by Elce, Jane Franklin starts recollecting her
memories with her husband, mentioning their "old map of London" (Elce
Citation2018, 29) and reminiscing about how they drew the lines of their
travels, with the "routes in and out of the city almost soaked through
with ink" (29). Lady Franklin's use of the map as a compass for her
memories and the couple's sense of geographical imperative is indicative
of imperial ontology. Their map, in this case, is reflective of what
Huggan calls [the "dialectic between the formulation of the map, during
which the real world is concentrated in model form, and the
implementation of the map, during which the model is tested against
reality"]{.underline} (Citation1994, 4). Lady Franklin's and her
husband's act of tracing their travels reflects the process of testing
the map's abstract model against their lived experiences. The ink-soaked
routes symbolize how their personal memories and physical journeys are
inscribed onto the map, transforming it from a mere representation of
London into a record of their experiences, thus highlighting the
relationship between the conceptual model of the map and its real-world
application. [Similarly, the imperial venture of finding the Northwest
Passage was all about geography,]{.underline} although John Franklin's
map towards the Northwest Passage represented a model that could not be
implemented when tested against reality.

#### The alternative is refuse the aff's cartographic practices -- this is a prerequisite for ethical decolonial politics

Daniel **Chartier et. al \'24** \[(Daniel Chartier ; Hanna Guttorm ;
Britt Kramvig ; Berit Kristoffersen ; Johannes Riquet ; and Philip
Steinberg ) \"Decolonial cartographies: Counter-mapping in the arctic,\"
Chapter 7 of The Mediated Arctic, Manchester University Press, Accessed
through UM libraries, pp. 184-185,
https://www.manchesterhive.com/display/9781526174024/9781526174024.00016.xml,
9-24-2024\]

The [North in its entirety can be considered a colonial and militarised
territory, where]{.underline}, nowadays, [decolonial initiatives have
arisen]{.underline}. Among these, several are [rooted in
mapping]{.underline}. [Cartography has been forced upon the land in such
violent ways that the only way forward is to overthrow the practice
itself.]{.underline} Settler colonialism is not only a political issue;
it also consists in imposing a way of thinking (considered superior)
which devalues and replaces Indigenous cultural practices and
ontologies. [Maps determine the locations of the centre and the fringes,
as well as the ways in which the land is occupied]{.underline}. As
[historically strategic tools]{.underline}, they [make it possible to
claim an entire territory without even setting foot on it]{.underline}.
The [Rupert's Land treaties and]{.underline} the [monopolies held
by]{.underline} the major [colonial companies]{.underline}, such as the
Hudson's Bay Company's hold over what is now the Canadian North, for
instance, [gave precedence to the authority of the word]{.underline} --
by the power vested in me, I hereby declare [that this land belongs to
such and such -- over physical occupation.]{.underline} As Bernard
Nietschmann puts it, '\[[m\]ore indigenous territory has been claimed by
maps than by guns']{.underline} (1994, 37). Indeed, [Europeans took
possession of territories before they set out to explore
them.]{.underline} [Maps are agents of this power and replace the
experience of roaming the land with a cartographic abstraction that will
later serve as proof of property,]{.underline} for instance during
subsequent territorial negotiations. Furthermore, [maps thwart
toponymy]{.underline}, [replacing it]{.underline} sometimes [with the
settler's lexicon, and]{.underline} at other times, rather cynically,
with [something like 'great white expanse', signalling the unknown
(rather than]{.underline} admitting [ignorance]{.underline}) [and
emptiness]{.underline} [(in lieu of acknowledgement]{.underline}). In
the Americas, this [imperialist cartographic violence denies any
recognition of prior Indigenous presence, as acknowledging it would
negate the very vocabulary that Europeans used to claim the
land]{.underline}: 'the New World', 'New England', 'New France', 'the
discovery of America', 'the founding of Canada'. [To allow for the
conqueror's gesture, Indigenous toponymy must be silenced.]{.underline}
This [cartographic overwriting leads to the silencing not only of place
names, but also of prior cultures and practices]{.underline}. [Maps
render the land -- an]{.underline} [essential]{.underline} and concrete
element [for Indigenous cultures]{.underline} -- [abstract,
dematerialising it]{.underline} [and devaluing its empirical
understanding]{.underline}, favouring sedentary life over nomadism.
Geographer and linguist Louis- Edmond Hamelin, who advocated for a
'geography of the feet', calling for trajectories, experiences, and
field observations, writes that '[such condescending behaviour can only
serve to distort the perception of facts]{.underline} on the ground'
(Hamelin, Chartier, and D.sy 2013, 41).3 [For peoples under colonial
rule who]{.underline} still have to [live with the performative efforts
of colonial maps within their governance]{.underline}, such as the Inuit
and the Sámi, [overthrowing cartographic perspectives is a way to
transform the political order.]{.underline}

### 1NC\-\--Long

#### Treating the Arctic as a land for the US to explore and develop are rooted in a Eurocentric history of colonialism, which homogenizes the Arctic and creates the preconditions for colonial and hierarchical violence

**HUGGAN & Norum, 15** (GRAHAM HUGGAN & ROGER NORUM. **Graham Huggan is
Professor of Commonwealth and Postcolonial Literatures at the University
of Leeds, UK. His research spans the entire field of comparative
postcolonial literary/cultural studies, with further interests in the
areas of ecocriticism, travel writing, short fiction, and film, Roger
Norum is a Academy Researcher and Associate Professor in the field of
cultural anthropology, whose research focuses on the comparative and
praxeological dimensions of how mobility, infrastructure, and sociality
mediate human relationships to the environment**, "Introduction: The
Postcolonial Arctic." Moving Worlds: A Journal of Transcultural
Writings-Volume 15 Number 2-2015,
https://www.movingworlds.net/volumes/15/the-postcolonial-arctic, SL)

Never the Muse is absent

from their ways: lyres clash and flutes cry

and everywhere maiden choruses whirling.

They bind their hair in golden laurel and take their holiday.

Neither disease nor bitter old age is mixed

in their sacred blood; far from labor and battle

they live; they escape scandal and litigation

\... those blessed men1

A contemporary of Herodotus, **[the lyric poet Pindar penned these words
during the fifth century BC in an ode to the fabled]{.underline}**
Ὑπερβόρε(ι)οι, or **[Hyperboreans. To the Ancient Greeks, these were a
mythical people who lived beyond the North Wind in a fabulous landscape
of eternal spring, sunlight, and warmth. For them, light perpetual was
cast onto a land of enlightenment, devoid of shadow and full of
possibility.]{.underline}** Indeed, Nietzsche later invokes Pindar's
stanzas to argue that we are all Hyperboreans in that we seek out,
through solitude, a philosophical, hermeneutical happiness -- 'another
beginning', as he suggests -- in order to overcome the decadence and
malady of the modern individual.2 **[While contemporary imaginations of
the Arctic may have turned cooler and darker since the time of the
Greeks, the region continues to be mythologized as an unknown,
inaccessible, and forbidden land, a remote, wild, and often 'othered'
frontier. As an aggregate territory and a phantasy of the public
consciousness, there has been a drive to essentialize this diverse,
contested space in a way that heavily glosses over the complex
geopolitical, (trans)national, cultural and linguistic distinctions that
define it. Its history entangled with various cultural and ideological
representations, the Arctic is disputed as an idea as much as a broad
topographical description we use to orient ourselves.]{.underline}** It
is not merely that the alterities we ascribe to this part of the world
are socially, culturally, and historically embedded. It is that the
Arctic's multiple landscapes -- and its aesthetic, cultural, political,
economic and scientific interests -- are all inexorably intermeshed with
one another.3 Perhaps it is this seduction towards a unifying
singularity that draws in those who seek in the Arctic some measure of
inspiration. **[For if the Arctic has been a space for ideological
projections, it has also served as a physical nexus for thought and
experimentation, creativity and artistry, engagement and collaboration,
excitement and adventure -- for artists, writers, thinkers, scholars,
explorers and travellers. Many journeys North are constructed and
portrayed as odysseys towards self and truth, movements mobilized by the
poetic potential of exploration, both to the ends of the Earth and the
innards of the soul.]{.underline}**4 **[The Arctic's reluctant,
disorienting, and alienating fictional and factual landscapes, whether
characterized by remoteness, loneliness or vulnerability, also hold an
immense power to encourage the bridging of boundaries, categories, and
discourses.]{.underline}** Creative work conceived of in this region and
by its peoples -- or, indeed, through both -- can challenge dominant
analytic and interpretive frameworks, and question prototypical
conceptualizations and beliefs about extreme spaces, and about the
humans (and non-humans) who inhabit them. **[And yet, for many external
observers, the Arctic today is extreme no longer: it is tamed, and more
accessible than ever.]{.underline}** Two thousand five hundred years
ago, Pindar wrote that 'never on foot or ship could you explore the
marvelous road to the feast of the Hyperboreans'.5 Today, Ryanair flies
there direct. **[Changes (climatic, social, commercial) across the
region have further enabled this contested space to exist beyond mere
imagination, promising new access to new people. Encounters with the
Arctic are being re-envisioned today given the increased accessibility
of travel and mobility. This is due not just to us bearing witness to
the materialization of anthropogenic climate change, nor to what has
been called -- in deliberate reference to another colonial hot potato --
a 'scramble'6 for the Arctic, nor yet to new forms of travel and tourism
cropping up in various pockets of the region. It is also due to the
paradox that the more we are empowered to move closer to landscapes and
experiences that defamiliarize us from what we know in our everyday
lives, the more we seek to imagine new unfamiliarities.]{.underline}**
This issue of Moving Worlds is the outcome of a series of talks convened
by Arctic Encounters: Contemporary Travel/Writing in the European High
North, a collaborative and multidisciplinary HERA-funded research
project that explores the ways in which a changing Arctic changes ideas
not just about travel, but about the planet and its inhabitants more
broadly. **[The Arctic itself is very much a moving world. It has come
to stand for much more than its own landscapes. Movement here is an apt
metaphor for a globalizing topography in which immobility becomes more
and more anomaly: we move the Earth (with our chlorofluorocarbons,
fracking and terraforming); the Earth moves us (through its retreating
ice and rising waters). And indeed, if recent scholarly and public
debates around the Anthropocene are any measure, such movements mobilize
us into political and environmental action. Amid this, old contests over
control of Arctic resources and occupation of its territories resurface
as increasingly urgent social issues, and the fight again emerges to
define and (re)claim the Arctic's pasts, present, and futures. As the
Arctic moves forward, so does it become an even grander geography for
the projection of imperial anxieties. That these anxieties are writ
large today is hardly surprising. The history of the Arctic is -- albeit
at different times and in different ways -- one of subjection, both to
the absolutisms of imperial rule and to more locally inflected
colonialisms. That history continues apace, confirming the obdurate
legacies of 'old-style' modes of colonial governance, but also ushering
in the 'new imperialisms']{.underline}**7 **[that are generally
associated with latecapitalist globalization, one epiphenomenon of which
-- all too visible in the Arctic -- is accelerated climate change. If
none of this is in dispute, two questions still remain in the context of
this particular issue. The first is the extent to which the contemporary
Arctic can be seen as 'postcolonial'. The second is the extent to which
the region -- however defined, and there are many possible definitions
-- can benefit from 'postcolonialism': that loose toolkit of approaches,
theories, and methods which, rarely accepted with equanimity, has been
applied to so many other parts of the world. Neither question yields an
easy answer. If the Arctic is postcolonial, then it will only be so if
the term is seen as negating its own prefix; as challenging the very
conditions of colonialism within which it is itself inscribed. And if it
is amenable to postcolonialism, then it will only be so if those
approaches, theories, and methods are taken to their own limits. Indeed,
in some ways the Arctic, which has become central to 'one-world'
ecological debates around planetary stewardship even as it continues to
be socially and politically marginalized, pushes the boundaries of
postcolonial understandings of society, politics, ecology -- and
conceptions of itself. The essays included here engage with these
reflexive dilemmas without falling into the trap that postcolonial
criticism often sets for itself: that of debating 'real-world' issues
that are designed for the application of postcolonial methods, only for
those methods to end up circling back on themselves. One way of
confronting this is to elevate practice above theory.]{.underline}** The
Postcolonial Arctic begins with a moving experiential account by the
British writer Kari Herbert -- daughter of the famous polar explorer Sir
Wally Herbert -- of part of a childhood spent in northern Greenland. The
account is characterized by a love -- of people, of place, of the
multiple fascinations of the Arctic -- that has remained undiminished
over time. It is also remarkably free of nostalgia, recognizing the
harshness, even ugliness, of the far north and the acute social and
environmental problems that have arisen there. These problems are
accentuated in the essay that follows by the award-winning Norwegian
political geographer Tone Huse. Huse **[focuses on the notorious Blok P
-- a now-defunct housing estate in Nuuk, Greenland's capital -- which
becomes the site for a lyrical contemplation on the country's colonial
past. Blok P is perhaps best known for the violence -- suicide and
aggravated assault, domestic and substance abuse -- that happened inside
it. But Huse is more interested in the external violence that created it
in the first place: the violence of central government planning; the
violence of a certain kind of architectural modernism; and, above all,
the violence of colonialism itself. The Arctic's colonial pasts --
multiple, often confused, always internally
differentiated]{.underline}** -- are also the subject of the next four
essays, whether colonialism is refracted through mediatized geopolitics
(Johanne Bruun), the technologies of polar exploration (Marionne
Cronin), threatened indigenous life-ways (Tero Mustonen), or digital
governance regimes (Citt Williams). **[In all four cases, colonialism is
as much of the present as of the past, caught up as it is in the
enduring (albeit historically shifting) symbolic politics that has come
to characterize the region for those who seek to control it rather than
those who wish to inhabit it; for those who want to manipulate it either
as an object of Western knowledge or as an opportunity for resource
extraction -- or both, since the Arctic is a rich symbolic and material
space.]{.underline}** This sense of the symbolic as well as material
importance of the Arctic is maintained in the last two essays: a
visually arresting piece by the Tromsø-based architect, Kjerstin Uhre,
and a rousing cri de coeur by the Cambridge-based environmental
historian, Michael Bravo. Much in the spirit of the issue as a whole,
Bravo **[see]{.underline}**s **[the Arctic in terms of a complex set of
interrelationships between 'old' and 'new' colonialisms -- one that
offers opportunities to colonize others, but also to decolonize
ourselves. Who 'we' are in these particular instances is moot,
and]{.underline}** (as Bravo is well aware**[) the diverse peoples of
the Arctic are fed up with being spoken for by others. But if the
'postcolonial Arctic' is to make any sense, both for these peoples and
for those who see themselves as acting in their best interests, then it
needs to be a fully dialogical space within the wider context of an
increasingly globalized world.]{.underline}**

#### And, the AFF is inscribed within a Eurocentric notion of "the Arctic" -- their entitlement to explore and develop the arctic relies upon a colonial cartography that was designed by and for colonial powers.

**Ashcroft et al. '25.** Bill Ashcroft et al. PhD, Emeritus Professor in
the School of English, Media and Performing Arts. A founding exponent of
post-colonial theory, co-author of The Empire Writes Back, author and
co-author of twenty one books and 200 chapters and papers. Received the
Fellow of the Australian Academy of the Humanities award, the Global
Award of Academic Excellence award, the Australian Research Council --
Australian Professorial Fellowship award, the Distinguished researcher
award University of Hong Kong award, the ITET Fellowship award, the
Distinguished Visiting Professor, Beijing Normal University award, the
Distinguished Visiting Fellowship, University of Adelaide award, the
McRae Russell Prize for work of Australian criticism (Shortlist) award,
the Crawford Prize (PhD Thesis), the Colin Roderick Prize for Australian
Literature (MA Thesis); "Post-Colonial Studies: The Key Concepts (4th
ed.)" p. 49-51, Routledge, 2025; \\\\\\ SDM

CARTOGRAPHY (MAPS AND MAPPING) Both literally and metaphorically, [maps
and mapping are dominant practices of colonial and post-colonial
cultures]{.underline}. [Colonization itself is often consequent on a
voyage of 'discovery', a bringing into being of 'undiscovered'
lands]{.underline}. The process of [discovery is reinforced by the
construction of maps,]{.underline} [whose existence is a means of
textualizing the spatial reality of the other,]{.underline} naming or
,in almost all cases, [renaming spaces in a symbolic and literal act of
mastery and control]{.underline}. In all cases [the lands so colonized
are **literally reinscribed**, written over,]{.underline} as [the names
and languages of the indigenes are replaced]{.underline} by new names,
[or are **corrupted** into new and Europeanized forms by the
cartographer]{.underline} and explorer. [This was a long-standing
feature of colonialism]{.underline} and not restricted to distant
unknown lands. Irish playwright Brian Friel graphically illustrates this
in Translations, which deals with [the mapping and renaming of the West
of Ireland by the English]{.underline} army in the nineteenth century,
[a process in which the native Irish (Gaelic) culture is literally
overwritten by English imperialism.]{.underline} [Maps also **inscribe
their ideology** on territory in numerous way]{.underline}s other than
place-names. [The blank spaces of early maps signify a literal **terra
nullius**, an open and inviting (**virginal**) space into which the
European imagination can project itself and]{.underline} into which [the
European (usually male) explorer must **penetrate**]{.underline}. Such
[blank spaces invite other cultural superscriptions, such as the
elaborately drawn monsters and **sub-human wild-men** (savages) of most
early maps]{.underline}. Imaginative transferences are frequent. Thus
[early maps of Australia]{.underline} (Terra Incognita or Terra
Incognita Australis as it was known in the seventeenth century when the
first European maps of the northern coast-line were drawn) [show an
interior populated by elephants and pygmies]{.underline}. Similarly [the
so-called cannibals of the Indies]{.underline} (a term derived from the
corruption of the Spanish name for the Carib peoples) [appear frequently
on maps of the interior of Africa]{.underline}. Indeed[,]{.underline} by
the early twentieth century, the term is almost synonymous with Africans
in popular literature and humour, as witness the numerous cartoons that
represent cannibals as black people with bones through their noses
roasting missionaries in a pot. [The fictionality of the narratives of
such processes of 'discovery', which the process of mapping objectifies,
is emphasized by the role of the native guide in such explorations, who
leads the explorer]{.underline} to the interior. [The prior knowledge of
the land that this dramatizes, and which **cannot be wholly silenced**
in the written accounts of these explorations, **is ignored, and
literally silenced** by the act of mapping]{.underline}, [since the
indigenous people **have no voice** or even presence that can be heard
in the new discourse of scientific measurement and written texts that
cartography implies.]{.underline} [They figure, if at all, only as
illustration -- savages, cannibals or monsters. The allegorization of
space (and its hierarchization) is also defined by maps, first by the
decision as to which projection to favour,since this privileges some
land-spaces over others]{.underline}; or [by the decision to orient all
maps on a North--South axis]{.underline}; or [by the creation of a body
of special maps that divide the land into such 'objective' categories as
climatic regions, population densities and, not least, natural
resources.]{.underline} [Maps can also serve as (allegorical) **tools of
exploitation**]{.underline}.In his analysis of Mercator's Atlas, José
Rabasa (1993) demonstrates how [the projection map became a major **tool
of Euro-centrism**, defining European latitudes as pivotal reference
points for the conception of a **world hierarchy**,]{.underline} and
[embodying as geographical fact European attitudes about]{.underline}
the nature of [the world]{.underline}. In effect, [the European map
created]{.underline} what has remained [the contemporary geographical
world reality.]{.underline} [The definition of cultures as tropical in a
world where temperate is the norm]{.underline}, [or]{.underline} as
[densely populated or thinly populated]{.underline},[or]{.underline} as
[resource rich or poor]{.underline}, [are essential discriminations upon
which]{.underline} a practice of [exploitation or **control** can be
erected]{.underline}. That [these techniques also feature in such modern
controlling **discourses as 'development'**]{.underline} studies
[illustrates how persistently the sciences of cartography and mapping
have contributed to the establishment and reestablishment of the
**various world orders**]{.underline} of the last few centuries.
[Although ethnography]{.underline} (the supposedly objective scientific
description of 'primitive'peoples and cultures) [has frequently borne
the brunt of anti-colonial attacks]{.underline} as being the principal
intellectual discourse of colonization, [it is worth noting that
geography and geographers,and the science of **cartography, played at
least as important a role in** underpinning the objects and values of
**the colonial enterprise**]{.underline}. The Royal Geographical Society
was a prime mover in the imperial conquests of the 'undiscovered'
regions of the world, and it is significant that as Kipling's Kim
illustrates (the cover of the Head of the Secret Service in that text,
Creighton Sahib, is that of the Director of the General Survey of
India), [**the colonial mapper** and surveyor **was** frequently **the
most ubiquitous figure of imperial control**.]{.underline}

#### Impact: Cartography is made to appear neutral, but it serves a political function: it is a form of intellectual colonization that justifies the epistemological and material erasure of indigenous life.

**Kumar '23,** Professor of International Relations and Global Politics,
Department of Political Science, University of Delhi; Sanjeev Kumar H.M.
Decolonizing Grand Theories: Postcolonial Ontology, Historical Sociology
and Mid‑Level Theories in International Relations. Palgrave Macmillan
Singapore, September 8 2023. <https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-99-4841->
Craig

This book begins by reading into the [decolonial argument that the
**colonial** matrix of power largely tends to transform modernity into
an instrument of domination which manifested in the colonization of
**political cartographies**.]{.underline} [As regards the colonial
matrix of power is concerned, it is characterized by the
operationalization of **ontological** and **episte- mological** binaries
and hierarchies that was engendered by the unequal politico-economic and
**psycho**-cultural relations between the colonizing metropolitan core
and the colonized periphery. Such an unequal rela- tionship of
**domination** and **subjugation** emerged as crucial elements in
determining the structural/functional conditionalities of the working of
international relations, as well as the epistemologies that attempted to
explain the same. It happened as the grand theories of International
Rela- tions that have assumed the status of the disciplinary canon
tended to impart a kind of epistemological legitimacy to the unequal
relationship between the Western **metropolitan** core and the
non-Western periphery. Such a legitimacy was mediated through the
**hegemonic** epistemolog- ical and pedagogical practices of the grand
theories of **IR**. In this way, the grand theories in IR tended to
demonstrate a kind of deep-seated neocolonial imperial attributes that
are disseminated across the world as axiological postulates of the
**metaphysics** of cosmopolitanism. Considering this, it may be stated
that any satisfactory explanation of the nature of the working of IR,
hence must involve an epistemological engagement with the
**postcolonial** **ontology**.]{.underline} Such an engagement manifests
as an exigency vii viii PREFACE when we begin unpacking the ontic and
epistemic foundations of the disciplinary matrix of the subject of IR.
[As we pursue this very process of **mapping**, it becomes evident that
the disciplinary matrix of IR has largely evolved by way of treading a
teleolog- ical trajectory of synchronic **Eurocentric** and
**ethnocentric** history. Owing to such a teleological trajectory, the
scope and subject matter of IR has deeply grounded itself into a
paradigmatic thrust on Western ontic and epistemic **supremacism**. With
such a supremacist epistemological asser- tion, the world considerably
gets interpreted from the mere lens of the stagiest doctrinal progress
of European history.]{.underline} By virtue of such a West- centric and
ethnocentric intellectual project, a section of the theory in the
disciplinary matrix of IR has incarnated into the status of a canon. On
its part, the elite minority of the epistemic community of IR, who are
the treasurers of such a canon, have been hypostatized into the status
of the apostles. [The **hegemony** of the discourse of such a canon and
the hierarchy of the theoreticians privy to such a discourse tends to be
medi- ated through the universalizing hegemonic tropes of knowledge that
are embodied in the 'grand theories' of IR.]{.underline} Hence, for any
project that aims at making the discipline of IR more cosmopolitan in
character that would commend a wider contextual significance, the
dismantling of the theistic doctrinal hegemony and the primacy of the
sacrosanct position of grand theories of IR manifest as pressing
disciplinary imperatives. [Such an enterprise in the act of dismantling
seems to have become inevitable, mainly because the grand theories are
largely situated in a kind of an **axiological** postulate of the need
for the substantiation of the meta-histories that are constituted
through an epistemic focus upon the local history of Western
civilization. This kind of focus on the **local** history of **Europe**
renders world history to appear as a mere **reductionist** synchronic
narrative. It is in such a narrow ontic narrative on history and the
supremacism of Western epistemology that the meta-theoretical
foundations of the discipline of IR tend to be pivoted. The tropes of
knowledge that are produced in such a circumstance considerably shape
the pedagogical and epistemological processes in the discipline of
Interna- tional Relations.]{.underline} Such a heuristic
over-determinism that characterizes the technical discourse of the
discipline of International Relations is firmly grounded in the
ontological roots of a Eurocentric interpretation of the past and an
ethnocentric definition of the epistemology of IR. This kind of
disciplinary ontology is propelled by what has been desig- nated as IR's
Westphalian common-sense. For such a common-sense, our PREFACE ix
present-day international society has directly descended from the
universal European system. Owing to this, the technical discourse of the
disci- pline of IR has largely tended to be shaped in terms of the
grammar of the Westphalian conception of international order. By virtue
of this, the paradigm of Westphalia tends to act as the core ontological
principle driving the formation of grand theories in IR. What gets
erased in such an epistemological trajectory is the very diachronic
history of international relations that is constituted by the pres- ence
of a world that is outside Europe. Such a world that is designated as
the postcolonial spatio-temporal topoi seems to have been regarded as a
disjunctive structural imaginary, a historical break and an epistemolog-
ical lag by the dominant hegemonic technical discourse of the discipline
of IR. Considering this kind of epistemology of IR, the task at hand is
to look at historical narratives by situating them in their specific
spatio- temporal idiosyncrasies. To do this, the imperative is to
decolonize the meta-theoretical foundations of IR. In order to do this,
the restruc- turing of the ontological, epistemological, methodological
and theoretical underpinnings of the discipline becomes an exigency. An
attempt has been made in this book to undertake such a task of
restructuring, by interrogating the disciplinary matrix of IR at the
level of meta-theory. This interrogation has been situated largely on
the plank of ontology and it is done by critically examining the working
of grand theories in IR. Based on this, a case has been made in this
book for placing mid-level theories at the core of the ontological
concerns of the discipline. Such a thrust on the significance of
mid-level theories, it has been argued in this book, goes a long way in
the restructuring of the disciplinary matrix of IR at the level of
meta-theory. Taking cognizance of this, the book has been designed as a
venture toward committing a kind of disciplinary heresy. This is because
it seeks to question the deep-seated conceptual grammar and the
meta-theoretical structure of the grand theories of the discipline of
IR. Such a struc- ture and content of the grand theories have
considerably contributed to the substantiation of the imperializing
nature of the epistemology of the discipline[. In any case, the origin
and growth of such a process of epis- temological **imperialism**
happened as the product of the genealogies of the West-centric
ethnocentric disciplinary matrix of IR.]{.underline} However, such an
argument seems to be a mere reductionist one that renders a less-than-
perfect explanation for the sustenance of the hegemony of grand theories
x PREFACE that are produced in the West. Rather, the rote
epistemological and peda- gogical practices that have characterized the
structure and content of the disciplinary history of IR seem to have
been equally responsible. Such practices are manifest in the mimetic
reproduction of the arguments of the grand theories that are undertaken
for [the purpose of mapping the terrain of the 'international', a task
that is done by being completely oblivious of the phenomenological
caveat toward the need for suspension of our intentions and the
hermeneutic postulate of recognizing the contextual significance of any
phenomenon.]{.underline} [Such epistemic endeavors factor deeply in
perpetuating the **intellectual** **colonization** of the discipline of
IR by the imperializing heuristic power of the grand
theories.]{.underline} Hence, even at the cost of committing a kind of
apostasy, I dare to question the established canon that has been
disseminated across generations. By doing this, I present to the
readers, my ideas for liberating the discipline of IR from the
colonizing mental yoke of the grand theories. In any case, while the
book undertakes such a task, I wish to render a caveat at the very
outset. I intend to make it very clear here that neither I am aiming to
engage in any kind of a nihilistic endeavor at dismantling a particular
form of theory, nor do I seek to propose any kind of liberation theology
that would venture toward recrafting the disciplinary matrix of IR by
way of scripting its theoretical space on a clean slate. Rather, the
simplest objective in this book is to involve myself in a flirtatious
but not very dilettantish engagement with second-order theorizing. While
I do this, the aim here would be to carry out a rigorous critique of the
grand theories of IR which form part of first-order theorizing. Such a
pursuit of course cannot be undertaken by being oblivious to the need
for adherence to the logic of pedantic precision and methodological
agnosticism.

#### The alternative is refuse the aff's cartographic practices -- this is a prerequisite for ethical decolonial politics

Daniel **Chartier et. al \'24** \[(Daniel Chartier ; Hanna Guttorm ;
Britt Kramvig ; Berit Kristoffersen ; Johannes Riquet ; and Philip
Steinberg ) \"Decolonial cartographies: Counter-mapping in the arctic,\"
Chapter 7 of The Mediated Arctic, Manchester University Press, Accessed
through UM libraries, pp. 184-185,
https://www.manchesterhive.com/display/9781526174024/9781526174024.00016.xml,
9-24-2024\]

The [North in its entirety can be considered a colonial and militarised
territory, where]{.underline}, nowadays, [decolonial initiatives have
arisen]{.underline}. Among these, several are [rooted in
mapping]{.underline}. [Cartography has been forced upon the land in such
violent ways that the only way forward is to overthrow the practice
itself.]{.underline} Settler colonialism is not only a political issue;
it also consists in imposing a way of thinking (considered superior)
which devalues and replaces Indigenous cultural practices and
ontologies. [Maps determine the locations of the centre and the fringes,
as well as the ways in which the land is occupied]{.underline}. As
[historically strategic tools]{.underline}, they [make it possible to
claim an entire territory without even setting foot on it]{.underline}.
The [Rupert's Land treaties and]{.underline} the [monopolies held
by]{.underline} the major [colonial companies]{.underline}, such as the
Hudson's Bay Company's hold over what is now the Canadian North, for
instance, [gave precedence to the authority of the word]{.underline} --
by the power vested in me, I hereby declare [that this land belongs to
such and such -- over physical occupation.]{.underline} As Bernard
Nietschmann puts it, '\[[m\]ore indigenous territory has been claimed by
maps than by guns']{.underline} (1994, 37). Indeed, [Europeans took
possession of territories before they set out to explore
them.]{.underline} [Maps are agents of this power and replace the
experience of roaming the land with a cartographic abstraction that will
later serve as proof of property,]{.underline} for instance during
subsequent territorial negotiations. Furthermore, [maps thwart
toponymy]{.underline}, [replacing it]{.underline} sometimes [with the
settler's lexicon, and]{.underline} at other times, rather cynically,
with [something like 'great white expanse', signalling the unknown
(rather than]{.underline} admitting [ignorance]{.underline}) [and
emptiness]{.underline} [(in lieu of acknowledgement]{.underline}). In
the Americas, this [imperialist cartographic violence denies any
recognition of prior Indigenous presence, as acknowledging it would
negate the very vocabulary that Europeans used to claim the
land]{.underline}: 'the New World', 'New England', 'New France', 'the
discovery of America', 'the founding of Canada'. [To allow for the
conqueror's gesture, Indigenous toponymy must be silenced.]{.underline}
This [cartographic overwriting leads to the silencing not only of place
names, but also of prior cultures and practices]{.underline}. [Maps
render the land -- an]{.underline} [essential]{.underline} and concrete
element [for Indigenous cultures]{.underline} -- [abstract,
dematerialising it]{.underline} [and devaluing its empirical
understanding]{.underline}, favouring sedentary life over nomadism.
Geographer and linguist Louis- Edmond Hamelin, who advocated for a
'geography of the feet', calling for trajectories, experiences, and
field observations, writes that '[such condescending behaviour can only
serve to distort the perception of facts]{.underline} on the ground'
(Hamelin, Chartier, and D.sy 2013, 41).3 [For peoples under colonial
rule who]{.underline} still have to [live with the performative efforts
of colonial maps within their governance]{.underline}, such as the Inuit
and the Sámi, [overthrowing cartographic perspectives is a way to
transform the political order.]{.underline}

#### And, ethical scholars must refuse to participate in colonialism

Clare **Coultas \'21** \[(School of Population Health and Environmental
Sciences, King\'s College London, London, UK) \" Accounting for colonial
complicities through Refusals in researching agency across borders,\"
Society for the Psychological Study of Social Issues,
https://doi.org/10.1111/josi.12473, 8-22-2021\]

[We must remain inquisitively expectant of the blindspots that are
intrinsic to our enduring complicities in whiteness as a
\[neo-\]colonising system of power]{.underline}. As emphasized by Fanon
(1952/2008), [white people and researchers operating in white knowledge
systems, need to cultivate an "attitude," "which is profoundly
epistemological, as well as ethical, political, and
aesthetic]{.underline}\... \[requiring\] that the [subjects of knowledge
question themselves,]{.underline} not about the humanity of others, but
[about the extent to which they are expressing their own humanity \[by
actively countering the continued dehumanisations of people living in
precarious conditions\]]{.underline}" (Maldonado-Torres, 2017, p.
439---emphasis added). [This requires "epistemic modesty"
(]{.underline}Teo, 2019); a value that [means being "aware of one's own
horizon, the strengths and limitations of one's own
approach]{.underline}, [while being knowledgeable about the history,
sociality, and culturality of knowledge."]{.underline} (p. 40).
Developing such an attitude and value depends on us recognizing our
entanglements with the world around us, which also demands that we move
beyond human-centered notions of agency. Singh (2018) locates the
decolonial project in a dehumanist (but importantly, not disembodied)
understanding of agency, viewed through our ''non-masterful
entanglements" with other humans, animals, and the environment; a
practice and a "promise of vulnerability" that remakes the human "from
its outside," from the standpoint of those who have been dehumanized.
[For white people this "remaking" affords an opportunity for the
co-construction of a new positionality, beyond Western binary, fixed,
categorical thinking---a "third space"---that is "guided by" Black,
Indigenous, and people of color knowledges, where we are neither "enemy
or ally," but rather a "concrete subject of struggle"]{.underline}
(Leonardo, 2009, p. 186). Puig de la Bellacasa (2017) recognizes this
reciprocity within more-than-human worlds "through a collective web of
obligations, rather than individual commitments\... \[exposing\] the
multilateral circulation of agencies of care\... \[that\] follows the
requirement of looking at the specificity of moments, particular
relations, of ecologies where the ethical is both personal agency and
embedded in the 'ethos' of a community of living\... \[focussed on the\]
doings and undoings of the ethical embedded in technoscientific
assemblages and naturecultures" (p. 120; 151). Specifically in regard to
universities, paperson (2017) identifies us as "scyborgs" (drawing on
Haraway's concept of the cyborg) that spring from the colonizing
technological assemblage of the university: "The agency of the scyborg
is precisely that it is a reorganizer of institutional machinery; it
subverts machinery against the master code of its makers; it rewires
machinery to its own intentions (p. 55) \... Your agential capacity
extends beyond your being, into the system's capacity. Your agency is a
system. This is why I put the s in front of the cyborg" (p. 61).
Accordingly, s-he argues, [there is no utopian decolonized university
but there are opportunities for operating as a decolonizing university
through the subversive scyborgian acts of reassemblage]{.underline}
(paperson, 2017). So what does this mean practically for white people
and those of us connected to whiteness in the neoliberal academy? At the
most basic level, [cultivating this "attitude" and the post-/dehumanist
understanding of agency that underpins it, means recognizing that our
decolonizing potentials are contingent on the ways in which we maintain
our relationships with "the outside." In decolonial and Indigenous
literatures this is described as "relational accountability," which
calls on us to be responsible and locate ourselves (and our theories)
within the political and violent histories which continue to structure
sites of oppression in our societies today]{.underline} (Chilisa, 2020;
Dei & Asgharzadeh, 2001; Naylor et al., 2018). Therefore in reading
about the subjectivities, ontologies, and epistemologies of Others, this
implicates the importance of holding "proximity and distance together"
in such encounters (Ahmed, 2000). In explanation, Ahmed (2000), drawing
on Levinas, likens an ethical interaction to a caress, as opposed to a
grasp, shake, beating, or withdrawal, reflected through a respect of the
"boundary lines that have already been drawn as markers not only of
territory, but of power" (p. 156--157). For example, in the case of
paperson's (2017) scyborg, s-he locates the reassembling potentials of
the scyborg in desire; how "you, as scyborg might use these
\[colonising\] technologies to bend the fabric of power to suit your
decolonial desires" (p. 59). However, [for those of us who move
relatively easily through the unmarked whiteness of Western academia, we
must question whether we can ever fully trust our own decolonial
desires]{.underline}? As scyborgs who do not feel \[ever nearly as
strongly\] the friction and violence of colonial technologies on our
bodies, it is crucial that we recognize our difference. Namely, that [we
understand the importance of formulating our decolonial desires in more
"co-conspiratorial" ways]{.underline} (Love, 2019), taking care to
[ensure that our \[removals from\] action align with broader decolonial
and social justice strategizing]{.underline}. It is for this reason that
I would never describe myself as a decolonial or anti-colonial scholar;
I recognize that in order to work in anti-colonial ways I must think
with Others (through interdisciplinary readings and real interactions)
about how and when I take-up and deoccupy space. [Refusals are integral
to this. They are about our responsibilities as scholars, practitioners,
and activists in needing to be explicit about how we are engaging with
the knowledge of Others in our attempts to manage and remain accountable
for our continued complicities in oppressive structures and forces.
Refusals demand that we go beyond the "self-deceiving]{.underline}"
(Maldonado-Torres, 2017) [practices of some reflexive accounts, in which
"privileges" are named only in relation to how they were \[supposedly\]
mitigated]{.underline}. Refusals call for the telling of 'involved
stories' (Puig de la Bellacasa, 2017) in which the complexities of our
relationships with the worlds that we research and work with, are
acknowledged through remaining "openly moved by a commitment to treat
the emerging issues as a matter of care\... \[providing accounts that
are\] neither theoretical nor descriptive, \[and\] open to alternative
readings, yet situated" (p. 22). This connects with Tuck and Yang's
(2014) identification of refusals "as a way of thinking about humanizing
researchers" (p. 223), and too, with Fanon's (1952/2008) emphasis on the
importance of narrative and description as a means of disrupting the
coloniality of the scientific method (Maldonado-Torres, 2017). [Refusals
therefore provide an opening for white scholars, practitioners, and
activists, and all of us connected to whiteness, to contribute to the
telling of stories and assembling of practices around and outside of the
"sameness"]{.underline} (Essed & Goldberg, 2002) [and
"mastery]{.underline}" (Singh, 2018) [that is actively cultured in
academia.]{.underline} By the very nature of complicities, and also the
long arc of collaborative change[, the stories of our refusals will
remain imperfect, undone, and particular to each own's
situation]{.underline}. In my current situation, working on a fixed-term
postdoctoral research contract, I am not always able to set limits on
the work that I do. I raise the issue of complicities, open-up
dialogues, and take a stand when I can, but also sometimes I don't have
the energy, opportunity (because of pressured timeframes), or relational
power to do so. Nevertheless I'm also taking this experience as a
learning opportunity. Even when I cannot refuse, I can still identify
specific examples of how I do not want to work, as much as how I hope to
when I have more institutional power, and I imagine I'll spend my career
trying to cultivate these practices. For those of you who currently hold
institutional power, educate yourself, and share learning on issues of
care and wellbeing in academia (e.g., see Boynton, 2021). Support and
shield students and colleagues who are striving to operate in subversive
and decolonizing ways; listen, and learn from them, and cultivate space
for the sharing of "involved stories" that speak to the discomfort and
unease of being complicit in the coloniality of social science research.
Furthermore, co-conspire and mobilize (also with alumni \[see Canham et
al., in, press\] and movements and communities outside of academia)
against the precariousness that pervades so much of this work. [We do
not engage in refusal to absolve ourselves of complicity or attempt to
move towards innocence, we engage in refusal to "unsettle
innocence]{.underline}" (Tuck & Yang, 2014), [and this ultimately, must
be a collective endeavor.]{.underline} It is essential that we attend to
both the long arc of collectively strategized change as well as moments
of ethics, in which our responsivity to more-than-human worlds is never
mechanistically presumed, but rather viewed as requiring, "a moment by
moment process of ethically sensitive negotiation at the boundaries of
our being" (Shotter, 1992, p. 11). In order to put our differences "to
work" (Ahmed, 2000) we must form entire webs of refusals, through which
new ethico-political and agentic possibilities can emerge.

#### The aff's acceptance of the Arctic as an inscribed space is complicit with Eurocentric drawings

[EUROCENTRISM The conscious or unconscious process by which Europe and
European cultural assumptions are constructed as, or assumed to be, the
normal, the natural or the universal]{.underline}. [The
first,]{.underline} and possibly most potent [sign of
Eurocentrism]{.underline}, as José Rabasa explains (1993), [was the
specific projection]{.underline} employed [to **construct the Mercator
Atlas**]{.underline} itself, [a projection that favoured the European
temperate zones in its distribution of size]{.underline}. [This map of
the world is not merely an objective outline of discovered continents,
but an 'ideological or mythological reification of space' which opens up
the territories of the world to domination and
appropriation.]{.underline} '[The world']{.underline} only [acquired
spatial meaning]{.underline} [after different regions had been
**inscribed by Europeans**]{.underline}, and this inscription, [apart
from locating Europe at the top of the globe or map, established an
ideological figuration]{.underline}, through the accompanying text and
illustrations, which [firmly centralized Europe as the source and
arbiter of spatial and cultural meaning]{.underline}. By the eighteenth
century [this conception of a collective 'Europe' constructed as a sign
of superiority]{.underline} and [in opposition to the rest of the
world's cultures]{.underline} had become firmly consolidated. Then, as
now, [such collective constructions existed in a troubled or ambivalent
relationship with an alternative stress on the nationalism of emerging
individual European nation-states]{.underline} and their particular
cultures. [European colonization]{.underline} of the rest of the globe,
which accelerated in the eighteenth century and reached its apogee in
the nineteenth, actively promoted or [facilitated Eurocentrism through
**exploration**, conquest and trade]{.underline}. Imperial displays of
power, both in the metropolitan centres and at the colonial peripheries,
and assertions of intellectual authority in colonialist institutions
such as schools and universities, and through the civil service and
legal codes, established European systems and values as inherently
superior to indigenous ones. Edward Said's Orientalism examines [the
ways in which Eurocentrism not only influences and alters, but actually
produces other cultures.]{.underline} Orientalism is 'a way of coming to
terms with the orient that is based on the orient's special place in
European western experience'(1978:1) or 'the western style for
dominating, restructuring and having authority over the orient' (3).
This authority is, in Said's view, a product of a systematic
'discipline' by which European culture was able to construct and manage
the Orient during the post-Enlightenment period. [Eurocentrism is
masked]{.underline} in literary study [by concepts such as literary
universality]{.underline}, in history by [authoritative
interpretations]{.underline} written from the point of view of the
victors, [and]{.underline} in early anthropology by [the unconscious
assumptions involved in the idea that its data were those societies
defined as 'primitive' and so opposed to a European norm of
**development**]{.underline} and civilization. Some cultural critics
have argued that anthropology as a discipline in its classic, unrevised
form came into being in such a close relationship with colonization that
it could not have existed at all without the prior existence of
Eurocentric concepts of knowledge and civilization. [Eurocentrism is
also present in the]{.underline} assumptions and practices of
Christianity through education and mission activity, as well as in in
the [assumed superiority of Western mathematics, **cartography**, art
and numerous other cultural and social practices which have been
claimed, or assumed, to be based on a universal, objective set of
values.]{.underline}

#### The aff [reinforces]{.underline} the [cartographic transformation]{.underline} of the world through a eurocentric lens

Bill **Ashcroft et al. '25**, PhD, Emeritus Professor in the School of
English, Media and Performing Arts. A founding exponent of post-colonial
theory, co-author of *The Empire Writes Back*, author and co-author of
twenty one books and 200 chapters and papers. Received the Fellow of the
Australian Academy of the Humanities award, the Global Award of Academic
Excellence award, the Australian Research Council -- Australian
Professorial Fellowship award, the Distinguished researcher award

ed.)" p. 359-360, Routledge, 2025 \\\\\\ SDM

[WORLDING A term coined by Gayatri Spivak to describe the way in which
colonized space is **brought into the 'world'**]{.underline}**,** that
is, [made to exist as part of a world essentially constructed by
Euro-centrism]{.underline}: If . . . we concentrated on documenting and
theorizing the itinerary of the consolidation of Europe as sovereign
subject, indeed sovereign and subject, then we would produce an
alternative historical narrative of the 'worlding' of what is today
called 'the Third World.' (Spivak 1985a: 128) Alluding to Heidegger's
essay 'The Origin of the Work of Art', Spivak describes the process as
the '[worlding of the world on uninscribed earth']{.underline}, [which
may be described in other terms as the 'inscribing' of imperial
discourse upon the colonized 'space']{.underline}. [This kind of
inscription is most obviously carried out by activities such as
**mapping**, both by putting the colony on the map of the world and by
mapping it internally so as to name it, and by naming it to know it, and
hence, **control it.**]{.underline} [But]{.underline} the process of
'[worlding' also occurs in much more subtle ways]{.underline}: Spivak
offers examples of the ways in which imperialism works to overwrite the
colonized place by simply being there, [pointing to the example of the
solitary British soldier walking across the countryside of India in the
early nineteenth century]{.underline}: H[e is actually engaged in
consolidating the self of Europe by obliging the native to cathect the
space of the Other on his home ground]{.underline} \[that is, he is
obliging the native to experience his home ground as imperial space\].
He is worlding their own world, which is far from mere uninscribed
earth. . . . \[He is effectively and violently sliding one discourse
under another.\] (Spivak 1985a: 133) This is one of the many different
processes of othering, which characterize colonial contact. The point
Spivak is making here is that the imperial project itself is far from
monolithic, that its 'class composition and social positionality are
necessarily heterogeneous' (133). [This '**cartographic
transformation'** was not only achieved by the policy makers, but also,
and **more importantly**, by the little people like the solitary soldier
-- and the thousands of colonists **who followed** people like him to
places that are colonized by an imperial society.]{.underline}

#### Treating the Arctic as a land for the US to explore and develop are rooted in a Eurocentric history of colonialism, which homogenizes the Arctic and creates the preconditions for colonial and hierarchical violence

**HUGGAN & Norum, 15** (GRAHAM HUGGAN & ROGER NORUM. **Graham Huggan is

Never the Muse is absent

from their ways: lyres clash and flutes cry

and everywhere maiden choruses whirling.

They bind their hair in golden laurel and take their holiday.

Neither disease nor bitter old age is mixed

in their sacred blood; far from labor and battle

they live; they escape scandal and litigation

\... those blessed men1

A contemporary of Herodotus, **[the lyric poet Pindar penned these words
during the fifth century BC in an ode to the fabled]{.underline}**
Ὑπερβόρε(ι)οι, or **[Hyperboreans. To the Ancient Greeks, these were a
mythical people who lived beyond the North Wind in a fabulous landscape
of eternal spring, sunlight, and warmth. For them, light perpetual was
cast onto a land of enlightenment, devoid of shadow and full of
possibility.]{.underline}** Indeed, Nietzsche later invokes Pindar's
stanzas to argue that we are all Hyperboreans in that we seek out,
through solitude, a philosophical, hermeneutical happiness -- 'another
beginning', as he suggests -- in order to overcome the decadence and
malady of the modern individual.2 **[While contemporary imaginations of
the Arctic may have turned cooler and darker since the time of the
Greeks, the region continues to be mythologized as an unknown,
inaccessible, and forbidden land, a remote, wild, and often 'othered'
frontier. As an aggregate territory and a phantasy of the public
consciousness, there has been a drive to essentialize this diverse,
contested space in a way that heavily glosses over the complex
geopolitical, (trans)national, cultural and linguistic distinctions that
define it. Its history entangled with various cultural and ideological
representations, the Arctic is disputed as an idea as much as a broad
topographical description we use to orient ourselves.]{.underline}** It
is not merely that the alterities we ascribe to this part of the world
are socially, culturally, and historically embedded. It is that the
Arctic's multiple landscapes -- and its aesthetic, cultural, political,
economic and scientific interests -- are all inexorably intermeshed with
one another.3 Perhaps it is this seduction towards a unifying
singularity that draws in those who seek in the Arctic some measure of
inspiration. **[For if the Arctic has been a space for ideological
projections, it has also served as a physical nexus for thought and
experimentation, creativity and artistry, engagement and collaboration,
excitement and adventure -- for artists, writers, thinkers, scholars,
explorers and travellers. Many journeys North are constructed and
portrayed as odysseys towards self and truth, movements mobilized by the
poetic potential of exploration, both to the ends of the Earth and the
innards of the soul.]{.underline}**4 **[The Arctic's reluctant,
disorienting, and alienating fictional and factual landscapes, whether
characterized by remoteness, loneliness or vulnerability, also hold an
immense power to encourage the bridging of boundaries, categories, and
discourses.]{.underline}** Creative work conceived of in this region and
by its peoples -- or, indeed, through both -- can challenge dominant
analytic and interpretive frameworks, and question prototypical
conceptualizations and beliefs about extreme spaces, and about the
humans (and non-humans) who inhabit them. **[And yet, for many external
observers, the Arctic today is extreme no longer: it is tamed, and more
accessible than ever.]{.underline}** Two thousand five hundred years
ago, Pindar wrote that 'never on foot or ship could you explore the
marvelous road to the feast of the Hyperboreans'.5 Today, Ryanair flies
there direct. **[Changes (climatic, social, commercial) across the
region have further enabled this contested space to exist beyond mere
imagination, promising new access to new people. Encounters with the
Arctic are being re-envisioned today given the increased accessibility
of travel and mobility. This is due not just to us bearing witness to
the materialization of anthropogenic climate change, nor to what has
been called -- in deliberate reference to another colonial hot potato --
a 'scramble'6 for the Arctic, nor yet to new forms of travel and tourism
cropping up in various pockets of the region. It is also due to the
paradox that the more we are empowered to move closer to landscapes and
experiences that defamiliarize us from what we know in our everyday
lives, the more we seek to imagine new unfamiliarities.]{.underline}**
This issue of Moving Worlds is the outcome of a series of talks convened
by Arctic Encounters: Contemporary Travel/Writing in the European High
North, a collaborative and multidisciplinary HERA-funded research
project that explores the ways in which a changing Arctic changes ideas
not just about travel, but about the planet and its inhabitants more
broadly. **[The Arctic itself is very much a moving world. It has come
to stand for much more than its own landscapes. Movement here is an apt
metaphor for a globalizing topography in which immobility becomes more
and more anomaly: we move the Earth (with our chlorofluorocarbons,
fracking and terraforming); the Earth moves us (through its retreating
ice and rising waters). And indeed, if recent scholarly and public
debates around the Anthropocene are any measure, such movements mobilize
us into political and environmental action. Amid this, old contests over
control of Arctic resources and occupation of its territories resurface
as increasingly urgent social issues, and the fight again emerges to
define and (re)claim the Arctic's pasts, present, and futures. As the
Arctic moves forward, so does it become an even grander geography for
the projection of imperial anxieties. That these anxieties are writ
large today is hardly surprising. The history of the Arctic is -- albeit
at different times and in different ways -- one of subjection, both to
the absolutisms of imperial rule and to more locally inflected
colonialisms. That history continues apace, confirming the obdurate
legacies of 'old-style' modes of colonial governance, but also ushering
in the 'new imperialisms']{.underline}**7 **[that are generally
associated with latecapitalist globalization, one epiphenomenon of which
-- all too visible in the Arctic -- is accelerated climate change. If
none of this is in dispute, two questions still remain in the context of
this particular issue. The first is the extent to which the contemporary
Arctic can be seen as 'postcolonial'. The second is the extent to which
the region -- however defined, and there are many possible definitions
-- can benefit from 'postcolonialism': that loose toolkit of approaches,
theories, and methods which, rarely accepted with equanimity, has been
applied to so many other parts of the world. Neither question yields an
easy answer. If the Arctic is postcolonial, then it will only be so if
the term is seen as negating its own prefix; as challenging the very
conditions of colonialism within which it is itself inscribed. And if it
is amenable to postcolonialism, then it will only be so if those
approaches, theories, and methods are taken to their own limits. Indeed,
in some ways the Arctic, which has become central to 'one-world'
ecological debates around planetary stewardship even as it continues to
be socially and politically marginalized, pushes the boundaries of
postcolonial understandings of society, politics, ecology -- and
conceptions of itself. The essays included here engage with these
reflexive dilemmas without falling into the trap that postcolonial
criticism often sets for itself: that of debating 'real-world' issues
that are designed for the application of postcolonial methods, only for
those methods to end up circling back on themselves. One way of
confronting this is to elevate practice above theory.]{.underline}** The
Postcolonial Arctic begins with a moving experiential account by the
British writer Kari Herbert -- daughter of the famous polar explorer Sir
Wally Herbert -- of part of a childhood spent in northern Greenland. The
account is characterized by a love -- of people, of place, of the
multiple fascinations of the Arctic -- that has remained undiminished
over time. It is also remarkably free of nostalgia, recognizing the
harshness, even ugliness, of the far north and the acute social and
environmental problems that have arisen there. These problems are
accentuated in the essay that follows by the award-winning Norwegian
political geographer Tone Huse. Huse **[focuses on the notorious Blok P
-- a now-defunct housing estate in Nuuk, Greenland's capital -- which
becomes the site for a lyrical contemplation on the country's colonial
past. Blok P is perhaps best known for the violence -- suicide and
aggravated assault, domestic and substance abuse -- that happened inside
it. But Huse is more interested in the external violence that created it
in the first place: the violence of central government planning; the
violence of a certain kind of architectural modernism; and, above all,
the violence of colonialism itself. The Arctic's colonial pasts --
multiple, often confused, always internally
differentiated]{.underline}** -- are also the subject of the next four
essays, whether colonialism is refracted through mediatized geopolitics
(Johanne Bruun), the technologies of polar exploration (Marionne
Cronin), threatened indigenous life-ways (Tero Mustonen), or digital
governance regimes (Citt Williams). **[In all four cases, colonialism is
as much of the present as of the past, caught up as it is in the
enduring (albeit historically shifting) symbolic politics that has come
to characterize the region for those who seek to control it rather than
those who wish to inhabit it; for those who want to manipulate it either
as an object of Western knowledge or as an opportunity for resource
extraction -- or both, since the Arctic is a rich symbolic and material
space.]{.underline}** This sense of the symbolic as well as material
importance of the Arctic is maintained in the last two essays: a
visually arresting piece by the Tromsø-based architect, Kjerstin Uhre,
and a rousing cri de coeur by the Cambridge-based environmental
historian, Michael Bravo. Much in the spirit of the issue as a whole,
Bravo **[see]{.underline}**s **[the Arctic in terms of a complex set of
interrelationships between 'old' and 'new' colonialisms -- one that
offers opportunities to colonize others, but also to decolonize
ourselves. Who 'we' are in these particular instances is moot,
and]{.underline}** (as Bravo is well aware**[) the diverse peoples of
the Arctic are fed up with being spoken for by others. But if the
'postcolonial Arctic' is to make any sense, both for these peoples and
for those who see themselves as acting in their best interests, then it
needs to be a fully dialogical space within the wider context of an
increasingly globalized world.]{.underline}**

### Link\-\--Borders

The 1acs capture of arctic knowledge reifies immigrant stereotypes that
reinforce 'refugeness'. The border line is the distinction between being
and becoming. Your place on the map determines if you do or do not
exist.

**Khosravi '10,** Associate Professor of Social Anthropology at
