### Link\-\--Shipping / NWP

#### The dispute over the NWP has been growing---the low impact shipping corridors initiative incorporates indigenous spatial understanding into state-run cartographic efforts, especially when navigating territorial disputes, promoting indigenous recognition and sovereignty.

\*\*Note: I think this is very strategic because the is advocates for
something attainable which is to view arctic territory from a new lens
of indigenous spatial understandings superimposed on western
understandings to promote their sovereingty.

**Tsiouvalas & Solski 23** \[Apostolos. Jan Jakub. "'One Map to Rule
Them All'? Revisiting Legalities Through Cartographic Representations of
the Northwest Passage." Taylor & Francis, 4-18-2023.
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00908320.2023.2273288#d1e261\]
Ayan Mahajan 🤪

[Spatial thinking commonly reflected within these instruments is deeply
rooted in cartesian conceptualizations of the world based on ontological
dichotomies such as land versus sea and nature versus culture that can
be traced back to the solidification of legal modernity.112 Such
dichotomies are reflected in international law's fundamental spatial
order and revealed in the way space is partitioned upon cartographic
representations on the basis of state sovereignty.113 Yet this is not
the only way to conceptualize space in the Arctic. **Many Indigenous
spatial orders across the Arctic diverge from the fundamental
dichotomies that underpin the foundations of international law's
spatial** order and are instead traditionally grounded in holistic114
conceptualizations of space and animist or relational ontologies115
Inuit customary legal orders have been shaped by the particularities of
Arctic landscape conditions (e.g., sea ice, remoteness, coldness) and
are rooted in a special spiritual connection with the surrounding
environment and wildlife.116 According to Inuit worldviews, **human
beings are traditionally seen as not separated from the natural world,
while the sea ice constitutes an extension of the land, and, thus,
international law's land--sea dichotomy contravenes the holistic spatial
vision of the Inuit legal orders.**117 As the former Chair of the Inuit
Circumpolar Council (ICC) put it in a UN Chronicle, "the Arctic Ocean
and the sea ice is our nuna," which translated into "land,"
acknowledging the Inuit's strong bonds to the marine ecosystems---much
like nation-states conceptualize the "land."118 For the Inuit of the
NWP, the sea ice is thus not seen as a "barrier" in the development of
navigation and commerce, but **functions as a bridge to the sea, a
symbol of their own mobility across space**, reflecting their customary
relationship to nature and their historical seminomadic lifestyle.119
Indeed, as observed by Beveridge, there is also a "functional" (and far
from being ontological) boundary of land--sea among Inuit, mainly when
the sea ice is not present, which, however, becomes blurred when the sea
ice appears.120]{.underline}

[Through this understanding, and with Inuit settlement in the Arctic
preceding the arrival of Europeans for many centuries, the Inuit
pioneered their own traditional mapping techniques to navigate within
space in accordance with their hunting practices and migration cycles.
Such cartographic praxes may take a variety of forms, ranging from sewn
driftwood to sealskin representations and to carved portable
maps.]{.underline}121 Ethnographers and cartographic researchers most
commonly refer to the Wetalltok map of the Belcher Islands (Sanikiluaq),
a map of the islands in Disko Bay on the Western coast of Greenland by
Silas Sandgreen, or the portable wooden carving maps from eastern
Greenland,122 created out of driftwood to navigate coastal waters
(Figure 9).123 These compact tactile devices represent islands or
coastlines in a continuous line, up one side of the wood and down the
other. [Inuit traditional maps were predominantly of navigational or
**storytelling use that enabled a variety of senses** (e.g. sight,
sound, touch) and reflect the way Inuit view the land/seascape, beyond
dominant conceptualizations of territory and sovereign
borders.]{.underline}

Although most Indigenous peoples developed their own methods of mapping
to depict people's stories and communities' relationships to places and
other beings, most traditional modes of spatial expressions upon the
area now known as the Northwest Passage were deeply affected by the
arrival of the Europeans.124 European views and ontological assumptions
of space were gradually imposed upon preexisting Indigenous cartography,
and maps of the newly "discovered" places depicting borders, resources,
and claims upon space soon became common in the colonial world. Early
European expeditions acknowledged the precision of Inuit maps in
delineating the complex Arctic ice-scape, and in many cases, European
explorers trusted Inuit hunters in producing planimetrically accurate
maps consistent with European standards and their voyage's cartographic
needs.125 As observed by McGurk and Caquard, since the first contact
with the European settlers, Inuit mapping has had to "move back and
forth between ancestral and processual mapping and impacted by the
colonizers requirements for precise, standardized, and rational
mapmaking practices."126 For instance, under the umbrella of Moravian
missionaries and Scottish whalers, and using European cartographic
tools, the Inuit contributed to a comprehensive mapping of the Inuit
presence in the region.127 The map Nunat Kalâtdlit Najugait from
Atuagagdliutit no. 1, 1861 (Figure 10), is probably the first such known
map that illustrates the entirety of the Inuit territories of North
America, demonstrating both the predominant presence of the Inuit
throughout the Northwest Passage and the close cartographic work among
the Inuit and European settlers.

The familiarization of the Inuit with European cartographic means has
been used today to ensure opportunities to advance Indigenous decolonial
mapping efforts. Remarkably, Western mapping methods are now often
infused with Indigenous knowledge and are used by Indigenous groups as
tools to counternarrate space and reflect Indigenous beliefs and
political claims in light of an ongoing decolonial endeavor. Since the
1970s, Inuit people have been highly involved in mapmaking activities in
Arctic Canada, as part of the Inuit Land Use and Occupancy Project
(ILUOP) that constituted the legal basis for the Land Claim Agreements
of Inuvialuit (1984) and Nunavut (1993).128 [In 2005, after delineating
their last land claims, the Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami **(ITK),129 which
serves as the domestic representative organization for the Inuit of
Canada, created a new map of Inuit use and occupancy of the Arctic
entitled Inuit Nunaat (Inuit homeland).130**]{.underline}

[More recently, ITK developed a new map for Canada's Arctic region named
Inuit Nunangat, which aimed to reflect the Inuit settlements and
distinct conception of space.131 The map Inuit Nunangat (Figure 11)
encompasses all terrestrial, marine, and ice-covered areas, and
demonstrates that the Inuit consider the land, water, and sea ice to be
interconnected and "integral to their culture and way of life."132 The
map challenged dominant conceptions of space and replaced Canada's
provincial and territorial boundaries with terraqueous/frozen borders
based on traditional use and occupancy by the Inuit, raising concerns
regarding Canada's sovereignty and political jurisdiction within
traditional areas, particularly over the Northwest Passage.133
**Thinking of the NWP as part of the Inuit Nunangat may allow for a
comprehensive holistic conceptualization of space that erases Western
dichotomies, bridges water, sea ice, and land in accordance with the
Inuit way of life, and replaces ontologies of power and control with
ontologies of "being" in and with nature. Such an approach could better
reflect also the general scope of UNCLOS as provided in the Convention's
Preamble, which states that "the problems of ocean space are closely
interrelated and need to be considered as a whole."**134 Yet, as further
observed by Fabbi, a reconceptualization of "territory" as a notion
inclusive of ice, water, and land will have far-reaching implications as
to UNCLOS's ability to settle the current dispute over the Passage, as
well as to Canada's general commitments toward international law
instruments.135]{.underline}

Holistic cartographic representations of the Arctic space by Indigenous
peoples have also been embedded in online mapping representations, with
the Inuit increasingly engaging in such projects aiming to reclaim
Indigenous rights and decolonize cartography.136 Although online mapping
is inherently a Western tool, it has today become a part of the Inuit's
"decolonial agenda" and important tool for redefining space.137 For
instance, the project "The Northwest Passage and the Construction of
Inuit Pan-Arctic Identities" has mapped the historical Inuit presence
along the Northwest Passage, using, instead of precise geographic
locations or separate routes on land and in the sea, terraqueous lines
that spread across land, sea, and sea ice.138 Inuit trails are not
static lines as they are depicted in mainstream maps, but mobile
entities that represent fluidity across space, tangle with each other,
and create a complex meshwork139 that cannot be simply reduced to
Western cartographic representations.140 In that regard, the Nunaliit
Atlas Framework driven by the Geomatics and Cartography Research Centre
(GCRC) at Carleton University has managed to provide access to Inuit
communities to build their own interactive maps and digitally document
local and traditional Inuit spatial knowledge in cybercartographic
atlases.141 What emerges from these maps is that statist representations
of territory are mobilized and renegotiated from the bottom up.
Furthermore, they resonate with the uncertain materiality of the Arctic
environment such that it is dynamic in both space and time and may
change form inconsistently, exposing sovereignty's inherent tendency for
permanence.142

[The Low-Impact Shipping Corridors Initiative---One Map to Rule Them
All?]{.underline}

[The inter-state dispute over the legal status of the NWP is premised on
different visions of ocean space: one of the full and exclusive
sovereignty by the state of Canada, and one more akin to a res communis
vision of an international strait where other states are entitled to
exercise freedom of navigation for the purpose of transit passage.143
Although these different visions are difficult to reconcile, in light of
international developments with respect to the rights of Indigenous
peoples, the cartographic representation of the NWP/Inuit Nunangat is
today subject to other visions of space and developments in human
rights. The United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous
Peoples (UNDRIP),144 although initially rejected by Canada, has, since
2021, been supported through the United Nations Declaration on the
Rights of Indigenous Peoples Act.145 The 2021 Act affirms the UNDRIP as
a universal human rights instrument with application in Canadian law,
and provides a framework for its implementation146 that aims to ensure
the consistency of Canadian law with the Declaration.147]{.underline}

[Against this context, the Low-Impact Shipping Corridors Initiative
constitutes a proposal to reconcile, although on Canadian terms, the
different opposing considerations, including the pluralistic visions of
space, transcending the ones wielded by the sovereign states. Navigation
in the Arctic is a highly risky endeavor, and in that sense, the classic
"freedom of navigation" may be of little use if not supported by a
system of aids, appropriate charts, and mechanisms to assist in normal
operation and in emergency. The initiative avoids the formal recognition
of a navigational right or freedom, and instead focuses on the modality
of the use of the sea space by different actors (Figure 12). It is
revealing that in 2016 the leaders of the United States and Canada, the
two champions of the opposing sides of the sovereignty dispute,
announced a new partnership to embrace the opportunities and confront
challenges in the changing Arctic.148 The partnership includes a
commitment to identify sustainable shipping lanes through the Arctic in
collaboration with Northern and Indigenous partners. As a followup,
Canada earmarked CA\$ 1.5 billion for its Oceans Protection Plan and
launched an Initiative to identify and develop Low-Impact Shipping
Corridors. The corridors "are intended to be dynamic shipping routes
throughout Canada's North where the necessary infrastructure, marine
navigational support, and emergency response services could be provided
to ensure safer marine navigation, while respecting the sensitive
northern environment and its ecological and cultural
significance."149]{.underline}

[As such, the ambition is to identify the areas where three types of
concerns are taken into account: respect for the sensitive environment,
cost-effective provision of necessary navigational support and
infrastructure, and respect for cultural sensitivity. It is particularly
the latter issue that prompted the innovative collaboration toward
developing a governance framework to address local and Indigenous
communities' needs. The initiative is led by Transport Canada and the
Canadian Coast Guard, who has been leading engagement with Inuit, First
Nations, and Metis organizations and governments, provincial and
territorial governments, and other key stakeholders to identify priority
areas for investment and potential governance models. In the course of
Arctic Corridors and Northern Voices (ACNV) research project, 14
Canadian Inuit communities across Inuit Nunangat were invited to share
their views and recommendations for the corridors.150 The community
recommendations identified preferred corridors in different localities,
areas to be avoided, specific measures relating to the seasonal use of
different areas, recommendations on vessel operation, and areas of
inadequate charting. The multitude of factors to be considered when
implementing this ambitious initiative will inevitably lead to
compromise and trade-offs. It is unlikely that all the recommendations
will be given equal weight. However, there are potential and efforts
toward developing a genuinely collaborative governance structure to not
only design but also manage the low-impact corridors.151 This initiative
provides a good opportunity to reconcile the different visions of space
and give real voice to the local communities to translate their visions
into specific recommendations---indeed **to provide "one map to rule
them all."152 Yet incorporating Inuit interests and spatial views into a
state-driven cartographic initiative** bears the risk of perpetuating
statism via a territorially grounded conception of the Passage that
appears detrimental to the pursuit of self-determination for the Inuit
people.153 As of the time of writing, it is yet difficult to tell how
well all this can be done in practice, but the process is
there.]{.underline}

Concluding Remarks

[It is undisputable that cartography materializes the way societies
conceptualize space and legalities pertinent to it. Since the age of
discovery, European nations have been using cartography as a technology
of planning that legitimated the establishment of state sovereignty
through "lines" upon land and sea and demarcated borders and
jurisdictions. Under this rationale, as Harley and Laxton have noted,
"the state became---and has remained---a principal patron of
cartographic activity in many countries," determining the dominant
narratives of spatial thinking.154 This cartographic activity though is
strongly affiliated to a prolonged history of colonization, competition
among sovereign powers, and neglect of different ways of
thought.]{.underline}

[**A bird's-eye view of the development of cartographic thinking in
relation to the Northwest Passage demonstrates that the history of
spatial inscription of the Passage is not a static one, but has
historically been driven by a territorial rationale imposed on the
Passage ever since the first colonial charters were used as avenues for
the successful imperial expansion and imposition of colonialism.** In
this context, maps of the region have reflected a dominant Eurocentric
worldview of "space" that is not very relevant to the way the very own
people of the region think about it. If "to map is to think," and thus
"to inscribe" worldviews upon space,155 then there is paramount value in
examining how non-Western conceptions of space may challenge dualistic
assumptions about the surrounding world that lie at the core of modern
spatial thinking. Antithetical to dominant maps decided upon to serve
their "makers" and to demonstrate their brute assertion of ascendancy
over a colonized "other," Inuit cartography is nowadays seeking not only
to question existing legal spatial thinking but also to reshape it from
the ground up, resisting the long colonial legacy of Arctic mapping.
This demonstrates that the NWP is not only an arena of dispute among
sovereign states, but a rather plural space that currently accommodates
different ontological visions, legal orders, and ways of being in
relation to space. **Inuit cartography thus becomes a tool of
countermapping, retelling existing narratives of space and questioning
the very understandings of maritime zones and borders, even conceptual
dichotomies among land and sea, upon which Arctic law and governance are
today premised.** Should the Arctic states decide to fully
operationalize Indigenous self-determination in accordance with
International human rights law and, thus, acknowledge the Inuit
terraqueous materialities, then the legitimacy of state sovereignty's
territorial application and cartographic representation over coastal
waters and frozen areas would be drastically challenged. Reconciling
Inuit and state spatial orders is not only about geographical accuracy
but also about upholding and respecting the rights and heritage of
Indigenous communities.]{.underline}

[Navigating the history of NWP/Inuit Nunangat throughout cartography for
the purposes of this article is thus not only about recognizing Inuit
cartography, but also about using it to obtain and promote the local
understanding of the localities, which, given the particular
relationship with nature, may also be considered good environmental
practice. **The Low-Impact Corridors Initiative holds great potential to
merge worldviews without homogenizing them. Low-impact corridors will
not solve the inter-state dispute, and will not displace either the
political map of Canada (national boundaries) or Inuit cartography.
Rather, the Initiative superimposes one on another in a way that
enriches how we conceive of space, and facilitates a synergetic reading
of a pluralistic multivision of the Arctic. Recognizing Inuit interests
on official state maps helps validate Inuit rights to these lands and
waters, enabling them to maintain their cultural heritage and
traditional practices.**]{.underline}

### Link\-\--Scientific Exploration

#### "Scientific exploration" is all [political posturing]{.underline} that serves sovereign interests

Graham **Huggan**\* and Lars **Jensen**\*\* **16**, \*PhD in comparative
literature and former Harvard professor, Chair of Commonwealth and
Postcolonial Literatures at the University of Leeds, co-founding
director of the Institute for Colonial and Postcolonial Studies at the
University of Leads; \*\*PhD in postcolonial studies, associate
professor of the Department of Communication and Arts Intercultural
Studies at the Center for Arctic Studies at Roskilde University,
"Postcolonial Perspectives on the European High North; Unscrambling the
Arctic," p. 44-45, 2016 \\\\\\ SDM

Unscrambling the arctic

[While Arctic scientific research and exploration has contributed to
knowledge]{.underline} about the polar regions and the planet more
broadly, [one must not divorce these interests from economic
ones]{.underline}. [Scientific research and economic and political
interests have long been interconnected]{.underline} (Cornelia and
Summerhayes 2012; Roberts 2011). [Practices of mapping and survey- ing,
for example, **were central to the early geopolitical formation of
Arctic spaces**]{.underline}. [Early]{.underline} whalers and maritime
[explorers drew the first maps of the region,]{.underline} while [one of
the tasks of nineteenth-century industrial exploration was
to]{.underline} examine coastal areas and [improve geological knowledge
in the search for valuable **mineral resources**.]{.underline} A
state-funded expedition that led to the establishment of mining
activities could in turn improve the influence of that country in the
archipelago. From a broader perspective, [even much of the **seemingly
"pure" science** of today is economically and politically
motivated]{.underline}, with [countries often sponsoring expensive polar
research in order to bulk up their presence in these regions and thereby
increase their influence]{.underline}. The [search for exploitable
mineral resources continues]{.underline} up to the present
day---primarily for oil and gas, though there is still ongoing surveying
in Svalbard for further coal deposits. [Deep drilling]{.underline},
especially for oil, began in the 1960s, and even though some of the
results were withheld due to competition between the companies, these
expensive drilling programmes, like later seismic shootings,
[contributed to knowledge of geological structures deep under the
surface]{.underline}. [Research both in and on the Arctic has "long been
constituted via constellations of emotional and embodied practices as
well as epistemic ones."]{.underline} 23 [Such research has been
constituted by "complicated relationships between activities which were
construed to be scientific whilst also allowing some sort of
demonstration of territorial sovereignty."]{.underline} 24 Furthermore,
as was the case with jostling for mineral resources during the previous
cen- tury, [research itself now represents a **geography of
international competition**]{.underline} on Svalbard. Linked to the
[increase in research activity is the growing conflict between
ecological research and protection of the environment, or between
geopolitical research and mining or tourism]{.underline}. 25

### Link\-\--Climate Science

#### Science, mapping, and climate discourses are NOT neutral, they are all colonial tools that enforce settler control- they frame the Arctic as both a technical problem to be solved through governance and a site of inevitable Indigenous disappearance. Damage narratives turn Arctic life into a symbol of global loss.

**Dodds** and **Smith** **22** (Klaus Dodds, Professor of Geopolitics at
Royal Holloway, University of London, PhD Oxford; Jen Rose Smith,
Assistant Professor of Geography and American Indian Studies at
University of Wisconsin-Madison, PhD UC--Berkeley, 2022, "Against
Decline? The Geographies and Temporalities of the Arctic Cryosphere,"
The Geographical Journal, Vol. 189, Issue 3, pp. 392--395.
https://doi.org/10.1111/geoj.12481 TA)

3 \| THE STORIFICATION OF ICE IN ARCTIC GEOPOLITICS

In our reading of the Arctic, we argue that [contemporary Arctic
geopolitical discourse too often pivots around simple causalities such
as less sea ice equates to growth in maritime traffic and possible
geopolitical tension (]{.underline}for a critical review, Depledge,
2019). This in turn invites a phalanx of technical-scientific experts to
qualify claims about the preponderance of open water or dampen down
expectations that the Arctic will inevitably be a global maritime
highway. [These are particular and familiar colonial moves towards
possessing the Arctic. The relationship between colonialism and northern
territories, including water, is not straightforward given that many of
those lands incorporated into colonial-nation states such as Canada, the
United States and Russia that utilised varying projects, tactics and
techniques to dispossess Indigenous peoples of their lands, lifeways and
liveli- hoods (Kollin, 2001; Ruiz et al., 2019)]{.underline}. [What is
often understood as a settler impulse to eliminate to replace by way of
genocidal warfare is not neatly projected onto landscapes of the Arctic,
especially in thinking of the particularly situated (ongoing) colo- nial
histories in Alaska and Canada.]{.underline} The enactment of allotment,
and terra-forming forest and plain into agriculture including ranching,
though attempted, was not successfully replicated further north. The
early needs for resource extraction as animating the core principle of
settler-racial-capitalism was not easily met by virtue of the presence
of ice and glacier fields of Arctic landscapes. Colonial movements and
travels in search of the Northwest/east Passage of the white nationalist
race to the illusive North Pole were temporary and fleeting---apart from
later public policy directed towards the encouragement and support of
homesteaders and gold miners to populate northern spaces, colonial
actors in the North often did not come to stay. Elemental substances
such as frozen earth, sea ice and glaciers were surveyed, mapped and
manipulated, with colonial agents seeking to thaw out permafrost and
restructure parts of the Arctic landscape in terms of agriculture. The
needs of colonial desires to cultivate particular foods in particular
ways that aligned with property making through labour and land were
thwarted by ice and its liminal shapes of constituting land not water
(Smith, 2021).

[The coupling of scientific expertise and racist legal policies of
dispossession ultimately created similar outcomes of long- standing
oppressive colonial regimes that continue to constitute the present. For
one example, plans such as the so-called Inuit High Arctic relocations
in the early 1950s were hatched in Canada to resettle Indigenous
communities in what is for now understood as northern Quebec, as part of
a strategic calculation designed to populate a 'sparse and spartan'
northern territory. The relocation strategy proved to be a costly and
devastating failure, as it was built upon the notions that all cold
climates are ostensibly the same and therefore Indigenous peoples should
be able to live successfully within them---racist thinking that either
misunderstands or overlooks centuries of relationships built across
Indigenous peoples and more-than-human kin.]{.underline} At the base of
this thinking is a temperate-normative assumption that an adapted cold
weather community in one part of northern Canada could simply be
trans-located for the purpose of filling a less populated area. [The
Inuit High Arctic relocations serve as a poignant reminder of how the
initiatives of relocation are currently iterative---at one moment in the
1950s, Indigenous peoples of the Arctic are made to undergo enforced
relocation by decision of a colonial-nation-state meant to 'uplift' or
to meet the end of an act of colonial sovereignty,]{.underline} but in
the contemporary moment in which Native peoples find their homes
underwater due to carbon consequences, those same nation-states will not
assist in relocation efforts (Audlaluk, 2020).

[The storification of the extent and thickness of sea ice is integral to
Arctic geopolitics---often framed as loss followed by revelation and
enhanced activity and engagement by Arctic and non-Arctic states and
stakeholders (Dodds,]{.underline} 2010[, Dittmer et al.,]{.underline}
2011[).]{.underline} There have been three aspects to all of this that
we believe deserve more explicit and considered attention.
Fundamentally, further attention needs to be given to how these
analogies of sea ice work to produce different framings and
relationships within and beyond the Arctic. First, we might ask what is
the role of sea ice as 'buffer' rather than 'barrier' and/or 'hazard',
and how does such an analogy complicate iterative accounts of sea ice\'s
annual rates of extent or shrinkage (with the inference being that less
sea ice extent and thickness means more likelihood of activity and
possible conflict over the use of polar waters). If sea ice is
considered to be a barrier then that implies that there is either an
obstacle to movement (which someone or thing wishes to cross) and/or
that there is a distinction to be made between one area or another. For
example, the term barrier reef is used to signify an ecological space
betwixt shallow water and shoreline. Whereas sea ice as a material
barrier is often used to help identify the Arctic Ocean as distinct from
the warmer Atlantic and Pacific Oceans. [Sea ice as buffer foregrounds
something quite different; in this rendering, ice is said to perform a
series of ecological services including regulating ocean temperature,
reflecting heat away from ocean and absorbing the energy released by
wave action; whereas sea ice as hazard is framed as such because
commercial and military shipping wishes to traverse ice-covered waters.
Tropes such as 'polar Mediterranean' have long been used to re-configure
the Arctic Ocean and maritime passages as capable, at some point, of
being usable(Steinberg,]{.underline} 2014[).]{.underline}

This shift in the framing of sea ice has developed in two distinct
areas. In stories about climate change and coastal communities in
Alaska, it has become increasingly common to recognise the 'buffering'
effect sea ice plays in mitigating the erosive damage done by winter
storms to coastlines. The intersection between shoreland, water and ice
is thus recognised as interstitial space; with a capacity to absorb and
deflect wave energy away from the rock, soil and sand of the coastline.
A reduction in multi-year sea ice with thinner annual or young sea ice
in turn reveals buffering limits; thicker sea ice being far more likely
to be able to absorb the energy associated with wave action. We see this
enacted in real time with the recent typhoon Merbok that moved through
the Bering Strait and left many coastal communities with damaged homes
and destroyed subsistence infrastructure. Another way sea ice scientists
have framed sea ice is to posit it as a 'lid' to acidification, which is
caused by the ocean becoming saturated with carbon dioxide emissions.
Younger sea ice in particular being less able to act as a 'lid' to both
warming and amplification trends. With less sea ice, open water is
better able to absorb heat and disrupt in turn the relationship between
water, ice and air. Acidifying waters have direct implications for
future biodiversity, as some creatures including shell-building
organisms cannot tolerate acidification beyond a certain degree of
concentration. As Cheryl Katz (2019) noted in her investigation of the
acidification:

But as marine carbon levels climb, more and more carbonate ions are
being used up, lowering the ocean\'s ability to buffer, and causing
acidity---measured as a drop in pH---to rise. Those same carbonate ions
are needed by creatures like starfish and clams, whose shells or
skeletons are made of the calcium carbonate minerals aragonite or
calcite ... Melting away the ocean\'s icy lid exposes yet more water to
take up yet more carbon dioxide from the atmosphere.

The acidification of Arctic waters has direct implications for local and
Indigenous fishing and shellfish harvesting in coastal Alaska, with the
fastest rate of acidification occurring in the Beaufort Sea and Bering
Sea. Acidification affects growing seasons for molluscs and crustaceans,
reproduction rates and species distribution (for example, clams and
crabs). What the analogy of the 'buffer' does is to highlight energy
transfer rather than simply assume 'disappearance' of sea ice per se.
The relationship between land, sea and air in the Arctic reveals well
the 'vertical reciprocity' between the three, as more open water means
more heat absorption, rising air and water temperatures, greater
acidification and diminishment of multi-year ice (Adey, 2010).

Second, sea ice and its relationship to scientific-military imaginaries
and practices reveals polar waters to be hazard rather than buffer---an
obstacle to unfettered mobility. Contemporary iterations of Arctic
geopolitics owe their poignancy to earlier antecedents. During the Cold
War, sea ice was conceptualised as a barrier to movement (Roberts,
2015). A slippery subject that would prove a difficult adversary for
submarine captains as rival naval forces. Countries such as Canada
established national ice services in the 1950s to provide further
coverage and assessment for shipping users. [Sea ice research, and for
that matter permafrost science, was in part funded by national
governments to enable and improve awareness and understanding of
ice-covered waters and cold environments (and Indigenous knowledges and
expertise enrolled if often under-acknowledged). In the military arena,
institutions such as the Office of Naval Research and the US Arctic
Submarine Laboratory in Barrow Alaska were charged with funding research
that sought to make ice more knowable, predictable and
governable.]{.underline} And yet the memoirs of US nuclear submarine
captains and published research on sea ice revealed the opposite---sea
ice was and is notoriously dynamic, confusing and unsettling to those
seeking to go under it, transit it at the surface and/or break through
from below (for example, McLaren, 2008). [Depending on seasonality, wind
direction, ocean temperatures and currents, militaries and scientists
were involved in a host of national and international initiatives
designed to improve seasonal charting, environmental management plans,
strategic patrols, and resource extractions plans.]{.underline} Richard
Powell\'s work on Canadian scientific authority in the 1950s illustrates
well the manner in which sea ice was caught up with sovereign and
security agendas (Powell, 2017).

Coastal states such as Russia and Canada were also eager to ensure that
their ice-covered waters were properly protected---environmentally and
legally---from transit traffic (Steinberg et al., 2015). In the 1960s
and 1970s, Canadian governments in particular were alarmed by the
prospect of US oil tanker traffic going backwards and forwards from
Alaska via the Northwest Passage. The presence of sea ice acted as a
politically helpful shipping hazard and thus possible deterrent to
foreign-registered ships wishing to cross through waters the Canadian
authorities considered to be 'historic' rather than subject to transit
rights. While Canada put in place additional environmental regulatory
safe-guarding measures (for example, the 1970 Arctic Waters Pollution
Prevention Act), the Northwest Passage remains a source of official
legal disagreement between Canada and the United States. Diminishing sea
ice, thus, acts as a catalyst for speculation about whether this
waterway might become a source of future friction either because the
waters are thought to be in principle less hazardous (a point that is
often contested by shipping and sea ice experts alike) or because
relevant details of the United Nations Law of the Sea Convention (UNCLOS
and Article 234) for 'ice-covered areas' are thought to be less relevant
in operational terms. As the Article in question

Coastal States have the right to adopt and enforce non-discriminatory
laws and regulations for the prevention, reduction and control of marine
pollution from vessels in ice-covered areas within the limits of the
exclusive economic zone, where particularly severe climatic conditions
and the presence of ice covering such areas for most of the year \[i.e.
6 months and one day\] create obstructions or exceptional hazards to
navigation, and pollution of the marine environment could cause major
harm to or irreversible disturbance of the ecological balance.

Article 234 was the only example of where UNCLOS recognised the presence
of ice-covered areas. Later the UN body the International Maritime
Organisation\'s Polar Code (entered into force in 2017) sets out
requirements for shipping entering and leaving polar waters. While
acknowledging that coastal communities 'in the Arctic could be, and that
polar ecosystems are, vulnerable to human activities, such as ship
operation' (emphasis added, Polar Code 2017). While the Polar Code noted
a 'could be' for possible impacts on Arctic coastal communities of
climate change, it addresses the operational challenges posed by sea ice
with considerable assertiveness. [Sea ice is framed throughout as a
technical problem that can be mitigated by collective action. Solutions
to sea ice will emerge through legal, technological and training
interventions.]{.underline}

[Third, sea ice as register of global rather than local or Indigenous
futures. When coastal communities in Alaska are posited as on the
frontline of climate change, sea ice and permafrost are spectacularised
in the sense of being made to stand for a harbinger of an unwanted
global future (Bronen,]{.underline} 2010[). There is often a slippage
between the everyday and local and the global and planetary. What gets
lost in this slippage is the notion that ice/frost/snow become caught up
in an extractive economy which prioritises the ramifications for
extra-territorial parties, global elemental spaces and the 'buffering'
affordances of ice and frozen ground.]{.underline} The Arctic is in
crisis primarily because it will not keep its frozen ground frozen and
its sea ice intact. The very thing that Cold War frontier engineers
dreamed of annihilating is now being cursed for not being resilient
enough (Fleming, 2010). We further assert for a ground to articulate an
Arctic that does not lose the verifiable danger and precarity of ice
melt and the livelihoods that continue to be changed, transformed and
dispossessed, which is largely consequential from the enactments of
racial capitalism but that also holds a refusal to fall victim to an
alarmist, polemical, devastation and declensionist narrative. [The
declensionist narrative is one that has been historical and often
wielded to discuss Indigenous peoples and their inevitable disappearance
conveniently from resource rich areas.]{.underline} [Care must be taken
when reporting the disappearance of ice]{.underline}. As Inupiaq poet
Joan Naviyuk Kane writes:

[Much of the discourse around the Arctic and Indigenous people
preoccupies itself with destruction ... It is couched in either the
concrete documentation of disappearance---erosion or submersion of land
due to climate change or climate-caused displacement, for instance---or
in the figurative language of loss (cited in Nunatsiaq
News,]{.underline} 2018[)]{.underline}

Taking up Kane\'s project, is there a way to identify the enactments of
colonialism without a spectacularised event of damage, loss and the
horror of that loss, as the vessel of discussion? And is it possible to
have that conversation without lioning Indigenous resilience as a
life-lesson for non-indigenous peoples elsewhere? As Eve Tuck and C. Ree
put it:

[Damage narratives are the only stories that get told about me, unless
I\'m the one that\'s telling them. People have made their careers on
telling stories of damage about me, about communities like mine. Damage
is the only way that monsters and future ghosts are conjured (Tuck and
Ree,]{.underline} 2013[)]{.underline}

#### Green energy projects require maps that fail to represent Indigenous expertise

Rita Vasconcellos **Oliveira et al 24**, PhDs in Education and Education
of Philosophy and Religious Studies, MAs in Microbiology and Genetics
and Teaching of Biology and Geology, Researcher and Research Coordinator
for the Department of Industrial Economics and Technology Management at

"Hear the herd: the power of Sámi perspectives for achieving just

> 6 Under the lens of justice: some insights and recommendations
>
> From the interviews, and supported by general literature on the topic,
> it was possible to identify all [three dimensions of energy justice,
> as well as some ways how the Sámi People were/are being affected by
> the Norwegian energy transition strategy]{.underline}. The
> interviewees mentioned issues of recognition justice when they
> expressed how [the mainstream authorities and the ethnic majority did
> not properly consider their culture, living principles]{.underline}
> (e.g., "leave-no-trace") [and knowledge]{.underline} (TEK). In the
> same line, the contacted herders emphasized the relevance of respect
> to ensure a consistent recognition of the value of their traditional
> knowledge. [This value and recognition (in)justice were exemplified by
> how maps used for **energy project planning** and land management are
> elaborated]{.underline}. [Their opinion and expertise are not
> sufficiently included also because of how dominant scientific and
> governance **modes fail to acknowledge** the relevance of their
> individual and collective experiences.]{.underline}
>
> The interviewees mentioned equally issues of distributive justice
> probably in more subtle ways than with recognition shortcomings. To
> support and illustrate this claim, [consider the power imbalances over
> what information or representation are included in maps or how the
> herders do not want (to continue) being excessively burdened with the
> **negative impacts** of societal (in this case,
> **energy**]{.underline} decarbonization)
> **[transitions]{.underline}**. [By creating physical
> barriers]{.underline} (e.g., energy infrastructures, roads) and
> disruptions (e.g., flows of workers) in areas of reindeer grazing,
> [the Norwegian Sámi herders are being subjected to conditions for
> effective distributive injustice]{.underline}. [It is crucial to
> (re-)think concrete strategies and processes that mitigate this
> situation by addressing both the cause]{.underline} (e.g., use of
> areas for installing onshore wind parks) [and]{.underline} (e.g., loss
> of income, cultural erosion) also [ways to compensate]{.underline}, if
> possible and desired by the individuals, for the burdens of the
> Norwegian energy transition.
>
> Another facet of the distributive tenet of justice referred to by
> herders was in the form of the value of power. In the case of this
> article, [building and using maps in the context of communication of
> TEK revealed how it can be a struggle to integrate Indigenous know-how
> in these planning tools,]{.underline} also [because Western and
> Indigenous Knowledge are **not** always **easy to
> reconcile**.]{.underline} Still, scientific outcomes and scientific
> depictions hold extensive power and influence in decision-making that
> ultimately will influence the areas left for grazing.
>
> Finally, the Sámi interviewees highlighted the importance of the
> participatory process in clear reference to participatory justice.
> Designing inclusive participatory processes, especially given a
> history of exclusion, can be challenging, with many stakeholders
> rightfully hesitant to participate. Still, it is possible for
> policymakers to design and reinforce processes that ensure and
> facilitate adequate involvement of Indigenous People (and other
> minorities) in cases such as (e.g., public) research and
> governmental-supported and regulated business activities as mentioned
> previously. In direct relation, this study highlights the importance
> of these processes being based on principles of trust, and respect and
> that work to counteract power imbalances, especially those that are
> inherent when working with Indigenous Peoples. [Communicating
> knowledge]{.underline} about the barriers facing reindeer in nature
> [through maps can be an especially sensitive topic.]{.underline}
> Overarchingly, [there was a **fear that maps**,]{.underline} even
> those that depict barriers to herds\' movements, [would be used as
> justification for further **encroachment** on reindeer grazing land,
> rather than helping to protect the sustainable management of
> land]{.underline}. [This skepticism highlights that there is much work
> to do concerning creating spaces that facilitate participatory
> processes that are built on trust and respect and designed in ways
> that allow for co-creation and collaboration.]{.underline}

### AT: Link of Omission

#### Mapping is an ongoing process that requires subjects to continue to believe in and legitimize the artificial boundaries created by and for colonialism -- the K is not a link of omission

**Becker 22** (Matilda Becker, Strategic Partnerships Manager and a
Research Fellow at Oxford Net Zero; 2022; "Arctic mineral futures:
tracing geoscience knowledge from field to community in the Northwest
Territories, Canada";
https://ora.ox.ac.uk/objects/uuid:5220d418-281b-48d7-91de-8789fc8a149f)
-- Langham EY

[This thesis frames the subterranean as a space that is still in the
making and whose constitution is not yet settled. It views the
activities and products of **geological mapping as generative processes
that enable us to imagine the subterranean**, expand down into it and
draw it up into our surface worlds.]{.underline} As I will show in this
thesis, [**geological maps of the Arctic** today, as nearly 500 years
ago, contain reflections of societal meanings and aspirations of what
could or should become of a place, and resultingly, become a mechanism
to invent and **produce**]{.underline} (**[economic]{.underline}**)
**[spaces.]{.underline}**

[The production and realisation of imagined spaces is crucial to the
curation of socio-political and socio-environmental relations with
place.]{.underline} As political ecologists have noted, [**mapping** has
worked to **rationalise colonial interventions into colonised lands**
and supported the **transformation of lands'** existing ecosystems or
communities **into supporting** more **European, 'rationalised' forms of
spatial production**]{.underline} (c.f. Peluso, 1995; Tsing, 2005).
[**These actions enabled** forestry, mining, military or agricultural
use to become established and allowed **capitalist modes of accumulation
by dispossession to occur**]{.underline} (Harvey, 2003). While volumes
and the subterranean emerge in contemporary geographic literature as
relevant to territorial expansion, the extension of nation-states into
new geological frontiers using geological mapping techniques is
sometimes discussed as if it were a practice of the past, restricted to
early-stage (colonial) politics and histories of territorialisation
(Braun, 2000; Scott, 2008; Himley, 2019; Marston, 2019). However,
[**geological** **mapping**]{.underline}, and indeed mapping of
frontiers, [is **not** something that's **finished.** Geographers have
certainly not exhausted the critical leverage that new perspectives in
territorial politics and materiality offer to understand the meanings
and implications of different cartographies.]{.underline}

Geological mapping is not finished because many places that were
inaccessible 30 or 50 years ago have been brought closer to global
networks of geological knowledge and practice via a combination of
technological advances, changes in land tenure regimes, and climate
change opening up previously ice-covered spaces. Examples of this
include deep sea geologies (Childs, 2020), Antarctic landmasses (Yusoff,
2005) and parts of the Canadian Arctic, as discussed in this thesis.
[Expansion of human knowledge into new geological frontiers is therefore
an ongoing process: geological territorialisation and enrolment into
expansionist imaginations is not over, nor are the practices that enable
this to happen.]{.underline}

[Since geological mapping in 'remote' areas of the Earth]{.underline}
(such as parts of the Canadian Arctic or the sea floor) [draws the
natural environment into closer contact with human interference, **areas
that previously avoided intense industrial activity now stand to be
impacted by it.** As such, there is **increased pressure to** evaluate
not only what 'work' geological knowledge is doing to **rationalise
expansion into new**]{.underline} (subterranean) [**territories**, but
also a need to understand what trade-offs are made during the great
pinching together of surface and subsurface space.]{.underline} After
all, [geological knowledge production and the desire-driven philosophy
of resource extraction occurs against a backdrop of other land use
values and philosophies]{.underline} (Simpson, 2019). Political ecology
is well equipped to untangle what happens when these things meet: where
communities, environment and resources come into contact. However, the
story does not end, nor necessarily begin, there. There is, for example,
a need to understand who is making the knowledge we have about geology
and resources, how this happens and to what effect. How is the practice
of geological knowledge production supporting contemporary regimes and
dreamscapes of state-making, and who benefits and gets left behind in
the process?

#### The aff's assertion that their view of the Arctic is passive or "objective" normalizes the othering of Arctic peoples

Marjo **Lindroth** et al. **22**, D.Soc.Sc., University Researcher
(critical studies in Arctic politics) at the University of Lapland, with
34 published research works; published with Heidi Sinevaara-Niskanen and
Monica Tennberg; "Critical Studies of the Arctic; Unravelling the
North," p. 186-189, published by Palgrave MacMillan, 2022 \\\\\\ SDM

Contrapuntal Reading of Narratives

The work of Edward Said, a professor of literature and one of the
founders of post-colonial studies, has inspired IR scholars to consider
their discipline from a critical perspective (Bilgin, 2016, 2021; Duvall
& Varadarajan, 2007; Oliveira, 2020). [Said broadened the understanding
of colonialism from territorial control, political domination and
military rule to include issues of knowledge, language and
mentality]{.underline}. He explored how Western media, academia and
politicians have a distorted colonial lens called "Orientalism" on
non-Western peoples, societies and cultures (Said 1978).
[Orientalism]{.underline} establishes power relations with broad social,
political and economic implications between the Western centres and
peripheral, [imagined "Others" as marginal, peripheral, powerless and
lesser in value.]{.underline} Originally, Said focused on the relations
between the West and the Orient, but today [Orientalism is understood to
be a global phenomenon]{.underline}, and not alien to IR scholarship
(Saramago, 2020Tickner, 2016; Tucker, 2018). [In the Arctic, the idea
was introduced by cultural anthropologist Ann Fienup-Riordan using the
term "Eskimo-Orientalism"]{.underline} (Fienup-Riordan, 1995). Applying
Said's post-colonial theory [in the context of the Arctic, she used the
concept to describe the way Alaskan Eskimos were depicted in American
movies.]{.underline}

The IR narratives with strong Eurocentric, Western historical roots
struggle even today with lingering practices of "Othering" the
nonWestern world (Duvall & Varadarajan, 2007; Tickner, 2016; Tucker,
2018). [Orientalism produces a totalizing "Other"; that is, the Arctic
is represented as a unified, unitary entity from the perspective of
state sovereignty]{.underline}. [In IR, the Arctic is]{.underline} a
space that is ["deeply embedded within performances of 'paternal
sovereignty' in state-building, international relations and
security"]{.underline} (Norum, 2016, 55, see also Gricius, 2021). Thus,
the m[ainstream IR narratives restrict their "worldliness" to certain
agencies and relations through]{.underline} academic expertise,
linguistic practices and [claims of authority.]{.underline} [Arctic IR
narratives are normalized through their **repetition** in media,
politics and science in a way that they are seen as **true, objective
and apolitical**]{.underline}. The close [relations between research and
politics as well as between academic and political institutions keep
Orientalism alive (]{.underline}see Kamola, 2020, see also Ei-Malik,
2015).

[Orientalism is problematic in its ordering, valuing and differentiating
knowledge about the world]{.underline} in a particular way.
[IR]{.underline} narratives construct and [imagine the Arctic
paradoxically as an international region]{.underline} through a select
few regional markers, such as its [being a materially based,
economically significant "resource region" and]{.underline}, at the same
time, [having a vulnerable]{.underline}, fragile [nature in need of
protection]{.underline}, and by drawing selectively on existing
historical and contemporary narratives. [Such totalizing]{.underline},
yet paradoxical, [narratives]{.underline} focus on comparatively few
local particularities, [generalize them and establish them as the
overarching regional, politically significant markers]{.underline}.
[These narratives attain their official, authoritative and clearly
definable form in regional political processes]{.underline} (Larner,
2002, see also Paasi, 2009).

[Narratives inform us about social relations, authority and
power]{.underline}. Power relations are expressed in narratives by
[narrators]{.underline} who [position themselves and others in their
accounts of the world.]{.underline} IR narratives are often considered
"[methodologically elitist" with their focus on narratives of political
decision-makers]{.underline} and experts (Stanley & Jackson, 2016). Such
[narratives legitimize power relations, naturalize interactions and
mobilize action or enable inaction]{.underline}. The [totalized
narratives of the Arctic do not consider the diversity of the
region]{.underline}, [and produce a world view that does not match local
experiences, everyday lives and varied concerns]{.underline}. Even if
such insights from inside the Arctic are not reflected in IR narratives,
it does not mean that they do not matter, as the advocates of "Global
IR" point out (Bilgin, 2021; Tucker, 2018).

According to geographer Anssi Paasi (2021), "[Othering" is not a
constant, static and singular phenomenon]{.underline} that can simply be
wiped away as desired. Rather, "[it is a complex, dynamic and malleable
phenomenon, which is historically contingent, political and politicised
phenomenon that often finds new forms and that tends to multiply
borders]{.underline}" in social life (Paasi, 2021, 20). Critically
oriented scholars focus on how "[Othering" narratives continue to play a
significant role in making the Arctic world and employ their insights to
decolonize the IR narratives]{.underline} (Gricius, 2021, see also
Bravo, 2015; Jensen, 2015; Huggan, 2016).

Narratives are often understood as texts. Said warned that "[reading and
writing texts are never neutral activities: there are interests, power,
passions, pleasures entailed no matter how aesthetic or entertaining the
work]{.underline}" (Said 1993, 318). [Texts such as literary works and
curricula, as well as visual representations of different kinds **such
as maps**, show how contemporary Orientalism operates in IR
narratives]{.underline}. In the following, I will apply contrapuntal
reading to study the IR narratives of "Arctic exceptionalism" and the
"Global Arctic" in contemporary texts produced by Arctic IR scholars.
For Said, each text has both an interior and an exterior world of its
own. A text manifests its "worldliness", that is, a set of relations
with its world mediated by the author. Texts might be complex and
paradoxical, blurring Western and the non-Western, local and global,
centre and periphery at the same time. A paradox is a seemingly absurd
or contradictory situation. To capture this complexity, Said calls his
method "contrapuntal", evoking counterpoint, that is, music composed of
two or more relatively independent melodies and the way they sound
together. The method aims at a critical reading of texts to explore
their cultural backgrounds, intertwined colonial roots and paradoxes.
For IR scholars, the method makes it possible to not only establish new
connections in thinking about "worldliness", but also uncover already
existing relations and gaps in them; it also encourages new ways to see
problems in world politics (Bilgin, 2016; Chowdhry, 2007).

### AT: Maps inevitable

#### **Land doesn't have to be mapped with border and strict spatial boundaries**

**Remy 3** \[Lola. "Making the map speak: Indigenous animated
cartographies as contrapuntal spatial representations." NECSUS,
12-6-2018.
https://necsus-ejms.org/making-the-map-speak-indigenous-animated-cartographies-as-contrapuntal-spatial-representations/\]
Ayan Mahajan 🤪

In Adam Loften and Emmanuel Vaughan-Lee's short documentary Counter
Mapping (2018), Jim Enote, a Zuni farmer and director of the A:shiwi
A:wan Museum and Heritage Center in New Mexico, declares:

**[\[t\]o assume that people would look at the earth only from a vantage
point that is above and looking straight down doesn't consider the
humanity of living on the landscape. Saying that there's a pond, there
are cattails, there are turtles in that water -- that is a different
view that expands the human experience of a place.]{.underline}**

[A central concern of Indigenous mapping, as stressed by Margaret W.
Pearce, is the question of **expressing 'the geographies of human
experience and place in the map', far from a view of the land from
above, devoid of life and interactions.**\[43\] How can a map become a
testimony of the Indigenous experience and subjectivity? An answer to
this question lies in the practice of cognitive, or mental, mapping.
This type of map-making, simply put, captures the representations of
people's perception of an area, of their 'imaginary relationship to
\[their\] real conditions of existence'.]{.underline}\[44\] Originally
developed in relation to the urban experience of a fragmented space, it
was then used in various situations as an alternative to scientific
cartography.\[45\] For her video installation Memory Cards (2013),
Mohawk artist Hannah Claus, member of the Tyendinaga Mohawks of the Bay
of Quinte (birthdate unknown-), collaborated with First Nation
communities from Carleton-sur-Mer, Listuguj, and Gesgapegiag in Quebec.
She invited people to draw personal maps of familiar places of their
choice (their 'favourite territories'), and filmed the process of their
drawing. Using a self-constructed apparatus, she filmed their drawings'
evolution through (slightly transparent) paper. As a result, one can see
the image appearing out of nowhere, and our eyes follow the animated
line traced by the pencil with a sense of indeterminacy. Sometimes, the
drawer's hand casts a shadow on the paper, giving a sense of embodiment
and diffuse presence to the maps. Her installation, a floor-based
projection, superimposes two drawings or more, creating a play of
transparency and inscription. **[Refusing a single interpretative frame,
the superimposition of images insists on the communal aspect of
counter-cartography and on the multiplicity of perceptions of a given
space.]{.underline}**

Here, the film depicts map-making as a subjective process, and not
simply a product. The original French title of her work, Cartes
Mémoires, explicitly links maps (cartes) to memory and subjectivity. In
the hands of non-professional communities, they become a tool for
thinking about space in an embodied and personal way, cut off from
scientific measurements and objectivity. With her work, Hannah Claus
returns to the Indigenous tradition of map-making illustrating one's
personal stories. The performative aspect of Indigenous maps is further
transmitted through the immediacy of the apparatus, and the use of film
as a recording device. Rather than an argument about land ownership and
use, Cartes Mémoires presents a more intimate framework, transcribing
the subjectivity and memories of the community against their erasure by
official state maps as noted by Eades. Furthermore, Claus complicates
the colonial state's demarcation and bordering of Indigenous territory.
Rather than conforming to scientific cartographic codes, the circular
shape of the paper allows for an alternative representation of space.
Whereas some maps mirror the format of the frame and use a circular
composition, most of them seem to bleed out of the framed space and
expand in the mind of their maker.

By giving the means of animated cartography to Indigenous communities,
Hannah Claus and other filmmakers claim a new kind of representation of
space, one that includes different bodies and experiences and contains
the potential to threaten a colonial geography. In one scene of Jack
Pettibone Riccobono's (birthdate unknown-) controversial documentary The
Seventh Fire (2015), William 'Bill' Brown, an Ojibwe community member of
the White Earth Indian Reservation (Minnesota) draws a map of the
reservation to help the filmmaker locate Rob Brown, the person he is
looking for. This scene is shortly followed by an aerial shot of the
land in question, which starkly opposes the imagined and lived geography
with the synoptic view characteristic of American survey teams.\[46\]

Against the disembodied, impersonal rendition of the reservation,
characteristic of official governmental maps, this scene conveys the
experience and perception of a colonial space from the inside. It
reintroduces subjectivity (not only the narrator's but also the people
he mentions) into the spatial imaginary of the reservation. Through the
drawing and the voice of the map-maker, we are also told an indirect
narrative of Rob Brown: where he lives, who he is close to and visits
often, his habits, etc. The voice, followed by the movements of the
camera over the drawing, directs us through the space of the reserve in
a linear way, similar to the panning of Obomsawin's camera over the
historical documents she is commenting on. The question of scale and
perspective, as noted by Martin Mitchell, is at the centre of an
Indigenous approach to mapmaking.\[47\] In Obomsawin's and Riccobono's
films, cartography becomes a way to emphasise the relationality inherent
to space, the links between places, people, and things, as Jim Enote
describes in Counter Mapping:

[**\[m\]odern maps don't have a memory. For me, the whole landscape
around here is home. I have patterned languages that help me to remember
how I get from one place to another.** I go to my field in the summer. I
collect wood in the fall and winter. I may be pinion picking or going to
collect tea. This whole constellation of what makes up a map to me has
always been far beyond a piece of paper.]{.underline}

### !\-\--Euro-Colonialism

#### Mapping is a colonialist tool used [perpetuate]{.underline} eurocentrism and racial hierarchies

Bill **Ashcroft et al. '25**, PhD, Emeritus Professor in the School of

CARTOGRAPHY (MAPS AND MAPPING) Both literally and metaphorically, [maps
and mapping are dominant practices of colonial and post-colonial
cultures]{.underline}. [Colonization itself is often consequent on a
voyage of 'discovery', a bringing into being of 'undiscovered'
lands]{.underline}. The process of [discovery is reinforced by the
construction of maps,]{.underline} [whose existence is a means of
textualizing the spatial reality of the other,]{.underline} naming or
,in almost all cases, [renaming spaces in a symbolic and literal act of
mastery and control]{.underline}. In all cases [the lands so colonized
are **literally reinscribed**, written over,]{.underline} as [the names
and languages of the indigenes are replaced]{.underline} by new names,
[or are **corrupted** into new and Europeanized forms by the
cartographer]{.underline} and explorer. [This was a long-standing
feature of colonialism]{.underline} and not restricted to distant
unknown lands. Irish playwright Brian Friel graphically illustrates this
in Translations, which deals with [the mapping and renaming of the West
of Ireland by the English]{.underline} army in the nineteenth century,
[a process in which the native Irish (Gaelic) culture is literally
overwritten by English imperialism.]{.underline} [Maps also **inscribe
their ideology** on territory in numerous way]{.underline}s other than
place-names. [The blank spaces of early maps signify a literal **terra
nullius**, an open and inviting (**virginal**) space into which the
European imagination can project itself and]{.underline} into which [the
European (usually male) explorer must **penetrate**]{.underline}. Such
[blank spaces invite other cultural superscriptions, such as the
elaborately drawn monsters and **sub-human wild-men** (savages) of most
early maps]{.underline}. Imaginative transferences are frequent. Thus
[early maps of Australia]{.underline} (Terra Incognita or Terra
Incognita Australis as it was known in the seventeenth century when the
first European maps of the northern coast-line were drawn) [show an
interior populated by elephants and pygmies]{.underline}. Similarly [the
so-called cannibals of the Indies]{.underline} (a term derived from the
corruption of the Spanish name for the Carib peoples) [appear frequently
on maps of the interior of Africa]{.underline}. Indeed[,]{.underline} by
the early twentieth century, the term is almost synonymous with Africans
in popular literature and humour, as witness the numerous cartoons that
represent cannibals as black people with bones through their noses
roasting missionaries in a pot. [The fictionality of the narratives of
such processes of 'discovery', which the process of mapping objectifies,
is emphasized by the role of the native guide in such explorations, who
leads the explorer]{.underline} to the interior. [The prior knowledge of
the land that this dramatizes, and which **cannot be wholly silenced**
in the written accounts of these explorations, **is ignored, and
literally silenced** by the act of mapping]{.underline}, [since the
indigenous people **have no voice** or even presence that can be heard
in the new discourse of scientific measurement and written texts that
cartography implies.]{.underline} [They figure, if at all, only as
illustration -- savages, cannibals or monsters. The allegorization of
space (and its hierarchization) is also defined by maps, first by the
decision as to which projection to favour,since this privileges some
land-spaces over others]{.underline}; or [by the decision to orient all
maps on a North--South axis]{.underline}; or [by the creation of a body
of special maps that divide the land into such 'objective' categories as
climatic regions, population densities and, not least, natural
resources.]{.underline} [Maps can also serve as (allegorical) **tools of
exploitation**]{.underline}.In his analysis of Mercator's Atlas, José
Rabasa (1993) demonstrates how [the projection map became a major **tool
of Euro-centrism**, defining European latitudes as pivotal reference
points for the conception of a **world hierarchy**,]{.underline} and
[embodying as geographical fact European attitudes about]{.underline}
the nature of [the world]{.underline}. In effect, [the European map
created]{.underline} what has remained [the contemporary geographical
world reality.]{.underline} [The definition of cultures as tropical in a
world where temperate is the norm]{.underline}, [or]{.underline} as
[densely populated or thinly populated]{.underline},[or]{.underline} as
[resource rich or poor]{.underline}, [are essential discriminations upon
which]{.underline} a practice of [exploitation or **control** can be
erected]{.underline}. That [these techniques also feature in such modern
controlling **discourses as 'development'**]{.underline} studies
[illustrates how persistently the sciences of cartography and mapping
have contributed to the establishment and reestablishment of the
**various world orders**]{.underline} of the last few centuries.
[Although ethnography]{.underline} (the supposedly objective scientific
description of 'primitive'peoples and cultures) [has frequently borne
the brunt of anti-colonial attacks]{.underline} as being the principal
intellectual discourse of colonization, [it is worth noting that
geography and geographers,and the science of **cartography, played at
least as important a role in** underpinning the objects and values of
**the colonial enterprise**]{.underline}. The Royal Geographical Society
was a prime mover in the imperial conquests of the 'undiscovered'
regions of the world, and it is significant that as Kipling's Kim
illustrates (the cover of the Head of the Secret Service in that text,
Creighton Sahib, is that of the Director of the General Survey of
