most ubiquitous figure of imperial control**.]{.underline}

### !\-\--Indigenous Erasure

#### Cartography is made to appear neutral but it serves a political function: it is a form of intellectual colonization that justifies the epistemological and material erasure of indigenous life.

**Kumar '23,** Professor of International Relations and Global Politics,
Department of Political Science, University of Delhi; Sanjeev Kumar H.M.
Decolonizing Grand Theories: Postcolonial Ontology, Historical Sociology
and Mid‑Level Theories in International Relations. Palgrave Macmillan
Singapore, September 8 2023. <https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-99-4841->
Craig

This book begins by reading into the [decolonial argument that the
**colonial** matrix of power largely tends to transform modernity into
an instrument of domination which manifested in the colonization of
**political cartographies**.]{.underline} [As regards the colonial
matrix of power is concerned, it is characterized by the
operationalization of **ontological** and **episte- mological** binaries
and hierarchies that was engendered by the unequal politico-economic and
**psycho**-cultural relations between the colonizing metropolitan core
and the colonized periphery. Such an unequal rela- tionship of
**domination** and **subjugation** emerged as crucial elements in
determining the structural/functional conditionalities of the working of
international relations, as well as the epistemologies that attempted to
explain the same. It happened as the grand theories of International
Rela- tions that have assumed the status of the disciplinary canon
tended to impart a kind of epistemological legitimacy to the unequal
relationship between the Western **metropolitan** core and the
non-Western periphery. Such a legitimacy was mediated through the
**hegemonic** epistemolog- ical and pedagogical practices of the grand
theories of **IR**. In this way, the grand theories in IR tended to
demonstrate a kind of deep-seated neocolonial imperial attributes that
are disseminated across the world as axiological postulates of the
**metaphysics** of cosmopolitanism. Considering this, it may be stated
that any satisfactory explanation of the nature of the working of IR,
hence must involve an epistemological engagement with the
**postcolonial** **ontology**.]{.underline} Such an engagement manifests
as an exigency vii viii PREFACE when we begin unpacking the ontic and
epistemic foundations of the disciplinary matrix of the subject of IR.
[As we pursue this very process of **mapping**, it becomes evident that
the disciplinary matrix of IR has largely evolved by way of treading a
teleolog- ical trajectory of synchronic **Eurocentric** and
**ethnocentric** history. Owing to such a teleological trajectory, the
scope and subject matter of IR has deeply grounded itself into a
paradigmatic thrust on Western ontic and epistemic **supremacism**. With
such a supremacist epistemological asser- tion, the world considerably
gets interpreted from the mere lens of the stagiest doctrinal progress
of European history.]{.underline} By virtue of such a West- centric and
ethnocentric intellectual project, a section of the theory in the
disciplinary matrix of IR has incarnated into the status of a canon. On
its part, the elite minority of the epistemic community of IR, who are
the treasurers of such a canon, have been hypostatized into the status
of the apostles. [The **hegemony** of the discourse of such a canon and
the hierarchy of the theoreticians privy to such a discourse tends to be
medi- ated through the universalizing hegemonic tropes of knowledge that
are embodied in the 'grand theories' of IR.]{.underline} Hence, for any
project that aims at making the discipline of IR more cosmopolitan in
character that would commend a wider contextual significance, the
dismantling of the theistic doctrinal hegemony and the primacy of the
sacrosanct position of grand theories of IR manifest as pressing
disciplinary imperatives. [Such an enterprise in the act of dismantling
seems to have become inevitable, mainly because the grand theories are
largely situated in a kind of an **axiological** postulate of the need
for the substantiation of the meta-histories that are constituted
through an epistemic focus upon the local history of Western
civilization. This kind of focus on the **local** history of **Europe**
renders world history to appear as a mere **reductionist** synchronic
narrative. It is in such a narrow ontic narrative on history and the
supremacism of Western epistemology that the meta-theoretical
foundations of the discipline of IR tend to be pivoted. The tropes of
knowledge that are produced in such a circumstance considerably shape
the pedagogical and epistemological processes in the discipline of
Interna- tional Relations.]{.underline} Such a heuristic
over-determinism that characterizes the technical discourse of the
discipline of International Relations is firmly grounded in the
ontological roots of a Eurocentric interpretation of the past and an
ethnocentric definition of the epistemology of IR. This kind of
disciplinary ontology is propelled by what has been desig- nated as IR's
Westphalian common-sense. For such a common-sense, our PREFACE ix
present-day international society has directly descended from the
universal European system. Owing to this, the technical discourse of the
disci- pline of IR has largely tended to be shaped in terms of the
grammar of the Westphalian conception of international order. By virtue
of this, the paradigm of Westphalia tends to act as the core ontological
principle driving the formation of grand theories in IR. What gets
erased in such an epistemological trajectory is the very diachronic
history of international relations that is constituted by the pres- ence
of a world that is outside Europe. Such a world that is designated as
the postcolonial spatio-temporal topoi seems to have been regarded as a
disjunctive structural imaginary, a historical break and an epistemolog-
ical lag by the dominant hegemonic technical discourse of the discipline
of IR. Considering this kind of epistemology of IR, the task at hand is
to look at historical narratives by situating them in their specific
spatio- temporal idiosyncrasies. To do this, the imperative is to
decolonize the meta-theoretical foundations of IR. In order to do this,
the restruc- turing of the ontological, epistemological, methodological
and theoretical underpinnings of the discipline becomes an exigency. An
attempt has been made in this book to undertake such a task of
restructuring, by interrogating the disciplinary matrix of IR at the
level of meta-theory. This interrogation has been situated largely on
the plank of ontology and it is done by critically examining the working
of grand theories in IR. Based on this, a case has been made in this
book for placing mid-level theories at the core of the ontological
concerns of the discipline. Such a thrust on the significance of
mid-level theories, it has been argued in this book, goes a long way in
the restructuring of the disciplinary matrix of IR at the level of
meta-theory. Taking cognizance of this, the book has been designed as a
venture toward committing a kind of disciplinary heresy. This is because
it seeks to question the deep-seated conceptual grammar and the
meta-theoretical structure of the grand theories of the discipline of
IR. Such a struc- ture and content of the grand theories have
considerably contributed to the substantiation of the imperializing
nature of the epistemology of the discipline[. In any case, the origin
and growth of such a process of epis- temological **imperialism**
happened as the product of the genealogies of the West-centric
ethnocentric disciplinary matrix of IR.]{.underline} However, such an
argument seems to be a mere reductionist one that renders a less-than-
perfect explanation for the sustenance of the hegemony of grand theories
x PREFACE that are produced in the West. Rather, the rote
epistemological and peda- gogical practices that have characterized the
structure and content of the disciplinary history of IR seem to have
been equally responsible. Such practices are manifest in the mimetic
reproduction of the arguments of the grand theories that are undertaken
for [the purpose of mapping the terrain of the 'international', a task
that is done by being completely oblivious of the phenomenological
caveat toward the need for suspension of our intentions and the
hermeneutic postulate of recognizing the contextual significance of any
phenomenon.]{.underline} [Such epistemic endeavors factor deeply in
perpetuating the **intellectual** **colonization** of the discipline of
IR by the imperializing heuristic power of the grand
theories.]{.underline} Hence, even at the cost of committing a kind of
apostasy, I dare to question the established canon that has been
disseminated across generations. By doing this, I present to the
readers, my ideas for liberating the discipline of IR from the
colonizing mental yoke of the grand theories. In any case, while the
book undertakes such a task, I wish to render a caveat at the very
outset. I intend to make it very clear here that neither I am aiming to
engage in any kind of a nihilistic endeavor at dismantling a particular
form of theory, nor do I seek to propose any kind of liberation theology
that would venture toward recrafting the disciplinary matrix of IR by
way of scripting its theoretical space on a clean slate. Rather, the
simplest objective in this book is to involve myself in a flirtatious
but not very dilettantish engagement with second-order theorizing. While
I do this, the aim here would be to carry out a rigorous critique of the
grand theories of IR which form part of first-order theorizing. Such a
pursuit of course cannot be undertaken by being oblivious to the need
for adherence to the logic of pedantic precision and methodological
agnosticism.

#### Western cartography and geographical knowledge production always pushes indigenous understandings under the rug.

**Koushik 16** \[Jada Renee. "Considerations of place in sustainability
education policy: How local contexts inform the engagement of
sustainability in education policy enactment and practice", Journal of
Sustainability Education Vol. 11, 2-xx-2016\] Ayan Mahajan 🤪

There are many connections between capital and place, and places are in
competition with other places for highly mobile resources (Cresswell,
2004). Capitalism is dependent on its spatial fixes, and it "builds and
rebuilds a geography in its own image" (Harvey, 2000, p. 54). The
tension between capital and place is accentuated by neoliberal policies
and discourses, many of which seek to commodify places and re-story
histories to align with trends in extraction and consumption. The term
neoliberalism is used to describe the currently dominant system of
global governance that promotes free market conditions that "prioritize
corporations and economic growth over considerations of social equity or
environmental protection" (Tuck & McKenzie, 2015, p. 3). In today's
society, regrettably, commodifying everything and subjecting almost all
transactions to a cost-benefit analysis is the dominant way of thinking
(Harvey, 2000). Moreover, "\[n\]eoliberalism builds on a western
trajectory of cultural norms and practices" (McKenzie, 2012, p. 165),
while neo-liberal policy agendas have brought to light differentiating
spatial effects of unequal provision (Gulson & Symes, 2007). [An example
of how power influences place can be witnessed amongst Native American
and First Nations students living in the United States and Canada,
whereby the scientific method/**Western worldview oftentimes coexists
with Native spiritually along with a deep connection with the land and
animals. In this vein, power represents "the living energy that inhabits
and/or composes the universe, and place being the relationship of things
to each other"**]{.underline} (Deloria, 2001, p. 23). [**The connections
that Indigenous people have to places are undeniably some of the
strongest in the world.** As such, "Indigenous people represent a
culture emergent from a place, and they actively draw on the power of
that place, physically and spiritually"]{.underline} (Wildcat, 2001a, p.
32). [Furthermore, **tribal persons "find meaning in the world and
recognize through experience that they are of a people and
place"**]{.underline} (Wildcat, 2001b, p. 145). [This enduring sense of
place within Indigenous communities is not static, instead place serves
as a location for "contemporary Indigenous knowledge and language
practices to comingle with others, change, and yet persist"]{.underline}
(Hornberger & McCarty, 2012, p. 6). **[Many Indigenous epistemologies
view the world as a dynamic and living place, but many of these
perspectives have been challenged and/or supplanted by dominant
European/Western worldviews]{.underline}** (Lucas, 2013). **[Indigenous
communities use place/land as central reference point; in contrast,
Western communities use time as the narrative of central
importance]{.underline}** (Coulthard, 2010). **[Through the processes of
colonialism, Europeans worldview became known as The World History,
displacing all other experiences and cultural histories around the
globe]{.underline}** (Deloria, 2001; Simpson, 2002). **[This has had a
devastating impact on how we view and interact with places, since most
times the Western worldview is unable to understand any other history,
as it is the "truth" and superior to all others]{.underline}** (Deloria,
2001; Lucas, 2013). "Until relatively recently in human history, the
social activities through which distinctive forms of knowledge are
produced have for the most part been localized" (Gough, 2002, p. 1223).
Unfortunately, colonization has allowed Western thought to travel around
the world, appearing to be devoid of any one cultural fingerprint; an
undeniable universal truth and rational way of thinking (Gough, 2002).
While this Western conception of place, rooted in the power of
colonialism and resource extraction, narrows the possibilities of how
humans interact in and with places; the history of colonization and
cultural oppression creates a context for shared experiences that tie
identity to place (Kana'iaupuni & Malone, 2010). Wildcat (2001b) posits
that "[\[p\]lace is not merely the relationship of things, resources or
objects, it is the site where dynamic processes of interaction occur
-where processes between other living beings or other-than-human persons
occur]{.underline}" (p. 144). Thus, "\[e\]ducation space is therefore
revealed as fluid, interconnected and constructed through power
relations" (Cook & Hemming, 2011, p. 6). Coulthard (2010) states that
["\[p\]lace is a way of knowing, experiencing, and relating with the
world -- and these ways of knowing often guide forms of resistance to
power relations that threaten to erase or destroy our senses of
place"]{.underline} (p. 79). [This resistance to power is articulated by
arguing against the contemporary pathological drive for expansion,
growth, and accumulation]{.underline} (colonial-capitalist ideologies).
Coulthard (2010) stresses the importance of sharing, egalitarianism,
respect for one another and the land, and autonomy, thus nurturing a
sense of place. Sense of place research considers how people connect
with places and how those connections influence their sentiments and
behaviours towards the environment, which may have significant
implications for sustainability education (Ardoin, Schuch & Gould,
2012).

### !\-\--Genocide

#### Western forms of cartography facilitate the domination of external settlers over indigenous populations, greenlighting genocide in the name of exploration and discovery.

**Tsiouvalas & Solski 23** \[Apostolos. Jan Jakub. "'One Map to Rule
Them All'? Revisiting Legalities Through Cartographic Representations of
the Northwest Passage." Taylor & Francis, 4-18-2023.
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00908320.2023.2273288#d1e261\]
Ayan Mahajan 🤪

When looking at how cartography marks legalities within space, a
causational circular relationship between cartography and law can be
observed: [**Established legal orders may be incorporated on a map,
while, in turn, a map may put forward existing legal or political claims
over space in support of a legal argument. In other words, mapping
becomes both a "requirement" and a "tool" of power over space,4 since
maps operate as instruments of legal power, capable of narrating,
representing, generating, or even (re)claiming territory.**5 Drawing
lines on space (what Deleuze and Guattari call "striation")6 and
representing it as a horizontal flattened Euclidean surface7 have been
historically a necessary precondition for the creation of sovereign
states, **aiming to conceptualize space as "territory" and establish
sovereign power over a demarcated area,8 wherein the sovereign entity
succeeds independence from external unconsented intervention (or
interference) and determines the monopoly of violence.** Territory as a
bounded space in international law appears as a historical,
geographical, political, and juridical concept that is in constant need
of being exercised and performed by sovereign entities. **The concept of
"territory" is thus not a state of "existence" but a "semiotic practice"
originating from the land, which, in turn, gives existential meaning to
space and makes it amenable to sovereign power, appropriation, and legal
treatment.**9 In turn, the manner according to which states'
geographical limits are determined, as well as the way authority and
control over a people are exercised, is further known as
territoriality.]{.underline}10 Taking, however, the concept of territory
as a granted in thinking about space and idealizing the territorial
state as the sole molder of spatiality fall under what Agnew labels as a
"territorial trap."11 Maps thus as an instrumental means of
territorializing space and drawing lines upon it have historically borne
the responsibility of perpetuating the territorial trap of international
law. Yet with borders shifting, new actors emerging, and legal as well
as geopolitical relationships constantly changing, cartography becomes a
complex and ever-evolving tool in determining law's relationship to
space.12

Early modern European cartographic representations of the world provide
valuable information about how actors thought about political space,
organization, and authority upon it.13 Since the beginning of
colonization and the dawning of international law, the study of the
world gained enormous impetus from overseas voyaging that necessitated
an increase in both navigation and cosmography.14 [From the 15th century
onward, cartography has served as an instrumental means for European
nations to ascertain sovereignty over space and presume imperial claims
over previously uncharted areas legally conceptualized as, among others,
"terra incognita," "terra nullius," or "vacuum
domicilium."]{.underline}15 Cartography was of decisive importance in
these imperial endeavors of European nations, as the world previously
"unknown" to the west needed to be illustrated as a "blank space" on
maps, thereby opening new avenues for sovereign claims and colonial
expansion.16 [Against the backdrop of a territorial conceptualization of
space, the colonial world was subject to the acquisition of title
through occupation, since "space" in the preexisting societies of the
"new" world was not territorially organized by a sovereign authority,
delineated boundaries, and land ownership in a Western sense, and thus
was considered to be "vacant."17 **More specifically, nomadic societies
were seen as the furthest from sovereign authority, as they lacked
permanent territorial confinements and consistent agriculture and
fisheries operations: facts that allowed European empires to justify
colonization.**]{.underline}18 This dialectic between an imagined vacant
space and the processes of drawing lines on it is well anchored in the
foundations of the international legal order, and cartography served as
a major tool for the latter to attain its expansion.

[In this context, throughout the age of "discovery"---or better,
"rediscovery," as De Gourdon puts it19---the conceptualization of the
colonial world as a "terra nullius" waiting for European authority and
resource extraction was supported by maps of large void spaces that
portrayed the Americas (and not least other parts of the world) as an
empty territory open for appropriation and exploitation.20 Under the
doctrine of discovery, the newly charted areas soon became the European
states' transoceanic territories, vacant of any pre-\\existing authority
and hence susceptible to "occupation" and subject to the colonial rule's
political and economic ambitions.21 Soon after, cartography became a
device to **enable slavery, forced relocations of Indigenous
communities, systematized assimilation policies, massive land theft, and
even genocides** in the newly "discovered" world.22 Under this
rationale, America soon became a "produced" European territory charted
through maps and legal documents that established jurisdictions in
bounded spaces, while dispossessing and destructing preexisting
Indigenous populations.23 **In turn, non-Western conceptualizations of
space constructed through competing epistemologies were deemed inferior
or incorrect and misrecognized in many cases.24**]{.underline}

### !\-\--Racial Violence

#### The endless cycle of racial discrimination due to the temperate systems of where they subjugate indigenous peoples in an endless cycle of inferiority or rather know as [Temperate-Normativity.]{.underline}

**Smith 25,** JR Smith is an assistant Professor of American Indian
Studies and Geography at the University of Washington;\
Jen Rose. Ice Geographies: The Colonial Politics of Race and Indigeneity
in the Arctic. Chapter 3, Duke University\
Press, May 13 2025.//ganta

The [rise of **scientific racism** in the mid- nineteenth century
biologized race as something measured through supposedly innate
characteristics of the body.]{.underline} Before that, many philosophers
attributed human difference to geography and climate, a concept now
referred to as "[environmental determinism]{.underline}." The rise of
environmental determinism is often attributed to work by Ellen C. Semple
in the early twentieth century, which drew on Friedrich Ratzel's
Anthropogeographie (1882 and 1891).15 In that text, Ratzel influentially
analyzed "the geographical distribution of societies, the relation
between migration and the physical environment, and . . . the influence
of environments upon societies." 16 Environmental deter- minism was not
a unitary theory agreed upon across all thinkers but was a common theme
that transversed multiple conversations long before Ratzel. Scholars
drawing upon and criticizing his inferences rippled out following his
work. Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel writes in The Philosophy of History
that "[groups who lived in certain climatic zones should not be
considered as historical actors.]{.underline} . . . In the Frigid and in
the Torrid zone the locality of World- historical peoples cannot be
found." 18 For Hegel, inhabitants of the "frigid" and "torrid" locales
of the globe should not be considered as actors in the history of the
world. Hegel's characterization of appropriate historical actors
emerging from particular geographies was not created in a vacuum. His
influences were many and those influences constitute a genealogy that
can be traced back further still. The set of quotes that follow are not
exhaustive but [demonstrate the discursive proliferation of linking
climate and geography to racialized bodily characteristics, especially
in the Arctic]{.underline}. One common dimension of such thinking was
that [peoples of Arctic environments]{.underline} were the [strongest
example of environ- mental determinism that resulted in an inferior
intelligence.]{.underline} Georges- Louis Leclerc, also known as the
Comte de Buffon, wrote that "nothing can afford a stronger example of
the influence of climate than this race of Laplanders, who are situated,
along the whole polar circle . . . the breadth of which is limited by
nothing but excessive cold; for that race totally dis- appears whenever
the climate becomes a little." 19 Similarly, David Hume argued that "all
the nations, which live beyond the polar circles or between the tropics,
are inferior to the rest of the species, and are incapable of all the
higher attainments of the human mind." 20 In that same writing, Hume
references George (Bishop) Berkeley, who claimed that "the native of a
cold climate" have some intelligence, but "the north- ern geniuses are
like melons, of which not one in fifty is good; but when it is so, it
has an exquisite relish." 21. According to these men, Indigenous peoples
of the Arctic suffer from their surrounding northern climes, and by
contrast temperate zones have the opposite influence. [Immanuel
Kant's]{.underline} lectures from the 1750s to the 1790s include this
quote: "In the hot countries the human being matures in all aspects
earlier, but [does not, however, **reach the perfection** of those in
the temperate zones]{.underline}. Humanity is at its [greatest
perfection in the race of the whites."]{.underline} 22 Comte de Buffon
agrees, stating, "The [most **temperate climate**]{.underline} . . .
[produces the most handsome and beautiful men]{.underline}. It is from
this climate that the ideas of the genuine colour of mankind, and of the
various degrees of beauty, ought to be derived." 23 Hegel again concurs
and summarizes these relating bodies of thought when he wrote that "the
true theatre of History is therefore the temperate zone." 24 These
excerpts demonstrate the [perceived superiority of whiteness brought
about by the geographies of a temperate zone]{.underline} as well as a
supposed nongenerativity and [**inferiority** of both ice
geographies]{.underline} and peoples of a "frigid or torrid zone" or
"[polar circle]{.underline}." I call this **[phenomenon temperate-
normativity]{.underline}**.

### !\-\--Gender Violence

#### Cartography makes otherization inevitable and replicates hierarchies like that of gender

Marjo **Lindroth** et al. **22**, D.Soc.Sc., University Researcher
(critical studies in Arctic politics) at the University of Lapland, with
34 published research works; published with Heidi Sinevaara-Niskanen and

To grasp the discourse around Grind and the confusion that it has
caused, following Baumgarten, one has to analyse what has been fused
together in its aesthetic constitution and proceed to disentangle the
values, moral status, ethics, invested power relations and politics that
the case is pregnant with. This is not undertaken to "judge" the work of
art or its interpretations, to assess its value or to see whether
something has been "misused" or "misunderstood". [The purpose of the
inquiry is to examine how the work reflects the aesthetic constitution
of the Arctic and the politics of aestheticization.]{.underline}

Grind is a fusion of cultural signifiers, displaying a stereotypical
Arctic and the indigenous as a part of it. In addition to direct Sámi
references, such as commercial replicas of gákti, the men's headpiece
and footwear, the video includes possible references to Russia, such as
a black scarf with red floral patterns. The same scarf worn as a
headband brings to mind the First Nations of North America and the
leather fringes and geometrical patterns on fabrics in the video
resemble those used by the Navajo. The fur hats in the video may suggest
any Arctic area, as direct indications of the region's cold weather.
[Where the references to the Arctic are intermingled, so, too, are those
to the indigenous peoples living inside and outside the
region]{.underline}. Uusitalo (2017) describes the aesthetics as evoking
"murky" northern culture, [indicating the elusiveness and general nature
of visual elements that refer to hardly anybody or anything specific.
This fusion reveals common denominators: phantasy, Otherness, exoticism
and erotization.]{.underline}

[The geographical distance]{.underline} of the country's southern from
its northern regions [has made it possible to use Lapland as Finland's
"internal Other"]{.underline} (Hiltunen, 2019, 87). [The nationalistic
project of becoming a civilized European nation required providing a
counterpart from which to distance oneself, namely the Sámi, who have
been characterized as a backward and inferior people]{.underline}
(ibid., 87). Like Africa, Sápmi, [the Sámi homeland, was turned into an
exotic, constructed space used as a counterpart to the conceited
centre]{.underline} (Nordin & Ojala, 2018, 63). One sees the same
mindset at work when Europe defined the Orient as its opposite; [neither
had any more than imaginary ontological stability]{.underline} (ibid.,
88). [For colonial rule, a racial geography and orientalist epistemology
with regard to sexual boundaries constituted masculinized
management]{.underline} (Levine, 2000, 7, 13). [In]{.underline} [popular
representations]{.underline}, such as films, Lapland [was a romantic and
exotic locale]{.underline} (ibid., 89): [Sámi women]{.underline} in The
White Reindeer (1952) [possess]{.underline} [supernatural and sexual
powers]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} in Sensuela (1973) they [exhibit
unrestrained sexual behaviour]{.underline} (Hiltunen, 2019, 91), [more a
facet of nature rather than culture]{.underline} (ibid., 98).

Ulrike Schaper et al. [use a portmanteau word "sexotic" for the
intersection of sexualization and exoticization, a strictly relational
category that "build\[s\] on alleged differences in the sexual drive,
attitudes towards sexuality and sexual behavior]{.underline}, [which
construct them as the origin and determinant of the exotic quality" that
also "constructs the 'exotic' as sexually attractive, desirable and
stimulating]{.underline} \[...\] for example, [the sexual fetishization
of a certain skin color" (]{.underline}2020, 115). [Before the sexuality
discourse of the eighteenth century]{.underline}, the French term
"[exotic" referred to the natural world of flora and fauna]{.underline},
and [came to suggest race only after the human being became regarded
primarily as a biological being (]{.underline}ibid., 117). Although
[this naturalization]{.underline} did not concern all of humanity, it
[played into racial and gender stereotypes, equating non-European women
as wild, sensual and permissive]{.underline} (ibid.), [resembling the
aesthetic division in which the natural and the feminine share the same
qualities of irrationality and immorality.]{.underline}

Arctic]{.underline} specifically? [What may queer studies contribute to
Arctic studies]{.underline}? And [how do queer studies discuss with
Native or indigenous studies]{.underline}? When searching for academic
analyses on queer themes in the Arctic, one hardly finds an abundance of
publications as yet: some conference workshops focusing on queerness in
the North (e.g., Queering Indigeneity, 2018), a few journal articles and
book chapters on the topic (e.g., Huuki & Kyrölä, 2022; Kyrölä & Huuki,
2021; Peltomaa, 2012, 2013) and some academic theses (Løvold, 2014;
Peltomaa 2022). In the field of arts, there are some interesting
examples of exhibitions (Queering Sápmi 2019--2021) and documentary
filmmakers taking up queer sexualities among indigenous people (see
Kyrölä & Huuki, 2021). Nevertheless, [most of the burgeoning queer
Native studies have focused on the situation of the Native peoples and
settler colonialism in North America]{.underline}, although some queer
indigenous research has also been done in Australia. [The Arctic
non-Anglo settler colonialism needs to be analysed more, in dialogue
with existing Native and indigenous queer research.]{.underline}

When thinking about the notion of "queer in the Arctic", one is
immediately faced with critical questions: [How is queer intelligible as
a term in different Arctic societies and among different groups of
people living in the Arctic areas]{.underline}? [Are there other
terms]{.underline}, more pertinent, [used in the Arctic]{.underline}?
Unlike the term "queer", ["Arctic" has a rather well-defined and
well-understood referent;]{.underline} we can, for instance, use the
definition of the Arctic Human Development Report (2004, 17--18).
However, [there are multiple peoples or nations living in the
Arctic:]{.underline} the many different subgroups of Inuit in Alaska and
Greenland[;]{.underline} the different groups of Sámi in the Sápmi area
covering the northernmost parts of Norway, Sweden, Finland and Russia;
small nations in the Arctic region of Russia (from Nenets to Yupiits);
and ethnic Norwegian, Finnish, Swedish and Russian people living in the
area and belonging to the majority nationalities (Jessen Williamson,
2011, 24--27). Obviously[, the differences in cultures, histories and
positions in terms of settler colonialism, as well as languages, also
cause differences in adaptation of the ideas and identities connected to
"queer"]{.underline}. What is more, [the term "settler colonialism"
receives different interpretations in different contexts depending on
whether it is used, for example, to refer to the colonization of
non-European areas by European imperial powers, colonial
complicity]{.underline} (Keskinen et al., 2009) [in the Nordic Arctic,
or the Israeli colonization of Palestine]{.underline}. However, [the
concept]{.underline} essentially [refers to occupation and assertion of
ownership over land and resources and to the hierarchical power
relationship between settlers and indigenous peoples]{.underline} (see,
e.g., Vowel, 2016, 16--17).

When talking about queer in the Arctic, [are we referring to
identitarian definitions of queer, that is, people identifying as
LGBTIQ2]{.underline}? In the English acronym LGBTIQ, the letter T (for
trans) includes non-binary. In other languages, there may be more
specific words for non-binary, as in Finnish for example, where the word
muunsukupuolinen literally means "otherwise gendered". [The acronym has
been updated in queer indigenous/Native studies to LGBTQ2 to include
Indigenous people who understand their genders and sexualities to be
outside heteropatriarchal Eurocentric constructions]{.underline} (see,
for example, Driskill et al., 2011). [How can indigenous ways of
identifying beyond the sexual and gender binaries contribute to queer
studies?]{.underline} Or[, if we talk instead about queering the Arctic,
what does that mean]{.underline}? [How do generational and geographical
differences affect understanding what is queer in the Arctic, or what
queer does in the Arctic]{.underline}? Can we argue that [having been
othered within the Western world, the Arctic as an area, with its
peoples, has been made strange by the settler colonial
powers]{.underline} in multiple ways? [It has been defined quite
stereotypically as a "harsh and unforgiving environment]{.underline}"
(Williamson, 2011, 45) and [an empty space void of people yet
fascinating and exotic]{.underline} (Hiltunen & Oisalo, 2020, 3); it has
also been described as the "North of the North", [an area of
contestation for rights and representation, and a strange, marginal
realm from the perspective of the "centers" further south]{.underline}
(Keskitalo-Foley & Naskali, 2013, 9). One may say that, in an
interestingly parallel way, [a struggle for rights and representation
has been a key area of queer politics]{.underline} since the days of ACT
UP and Queer Nation. [Queer political demands have also been shunned as
"peripheral" because of the "minority" status of non-heterosexuality and
people identifying as non-cis-gendered.]{.underline}

However, in the third millennium, [the Arctic has become a focus of
global politics and monetary interests]{.underline}, [as climate change
and the fact of the ice melting in the Northern seas have offered new
economical possibilities for trade and commercial exploitation of the
environment]{.underline} (Keskitalo-Foley & Naskali, 2013, 9). Even
[many of the audio-visual representations imaging the North and the
Sápmi have ignored the indigenous people living there and thereby
realized the "colonial mind", pushing aside the indigenous culture and
history]{.underline} (Hiltunen & Oisalo, 2020, 3). [If we draw a
parallel again to queer cultures,]{.underline} there is a growing media
attention focused on non-heterosexual representations, and [the concept
of the "pink pound" or "pink dollar" has been developed to describe the
economic effects of the intersection of gay (male) sexuality, whiteness
and the middle- or upper-middle-class subject positions]{.underline}
(Hall, 2003; Karkulehto, 2011). As a matter of fact, [the Pride parades
and carnivals, which have spread throughout Europe---including the
Arctic--- from their original site in the US, the northernmost Pride
events in Sápmi having taken place]{.underline} in Kautokeino in 2016
and in Inari in 2017, [have been criticized by some queer communities
precisely because of their commerciality]{.underline}, [and even
normativity]{.underline} (being geared towards couples, wedding
celebrations and families with kids) (Eng et al., 2005; Hall, 2003;
Rossi, 2017). Indeed, [where there used to exist "fear of a queer
planet"]{.underline} (Warner, 1993) or pervasive homophobia among the
heterosexual majority, [what one sees today is the lure of a queer
market]{.underline} and growing interest towards queer representations
in mainstream entertainment. [Many queer-identifying activists have
wanted to protect queer subversiveness and anti-normativity from
commercialism and heteronormative familism]{.underline}, [much like the
indigenous people in the Arctic want to protect their environment and
culture against contemporary ecological colonialism]{.underline}
(Keskitalo-Foley & Naskali, 2013, 9).

I will now provide some queer readings, or readings of chosen phenomena
that can be considered queer in contemporary Arctic cultures----or
practices and processes that can be thought of queering some
communities, histories or places in the Arctic. This will be done in
discussion with indigenous and queer scholars who have analysed
non-normative or non-binary genders and sexualities in the North.

### !\-\--Climate

#### The 1AC's cartographic representations prefer its nature at a distance \-\-- visualizing the Arctic through technologies of affect distances us from the complexities of warming. 

**Dodds & Nutall 16** \[Klaus; Professor of Geopolitics @ Royal
Holloway, University of London; Mark; Professor of Anthropology @ the
University of Alberta; "The Scramble for the Poles: The Geopolitics of
the Arctic"; Polity Press; 2016\] sems

Going Beneath and Beyond: Thinking Volumetrically

[In an era of abrupt and rapid climate change, cartographic
representations of the Polar Regions showing receding and diminishing
sea ice have become increasingly familiar]{.underline}. This visual
rendering and understanding of the planet's circumpolar places remains
focused on surfaces and outer layers, [with maps and other images
depicting the gradual loss of whiteness, the appearance of deep blue
ocean space, and the northwards movement of the tree line.]{.underline}
The Arctic Climate Impact Assessment is an example of just how powerful
surface imagery and the technologies of such production can be when they
are deployed to bring the Arctic into focus as a region undergoing
dramatic change. Indeed, ACIA underscores Jody Berland's point that [the
production of satellite images **'extends a history of landscape
depiction which prefers its nature at a distance\'**]{.underline}
(Berland 2009: 246). [In a series of high resolution colour maps,
satellite images and graphics]{.underline} in both the comprehensive
scientific assessment and the shorter distillation report of ACIA's key
findings, [we are invited to gaze like Apollo]{.underline} (Cosgrove
2001). From the top of the globe, [we look (with alarm) at declining
snow cover, the melt zone of the Greenland inland ice, changes in summer
sea ice extent, northern places splashed with **oranges and deep reds**
indicating rising temperatures, changes in the boundaries of
discontinuous permafrost, northwards shifts in the ranges of plant
animal species, alterations in atmospheric and oceanic circulation, sea
level rise, and incoming, diffused and reflected solar
radiation]{.underline} (ACIA 2004, 2005). This a view from high above of
a changing Arctic [represented by lines, colours and
arrows]{.underline}. [The region is visualized through the products
of]{.underline} **[technologies of affect. It is
intoxicating.]{.underline}**

[Despite the best attempts of social scientists involved in ACIA to draw
attention to the realities and lived experience of climate change for
the people of the circumpolar North]{.underline} (e.g. Huntington and
Fox 2005; Nuttall et al. 2005), [the maps and graphics convey distance
and detachment and give us no idea about the complexity and richness of
human---environment relations, **which is all the more curious given the
seriousness of the melting and warming Arctic and the impacts on
environment and society they aim to say something about**.]{.underline}
[The projected loss of perennial sea ice in the Arctic
Ocean]{.underline}, for example, [is represented in ways that imagine
northern waters]{.underline} in a climate changed Arctic [as
increasingly blue rather than white spaces on the map, devoid of ice and
transected by lines indicating transpolar shipping routes]{.underline}.
[It is a tantalizing glimpse of]{.underline} open water and [the
possibility of shorter sailing distances between Europe, North America
and Asia]{.underline} rather than a view from the bridge of a ship
navigating through northern waterways that will still be filled with ice
regardless of the extent of summer melt. [When ACIA\'s key findings were
first released, they pointed to scenarios of possible environmental
catastrophe; now, open polar seas hint at the Arctic\'s economic
potential. This is reinforced through maps and graphics that attempt to
make the rhetoric of anticipation legible]{.underline}. As Berland
(2009: 247) puts it, **['maps constitute a visual index of traversable
space which is pragmatically linked to the anticipated exigencies of
unaccustomed, uncharted, or unfriendly movement\'.]{.underline}**

[When maps of a changing Arctic do try to point to what is below the
surface, they tend to mark]{.underline} [the location, or the
possibility for discovery, of oil, gas and other minerals.]{.underline}
[Ocean structures such as the Lomonosov Ridge become
emboldened]{.underline} as and [when they are connected to]{.underline}
possible [continental shelf claims]{.underline}. While they hint at
complexity, [they]{.underline} also [reinforce a geopolitics of the
surface and indicate what resources could be extracted, rather than an
appreciation of the stratigraphies and structures of what is
within]{.underline}, below and underneath. [They also underplay earthly
forces; the manner in which ice, snow, water pressure, atmospheric
turbulence, cloud cover, seismic activity and the like have an agency
that]{.underline} enables, [slows down, frustrates and even prevents
human activities]{.underline} and encounters. [Countless
explorers]{.underline}, [scientists, pilots and miners have
found]{.underline} to their cost [how polar environments can abuse,
confuse and refuse human interventions. Maps of the Arctic
Ocean]{.underline} and the Southern Ocean [rarely succeed in capturing
how little we really understand of these]{.underline} watery
[environments]{.underline}.

### !\-\--Turns Case

#### Cartography [destroys]{.underline} the territory is seeks to [dominate]{.underline}

Azza **Harras 25**, PhD in Postcolonial Studies, Assistant Professor in
the Department of English, Culture, and Communication at the Royal
Military College of Canada, "Varieties of imperial imaginary of the
Arctic and the Congo in *Lady Franklin of Russell Square* and *Heart of
Darkness,*" p. 257-371, Journal of Postcolonial Writing (a peer-reviewed
academic journal published by Routledge), v. 61, published 3/3/2025
\\\\\\ SDM

> [The territory no longer precedes the map, **nor does it survive
> it**]{.underline}. It is nevertheless [the map that precedes the
> territory]{.underline} -- precession of simulacra -- that engenders
> the territory. (Baudrillard Citation1995, 1)

[Baudrillard's theory]{.underline} of the precession of simulacra that
engenders the territory [highlights Said's notions of geography as a
colonial construct.]{.underline} [Cartography, one of the key concepts
in postcolonial studies, constitutes a significant part of Arctic and
African colonial narratives. Imperial powers have shown great interest
in cartography and charting maps as **manifestations of domination** and
material gain]{.underline}. The [imperial enterprise portrays maps as
parchments with vacant spaces awaiting a **glorious imperial
flag**]{.underline} to give them meaning, identity, and culture.
Canadian and British fiction frequently features maps with several
goals. According to Graham Huggan (Citation1994), featuring maps in
Canadian literature shows the close relationship between historical
development and geographical discovery and allows writers to come to
terms with and celebrate the rediscovery of their country. Maps, seen by
Huggan as a product and a process, because [the map "does not restrict
itself to the decoding of the model, but also involves itself in the
further recoding of the modelling system"]{.underline} (4), are
reflected in Elce and Conrad's texts in nearly identical ways. In one of
the letters imagined by Elce, Jane Franklin starts recollecting her
memories with her husband, mentioning their "old map of London"
(Elce Citation2018, 29) and reminiscing about how they drew the lines of
their travels, with the "routes in and out of the city almost soaked
through with ink" (29). Lady Franklin's use of the map as a compass for
her memories and the couple's sense of geographical imperative is
indicative of imperial ontology. Their map, in this case, is reflective
of what Huggan calls [the "dialectic between the formulation of the map,
during which the real world is concentrated in model form, and the
implementation of the map, during which the model is tested against
reality"]{.underline} (Citation1994, 4). Lady Franklin's and her
husband's act of tracing their travels reflects the process of testing
the map's abstract model against their lived experiences. The ink-soaked
routes symbolize how their personal memories and physical journeys are
inscribed onto the map, transforming it from a mere representation of
London into a record of their experiences, thus highlighting the
relationship between the conceptual model of the map and its real-world
application. [Similarly, the imperial venture of finding the Northwest
Passage was all about geography,]{.underline} although John Franklin's
map towards the Northwest Passage represented a model that could not be
implemented when tested against reality.

### !\-\--Takes out Solvency

#### Navigation hardships in the Arctic are the result of colonial mapping orientations and the land/water binary. Arctic \[exploration/development\] fails absent native adaptations.

Daniel **Chartier et. al \'24** \[(Daniel Chartier ; Hanna Guttorm ;
Britt Kramvig ; Berit Kristoffersen ; Johannes Riquet ; and Philip
Steinberg ) \"Decolonial cartographies: Counter-mapping in the arctic,\"
Chapter 7 of The Mediated Arctic, Manchester University Press, Accessed
through UM libraries, pp. 188-189
https://www.manchesterhive.com/display/9781526174024/9781526174024.00016.xml,
9-24-2024\]

From a [Western point of view, land stops where the sea
starts]{.underline}. [Maps create a clear visual differentiation between
land and water]{.underline}. [Bodies of water]{.underline} also
[have]{.underline} [a specific status in the eyes of the
law]{.underline}, and [seashores are particularly
significant,]{.underline} serving as precise borders surrounding states
(and mitigated by maritime law, which applies differently). In that
sense, [the Western meaning of 'territory' implies 'land']{.underline}.
[For Inuit]{.underline}, this [notion does not make sense seeing as the
territory does not stop at the shores, which are difficult to spot in
cold weather as they are extended by the ice that forms along
them]{.underline}. This is why [hunting and habitat are not based on a
clear distinction between terrestrial and maritime
behaviours]{.underline} (Collignon 1996, 100). This [connection of the
land to the frozen sea creates a world order based on surfaces rather
than on abstract directional concepts]{.underline} such as the North.
Moreover, [the intimate knowledge of the land is not an]{.underline}
[abstraction; it is the result of cumulative experience]{.underline} --
hunting, gathering, movement, and displacement -- shaping a [network of
lines connecting temporal notions]{.underline} with territorial ones,
[an 'axial perception' that 'is clearly expressed in the maps drawn by
Inuit in response to explorers' requests']{.underline} (1996, 98). These
[perceptions of space are based on a system of signs wherein language
plays a decisive role]{.underline}. Any contemporary Indigenous setting
is a bilingual one where one (Indigenous) language competes with one or
several dominant languages (Russian, English, French, Norwegian, or
Danish, for instance). So[, to create a map with Inuit knowledge of the
territory, it does not suffice to replace a language with
another]{.underline}; their [worldview must be translated, often
awkwardly, with words that necessarily displace meanings]{.underline}.
[Western temporal, legal, and philosophical systems, and the
corresponding ways of orienting oneself on the land, relating to it, and
organising it as a society]{.underline}, a[re either imposed on the
Inuit system or considered in relation to it, both of which lead to the
introduction of new concepts, translated or not, that destabilise Inuit
ways of thinking.]{.underline} Therefore, the [North becomes rooted as a
concept while nuna, which previously referred for the Inuit to the world
where people live, becomes an Inuit concept meaning North as it now
refers to Inuit territories]{.underline} (Nunavik, Nunatsiavut, Nunavut,
etc.). The [knowledge of space depends on an understanding of Inuktitut
as well as of its relationships to other languages, which in turn
transform its meaning]{.underline} (cf. Collignon 1996, 197). [To orient
oneself in the Arctic]{.underline}, [one must have a sophisticated
understanding of the environment]{.underline}. [For the Sámi and the
Inuit alike]{.underline}, [this is a matter of life or
death]{.underline} for herders, hunters, and fishermen, particularly
[when it comes to finding food and shelter]{.underline} from the cold
and the wind. [Inuit cartography thus unsurprisingly favours resources,
itineraries, previous experiences, as well as potential obstacles to
survival rather than abstract depictions]{.underline}. Inuit have been
[sharing this knowledge among themselves for millennia,]{.underline} as
McGrath writes: Inuit have a long history of map- making. For thousands
of years [they shared their knowledge of the country]{.underline} with
one another by creating [representations of areas, either visited or
known indirectly, by drawing and building outlines on sand or snow,
using rocks, bones and sticks to give a three- dimensional quality to
their images.]{.underline} (1988, 6)

### !\-\--Policy Failure

#### The aff's over-reliance on maps [dooms it]{.underline}

Azza **Harras 25**, PhD in Postcolonial Studies, Assistant Professor in
the Department of English, Culture, and Communication at the Royal
Military College of Canada, "Varieties of imperial imaginary of the
Arctic and the Congo in *Lady Franklin of Russell Square* and *Heart of
Darkness,*" p. 257-371, Journal of Postcolonial Writing (a peer-reviewed
academic journal published by Routledge), v. 61, published 3/3/2025
\\\\\\ SDM

Elce's portrayal of Lady Franklin continues to demonstrate the focal
position of maps for the imperial citizen; throughout her decade-long
search for her lost husband's expedition, the fictionalized Lady
Franklin has examined her maps so well that she feels that Captain
Hamilton "had overstepped personal decorum when his finger traced the
same line \[that hers\] had so many times" (Elce Citation2018, 34). Lady
Franklin's reliance on maps during her search for her husband's
expedition further underscores the political authority of maps as
representations of power. Nonetheless, [the over-reliance on
maps]{.underline}, as Baudrillard suggests, [leads to "substituting the
signs of the real for the real]{.underline}" (Citation1995, 2), meaning
that [the map becomes more important than the reality it represents. In
the context of colonial critique]{.underline}, [this]{.underline} does
not just involve creating a fictional version of reality, but it [also
represents the danger of explorers becoming overly dependent on these
abstract representations]{.underline}. This [over-reliance can lead to
loss or failure when explorers are misled or overwhelmed by the
**authority of the map**]{.underline} itself, [rather than engaging with
the actual terrain]{.underline}, thus [illustrating how the map's
symbolic power can lead to **real-world defeat**.]{.underline}

In addition to the use of maps as signs of the real, maps are used in
both texts as analytical tools in the journey to find Kutz or in
searching for the lost expedition. Nonetheless, [the tools, just another
dialectic themselves, become the cause of being lost]{.underline},
metaphorically and literally. Sir John Franklin's inability to overpower
the map by reaching the Northwest Passage is similar to Kurtz's failure
to "penetrate" the heart of Africa as he succumbs to the forest's
darkness. Similarly, with the passage of time and the numerous campaigns
launched to find Sir John Franklin and the Northwest Passage, Lady
Franklin's maps stop operating as simulacra, giving way to realistic
referentiality, which unravels the truth behind imperial explorations
and the impossibility of locating her husband beyond the signs of the
map.

#### Maps blur our perception of reality

Azza **Harras 25**, PhD in Postcolonial Studies, Assistant Professor in
the Department of English, Culture, and Communication at the Royal
Military College of Canada, "Varieties of imperial imaginary of the
Arctic and the Congo in *Lady Franklin of Russell Square* and *Heart of
Darkness,*" p. 257-371, Journal of Postcolonial Writing (a peer-reviewed
academic journal published by Routledge), v. 61, published 3/3/2025
\\\\\\ SDM

> [when there is no longer any virgin territory]{.underline}, and thus
> one available to the imaginary, *[when the map covers the whole
> territory]{.underline}, something like [the principle of **reality
> disappear**]{.underline}***[s]{.underline}**. In this way, [the
> conquest of space constitutes an irreversible crossing toward the loss
> of the terrestrial referential]{.underline}.
> (Baudrillard Citation1995, 120; emphasis in original)

[Baudrillard's concept of the "loss of the terrestrial referential"
speaks to the diminishing connection between symbols (maps,
representations, or models) and the real-world spaces they are meant to
represent.]{.underline} This idea is [rooted in Baudrillard's broader
theory of simulation and hyperreality, where the distinction between the
real and the simulated becomes **increasingly blurred** until
the]{.underline} simulated (the [map]{.underline} or model) effectively
[**becomes the real** in our perceptions and interactions]{.underline}.
The confusion paradoxically leads to Kurtz succumbing to his nihilism,
enslaving the Congolese and establishing his own imaginary kingdom.
However, it is also the loss of terrestrial referential that leads
Marlow to a realization of the ugly face of imperialism. Hence, maps in
Elce's and Conrad's texts can be seen as models of reality in which the
imperial imaginations of the two protagonists are countered. In both
cases, [maps **preceded reality** in a Baudrillardian sense before
disillusionment started.]{.underline}

### !\-\--Root Cause

#### Modern conceptions of arctic threats come from mapping

Kathryn C. **Lavelle 25**, PhD, the Ellen and Dixon Long Professor of
Political Science at Case Western Reserve University and the author of
*The Challenges of Multilateralism*. She has been a congressional fellow
and a residential fellow at the Woodrow Wilson International Center for
Scholars in Washington, D.C; "Reluctant Conquest; American Wealth,
Power, and Science in the Arctic," p. 66, New Haven, Yale University
Press, 2025, accessed through the Umich library \\\\\\ SDM

On the domestic American political side, [the experience of two world
wars transformed engagement in the Arctic because the nature of the
conflicts forced Americans to **rethink their terri-torial position** on
the globe and how the Arctic connected some spaces and divided
others]{.underline}. Whereas the initial security consider-ations for
Arctic affairs in the U.S. had centered on conflict with Japan and in
the Pacific, the widespread use of air transportation and [**newer
mapping images** during World War II reformulated the American
consciousness from one in which the country was largely protected by
oceans into one in which both Europe and Asia were **much closer** on
the globe.]{.underline} Thus, [the U.S. appeared to be **far more
vulnerable**]{.underline} than before.4 U.S. [productive capabilities
were instru-mental in securing allied victories as new transportation
networks emerged across the Arctic,]{.underline} and [new technology
allowed for more precise research and development into weather patterns
and per-mafrost engineering]{.underline} (or engineering on layers of
soil that remain frozen throughout the year). [The result is that the
reinforcing rela-tionships among science, economics, and national
security con-verged more sharply]{.underline} throughout the wartime
era.

### AT: Maps/Reps Don't Shape Reality

#### Maps of the arctic are not neutral. Rather, they are used to shape debates about the arctic and our very conceptions of it.

**Lindberg 19'** Helena Gonzales Lindberg (Ph.D) has an
interdisciplinary educational and research background within geography,
political science and environmental science. *The constitutive power of
maps in the Arctic*, pg. 32 -- 34, Lund University, 2019,
<https://lucris.lub.lu.se/ws/portalfiles/portal/70153744/Lindberg_The_constitutive_power_of_maps_in_the_Arctic_2019.pdf>
{gs}

[A region like the Arctic]{.underline}, argues Geir Hønneland (2017, p.
31), [is not a predetermined territory, but made in text and speech as
it is always possible to find supporting arguments for a geographical
area to constitute a 'natural' unit]{.underline}. [Thus, maps can be
viewed as part of the **discursive construction** of the Arctic as a
region,]{.underline} which Albert and Vasilache (2018) argue is based on
a mixture of actors, knowledges and representations that (re)define and
(re)constructs the region into what it is understood as. Similarly,
Karen Culcasi (2010) considers maps to be central in constructing 'the
Middle East' as a particular 'world-region'. Therefore[, I find maps to
be central knowledges and representations that define and construct the
Arctic 'world-region'. Where it is, what it is, what should happen
next:]{.underline} [these questions are full of power-knowledge
problematisations which are expressed and reproduced in
maps.]{.underline} However, to gain access to the underlying assumptions
about the Arctic and to understand the kind of place the Arctic has
become (and is becoming) requires us to examine how the [maps themselves
problematise Arctic issues and developments]{.underline}. [Contrary to
authors who emphasise the ways in which geography influences Arctic
politics, for example by exploring how melting sea ice impacts on the
prospects of the Arctic coastal states]{.underline} (cf. Wegge and Keil
2018), [I argue that such **changes and problematisations are not given,
but often constructed and mediated in maps**. A key contention of this
thesis is indeed that maps influence politics by projecting certain
assumptions about the geographical areas and issues in
question]{.underline}.

[To illustrate how particular problematisations about the Arctic are
being (re)produced and sustained via the use of maps, I point to two
articles]{.underline} by Scott Borgerson (2008, 2013) in the journal
Foreign Affairs. In "Arctic Meltdown", [Borgerson]{.underline} ([2008)
displays two maps showing the extent of Arctic sea ice. One map depicts
ice coverage in September 2001 and the other map depicts the decrease of
ice coverage as of September 2007.]{.underline} [After establishing the
sea ice changes, there is a map showing 'Arctic Energy Resources', and
later, also two maps illustrating 'Arctic Shipping
Shortcuts']{.underline}. However, none of these three maps show signs of
sea ice in the Arctic Ocean. Moreover, [in the largely realist
Borgerson's article from 2013]{.underline}, "The Coming Arctic Boom",
[the first sentence identifies one of the issues: "The ice was never
supposed to melt this quickly"]{.underline} (2013, p. 76). [Later, there
is a map showing 'Economic Opportunity in the Arctic'. This map includes
shaded areas marking the probabilities of oil, a line marking the extent
of Arctic sea ice extent in September 2012, and an arrow running across
the ocean, named the Northern Sea Route]{.underline} 33 (Borgerson 2013,
p. 83). [He concludes that it is only a matter of time before the summer
sea ice is gone and the Arctic opens up to extensive development, "with
enormous benefits for both outsiders and the inhabitants of this prime
real estate]{.underline}" (Borgerson 2013, p. 89). **[This
conclusion]{.underline}**, I argue, [**is being substantiated through
the use of maps**, for example, by not showing any sea ice on the map as
a way to reveal economic opportunities.]{.underline} [Moreover, the maps
displayed in both the articles are familiar to people engaged in Arctic
issues and emerge from renowned mapping agencies,]{.underline} such as
the National Snow and Ice Data Center (NSIDC) and the United States
Geological Survey (USGS) -- whose maps that I analyse in Chapters 5 and
6. [Due to their circulation and authority, these maps often go
unquestioned in Arctic debates, reproducing dominant ideas about there
being 'challenges']{.underline} [(ice melting), but also 'opportunities'
(undiscovered petroleum) in the area.]{.underline} Hence, I find it
important to understand how maps become part of such discourses
surrounding 'challenges' and 'opportunities' in the Arctic, but more
importantly how they give way to particular visions and certain actors,
while silencing others luence politics by projecting certain assumptions
about the geographical areas and issues in question.

In this context, [it is interesting to observe that
Borgerson]{.underline} (2013) [sketches out a kind of 'win-win' scenario
for the future of the Arctic and uses many maps to illustrate this
point]{.underline}. [He uses the maps as a convincing, seemingly neutral
background against which his key arguments are articulated]{.underline}.
However, I will argue that instead of being neutral background
information figuring in a journal article, a scientific study, or a book
about the Arctic, these maps in themselves can be the subject of a study
about the Arctic. [Such **maps do not simply mirror reality, but form
our conception of what that reality entails**]{.underline}. [Equally
important is the ability of **maps to shape debates and visions about
the future of the Arctic**]{.underline}. In what follows below I will
explain the contributions made in the thesis.

#### Arctic representations shape arctic realities. 

Gunhild Hoogensen **Gjørv** **et al** **22**, MA, PhD, Professor in
Security Studies and Geopolitics and Arctic 6 Chair in Security Studies
