I've said I want to speak a word of black life; and no one has cleared
more theoretical ground for such a word, and so, for the skewed study of
black life as a genuine largeness, than Fred Moten. While certainly no
stranger to black death or antiblackness, Moten's black study is a party
more often than it isn't. He makes the bias of his black study plain
when he outlines his desire in Black and Blur to "subordinate by a
measure so small that it constitutes measure's eclipse, the critical
analysis of anti-blackness to the celebratory analysis of
blackness."Footnote49 Jared Sexton once heard Moten state "measure's
eclipse" numerically, detailing in "The Social Life of Social Death" how
[Moten]{.underline} once "[remarked to the effect]{.underline} that [he
always wants his writing to be comprised of 49.99% critique and 50.01%
celebration]{.underline}."Footnote50 [This delineation of black study
into the "celebration of blackness" and the "critique of anti-blackness"
yields another compelling example of black ambivalence]{.underline}. And
just as we observed of the cooperation between the testaments of black
life and death in Sharpe's black study[, the "critique of
anti-blackness" and "celebration of blackness" are]{.underline}, for
Moten, [not oppositional, but complementary operations that combine to
**produce black study**]{.underline}. Thus, he explains that the "rich,
rigorous, powerful, and utterly necessary analytic of anti-blackness"
actually enables the celebration of blackness; and that this
celebration, in turn, is "done not to avoid or ameliorate the hard
truths of anti-blackness but in the service of its violent
eradication."Footnote51

This delineation of black study offers a framework that bonds the
witness of our forked-tongued tradition together. But beyond serving as
just another example of black ambivalence, the subordination of critique
to celebration in Moten's black study also raises important questions
about the nature of the relationship between blackness and
anti-blackness. That there could be even marginally more blackness to
celebrate than antiblackness to critique pulls blackness out from under
anti-'s totalizing shadow. It leads us to question whether
"anti-blackness" is the first, only, and exhaustive word of black study,
whether the only blackness to speak of is that which gathers under
anti-'s orthographic shadow. When we take part in "the devotional
practice that is given in recitation of the sentence 'blackness is x,'"
have we been misspelling blackness?Footnote52 Leaving out a silent
"anti-"? Is there any word we can speak for blackness that does not
always already presume this prefix, which is nothing but the grammatical
sign of blackness' existence within what Frank Wilderson has theorized
as a broader "grammar of suffering"?Footnote53 [Does blackness have any
life outside of the anti-black "world]{.underline} that was going to
kill" Equiano?

Of course, in .02%, we are talking about a black lining thinner than
silver's. But razor thin as it is, its sheer excess is enough to put
significant pressure on the understanding of antiblackness as that which
"precedes and exceeds" blackness. It introduces the possibility that
this apparent relation can, by another frame, appear inverted, such that
[blackness rather "**precedes and exceeds**" anti-blackness, and is
**not diminished** by anti-blackness' capacity to occlude. By
establishing blackness]{.underline} (and by extension black life) [as
excessive, the .02% margin by which Moten subordinates the "critique of
anti-blackness" to the "celebration of blackness" models and
invites]{.underline} such [ambivalence as is **ultimately skewed** in
the direction of black life]{.underline}.Footnote54 But what grounds are
there to justify this sense of "the largeness that is black life?" And
are they quite so substantial as that which the antiblack world
furnishes to the thought of black death?

Perhaps [we can pursue the grounds of .02%]{.underline} in the nearly
nothing interval of "soon" that elapses between Equiano's initial
"astonishment" at the sight of an unknown ocean and his "terror" upon
boarding the slave ship.

> The first object which saluted my eyes when I arrived on the coast was
> the sea, and a slave ship, which was then riding at anchor, and
> waiting for its cargo. These filled me with astonishment, which was
> soon converted into terror when I was carried on board. I was
> immediately handled and tossed up to see if I were sound by some of
> the crew; and I was now persuaded that I had gotten into a world of
> bad spirits, and that they were going to kill me.Footnote55

Notwithstanding the terror of the antiblack world that was going to kill
him, I am struck that the very "first thing that saluted" Equiano, even
prior to the slave ship, was the sea. And that when he saw it, he was
astonished. That this astonishment was "soon converted to terror," in a
conversion which mirrors Equiano's own conversion into cargo---and thus,
the Black subject of the nonhuman hold---suggests an interval so short
as to be almost negligible. Especially when considered beside the sheer
scale of the antiblack world, and all there is proportionally to say
about something on the scale of a world when held up against a moment,
here and already gone. The agenda for black study that Equiano's record
of the beginning would seem to set is the study, critique, and
overturning of this world. And I am certainly about that life. But here
is where I stumble, felled perhaps too long in the passing interval of
"soon." It's Equiano's "astonishment" that gets me, again and again and
always. That there was, that there could be, astonishment. Then, in the
time of astonishment, for a moment, but only for a moment, the Earth
gathers, dancing.

Equiano did not jump to see the Earth before the end of his world and
the beginning of ours. But he did see it. And he was astonished. Just
prior to being "saluted," or hailed in the Althusserian sense, by the
slave ship---"Hey you there! Hey, cargo!"---to discovering that he
really was the cargo for which the ship was waiting, and in this way
being interpellated as black, Equiano was greeted by the blue face of an
overwhelmingly blue planet. What if, compelled by such astonishment, we
fall deep into the fleeting interval of soon? What would it mean to
think the dawning of blackness in Middle Passage as not reducible to the
"social death" hailed in the salute of the slave ship? What if, instead,
we think blackness as not just responding also, but first, to the
hailing waves of the astonishing sea? "Hey you there! Hey, earthling."
What if, as our original antiphony, blackness dawns in the ongoing
response of black ecological life to the hail of a blue planet?

[The study of black life as a genuine largeness begins when
we]{.underline}, with Equiano and an innumerable cloud of witnesses,
[stop to **behold the Earth before the end of the antiblack
world**]{.underline}. [This Earth still hails and is hardly as much
world as it appears on its surface]{.underline}, which is so much more
ocean than not. Or else do we suppose that whiteness has managed to
straightjacket the planet so utterly that its definition is no longer
possibility, as the narrator of Invisible Man once averred?Footnote56

If, as Ed Roberson explains, the "nature poem occurs when an
individual's sense of the larger Earth enters into the world of human
knowledge," and if the "main understanding that results from this
encounter is...that the world's desires do not run the Earth, but the
Earth does run the world,"Footnote57 then what would it mean for a
larger sense of the Earth to enter black studies? Or, to invoke a
beautiful line of Roberson's poetry that I can't stop thinking about,
how might we endeavor, "to see the Earth before the end of the
\[antiblack\] world?"Footnote58 And what would happen to our
understandings of black life as that which is insisted from the
totalizing grounds of black death, if we shift our critical frame from
the world to the Earth, as our era of climate crisis seems to demand?
Indeed, [if by "social death," black studies]{.underline} has [arrived
at its most complete understanding of what it means to be black in and
relative to the world, it is yet left to the field to attend ever more
robustly to what it means to be **black on and in relationship to the
Earth, that exceeds the world**. The necessity of this vector of black
study is all the more apparent in the light of]{.underline} a [**global
ecological crisis** that has illuminated the]{.underline} (Human)
[social **life of the world as a still deeper death**]{.underline}. So,
[if what we want is not only apocalypse, but an apocalypse we might live
in, then **even more than social death, we must learn to study and
celebrate what**]{.underline}, by another frame, **[can be perceived as
the largeness that is black ecological life. Because after the end of
the world, there will be black people on Earth, or none at
all]{.underline}**.

### Fiat Good---Freedom Dreaming---2AC

#### Fiat is [freedom dreaming]{.underline}. The optimistic imagination of role-played scenarios of policy change is [more empowering]{.underline} than negativistic [critique]{.underline}.

**Fernández, et al, 24**---Ethnic Studies Department, College of Arts &
Sciences, Santa Clara University (Jesica Siham, with Rashida Govan, Ben
Kirshner, Tafadzwa Tivaringe, and Roderick Watts, "Youth Community
Organizing Groups Fostering Sociopolitical Wellbeing: Three
Healing-Oriented Values to Support Activism," Youth 2024, 4(3),
1004-1025, dml)

[Freedom is anchored in the cultivation of **hope** and people
exercising their freedom to challenge oppression]{.underline}. [To
**envision possibilities** for freedom is to acknowledge and affirm that
change is **possible** and that both hope and action must go **hand in
hand**]{.underline} to make just change a reality. [**Imagining** and
therefore **working** to create a future where all people can heal and
thrive **beyond mere survival**]{.underline} characterizes the third
healing-oriented value, which [is **freedom dreaming**]{.underline}.
Robin D. G. Kelley \[65\] describes "freedom dreaming" as [**imagining
alternatives** to oppressive conditions]{.underline} of inequity,
violence, and trauma. Freedom dreaming [can help youth harness their
skills]{.underline}, [capacities]{.underline}, [and tools to move toward
**actualizing** change]{.underline} \[66\]. [To support political
action]{.underline}, [young organizers must practice **envisioning** the
future and **reimaging** the present]{.underline}. For example, asking
young organizers to reconsider what a school environment without police
and policing could be; instead of having police or security officers in
schools, they have access to counselors, mentors, and healing-centered
practitioners. Asking youth to question the way things are is in
alignment with freedom dreaming.

[Political action]{.underline} within YCO settings [was **most
sustained** when freedom dreaming was a part of their organizing
strategies]{.underline}. For example, young organizers described their
motivations for joining a campaign, as the following excerpts from EE
and PJU illustrate:

Well, my participation with EE is helping me pursue my dreams. It makes
me pursue my dreams in a way that EE is fighting for equal education.
... For example, if I don't have enough money to go to university, they
say: "You can talk to us. What can we do for you to go to university?"

---EE, Interview

At a PJU "accountability meeting" with the school district, youth
participated by introducing themselves using the same general phrase:
"My name is \[name\], I'm in \[grade in school\], and I'm pre-college.
Not pre-prison!"

---PJU, Fieldnote

[The process of transformation often **begins** with]{.underline} asking
for help or seeking resources to pursue one's dream, as well as changing
or challenging the dominant, often oppressive scripts. This is
consistent with Gutiérrez's \[67\] notion of "social dreaming", or
[**engineering social design experiments** with
alternatives]{.underline} \[68\]. In other words, [it relates to holding
dreams for a **collective** or on behalf of a broader set of social
others]{.underline}, a community, or a group of people. These personal
dreams are fundamental to fostering collective dreams. Within YCO, young
organizers are encouraged to dream as individuals, and then supported in
seeing these individual dreams as threaded together toward the
collective dreams, which is in line with Kelley's \[65\] process of
imagining alternatives. Freedom dreaming therefore involves questioning
and addressing the inequities within people's environments, their own
lives and relationships, or the settings and contexts wherein
communities are situated. [**Collectively visualizing** a campaign
victory]{.underline}, [**strategizing** how to make that
possible]{.underline}, [and **identifying** who the power players
are]{.underline} what stories, testimonies, and actions [will be **most
compelling** to leverage change]{.underline} are all elements of freedom
dreaming in practice.

As a healing-oriented value, freedom dreaming is oriented toward
transformation instead of repair or reform. In this regard, communities
impacted by inequities are involved in re-imagining and enacting change
as they wish to see it in their lives. For example, reconsidering the
culture of schooling to be more affirming of students' experiential
knowledge and seeing students and their communities flourishing are
examples of freedom dreaming that many young organizers voiced. The
following interview quotes by two young organizers with Coleman
Advocates demonstrate this point:

We've been trying to speak and get people angry about stuff. I feel like
anger is the fire to start it all up to make them passionate about
something. Because if they're not passionate about something, then it'll
just---like, the flame will just burn out, right? But if you're really,
if you're heated and you're like, "Wow, that's an injustice! This is
happening. Wow! I can't believe that. I want to make this change,
because I don't want to see my children go through this. I don't want to
see the next generation go through this. I'm tired of going through
this, myself." I think that's where it all starts, so yeah.

---Coleman Advocates, Interview

I guess that little spark could just explode into being something
awesome. You know, like a little bud blooming into becoming a flower.
Probably doing rallies at schools and---just spreading the word
throughout the city and getting youth organized would probably increase
the numbers of people coming to \[Coleman Advocates\], yeah. And
probably not just having YMAC \[Youth Making a Change, Coleman
Advocates\] based here, like, after school---probably have more offices
around the city or the school even, you know? Have them have their own
classroom at every school in the San Francisco Unified School District.

---Coleman Advocates, Interview

Young organizers' [dreams are **not naïve** or **utopic**]{.underline}
by any means. Instead, [their dreams are anchored in their
**experiences**]{.underline} as young organizers who firmly believe they
deserve better; they deserve equity, freedom, dignity, and liberation.
[These dreams are **possible** to actualize if people in positions of
power were activated to **care for** or **recognize** the root causes of
the inequities]{.underline}, which YCO supports [through **sustained
political action**]{.underline}.

Freedom dreaming, consistent with spiritual activism, is about building
a critical consciousness to take the necessary actions toward change.
Reflecting on the words of poet Jayne Cortez, "somewhere in advance of
nowhere", Kelly purports, "[Without **new visions**]{.underline}, [we
don't know what to **build**]{.underline}, [only what to **knock
down**]{.underline}. [We not only end up **confused**]{.underline},
**[rudderless]{.underline}**, [and **cynical**]{.underline}, [but we
forget that making a revolution is not a series of **clever maneuvers**
and **tactics** but a **process** that can and must transform
us]{.underline}" \[65\] (preface xii). [Political action]{.underline},
[when approached through the **healing-oriented value** of freedom
dreaming]{.underline}, [can help young organizers develop a **deep
understanding** of the mechanisms of power and oppression as well as
their sociopolitical wellbeing to **sustain themselves**
emotionally]{.underline}, [mentally]{.underline}, [and even physically
in their organizing]{.underline}, as one young organizer notes in their
interview:

Well, I think I've never been a very tolerant person. Every time someone
comes to me with a problem, I'm able to sit down and listen, even though
I'll cry, (I think that some of them think I'm a very emotional person).
Now I'm able to listen to people and give advice that they use, because
some of them would report back that, "You know what, I've used---I've
taken your advice on what you said the other day, and the result of that
came out to be like this." \[...\] Yeah, I can now analyze stuff and
listen because I've been equipped---I'm more tolerant. I think, again,
the scenario at our school of not having teachers, I was then, at the
time, forced, because we didn't want to be radical about this whole
issue of forcing the government to bring back our teachers. Then I was
forced to think of the things that I want to see happen to me and my
peers if we don't have teachers and then we are happy about that. At
first, I didn't take this seriously, that we don't have teachers. It was
fine at first. Then, I realized that I came to school to study. Now, if
there are no teachers at school, then why am I coming to school? That
was, in some way, negative, because it was going to go on and go on and
go on, and then maybe I would end up saying, "I will not go to school."

---EE, Interview

[Understanding oppression]{.underline}, much like dreaming alone, [is
**not enough** to bring about change]{.underline}, [which is **needed**
for healing]{.underline}. Both a [critical consciousness]{.underline} of
root causes [must be facilitated **alongside** the avenues to **engage
actions** that can allow for dreams to **take shape** in the real
world]{.underline}.

YCO created a range of [activities]{.underline} that [contributed to
young organizers' freedom to dream by]{.underline}, for example,
[**role-playing different scenarios** of how they would express their
needs and demands in front of leaders]{.underline}. Through reflections,
discussions, trainings, and workshops, as well as skits, reading
circles, and artistic expression, [youth **cultivated their
envisioning** of what **political actions** they would engage and what
they desired to see **actualized**]{.underline}. The following
demonstrates these moments among young organizers at EE:

Well, the only message that I would like to get across, is the fact that
[when one **puts oneself in the shoes** of the other and **feels the
pain** that others are going through and is able to help]{.underline}, I
think it is the uniqueness of one's journey [that gives one the
potential to contribute so much]{.underline}. ... Because at the end of
the day South Africa will not perish. It will have leaders. It will have
people who are willing to do this \[organizing\]. It will have lawyers.
It will have people who have confidence in themselves because of these
youth programs. You find that 80 percent---okay, you find that maybe, 60
percent of the learners in schools do not want to hold themselves in
such programs. I don't know why they feel like that, but they just don't
want them. You find that those who involve themselves in such \[YCO\]
programs, find it easier to think outside of the box. They have
confidence and they are go-getters. They're willing to fight for what
they want. ... Equal Education is grooming us to be leaders. It's
grooming us to be people who have knowledge. If you lack knowledge, you
perish.

---EE, Interview

Young organizers learned about topics relevant to their everyday lives
for the purpose of asking questions about them, crafting solutions, and
taking action to see those solutions realized. Such opportunities were
often facilitated through activities to help young organizers envision
what is possible when change happens. [Activities that fostered their
imagination were **valuable moments** for learning in **community** with
others]{.underline}. At times, [the activities]{.underline} included
youth engaging difficult emotions, such as anger, nervousness, and
vulnerability, as well as courage, joy, and hope.

Young organizers saw the relevance of their activities to their lives
and those of their peers, and this helped frame their campaigning
strategies. The relevance of the issues in youth lives [motivated them
to **continue** their actions and organizing]{.underline}. [It also
helped them foster **radical hope** and persistence to **remain
engaged** in political action in a way that was **sustainable** to their
wellbeing]{.underline}, as the dialogue among young organizers at
Coleman Advocates demonstrates:

Each youth was seated around the couches, and each one responded by
stating their name and their response to the prompt. Among some of the
responses were: organizing for a "better future," "for change," "to be a
leader," to "show \[others\] we can be somebody," "struggle for change,"
turning "anger into action." One young organizer talked about organizing
as an outlet, that she was tired of getting angry and started to
organize as a way to get that anger out and channeling it into to action
to make something happen. Additional responses included organizing to
address: "oppression," "change inequalities," "taking action instead of
waiting around to see things happen," "family" \[...\] "wanting to make
a change," and "the government isn't gonna care \[for us/people\]." Once
some of these responses were gathered, Tina transitioned into the
icebreaker and mentioned that some of the reasons for why we organized
are tied to who we are and the lives we live "struggles we go through."
And another young organizer added "the next best thing to freedom is a
struggle for justice."

---Coleman Advocates, Fieldnote

As this fieldnote demonstrates, young organizers' agency and voice were
fostered through activities that called them to reflect on why they
organize as well as what their hopes or dreams are. Furthermore, the
range of learning and leadership roles young organizers often took on
within YCO settings supported their development as spiritual activists
and freedom dreamers. YCO settings provided supportive and collaborative
environments where healing-oriented values of collectivized care,
spiritual activism, and freedom dreaming were possible.

[Freedom dreaming as a healing-oriented value that young organizers
develop]{.underline} within YCO settings [has **long been put into
practice** by communities of color or people who are impacted by
systemic inequities]{.underline}. [**Abolitionists** who lead
emancipatory struggles and **civil rights** efforts]{.underline}, for
example, [engaged in freedom dreaming]{.underline} as part of their
movement \[69,70\]. [Youth and women of color on the
frontlines]{.underline} of these efforts [were]{.underline} perhaps
[fueled by an **unwavering radical hope** for liberation for themselves
and future generations]{.underline}. [Forward thinking]{.underline},
[anchored in a recognition of the present struggle with a critical
reflexive understanding of the past]{.underline}, [enables]{.underline}
people to bridge healing with political action. In this way, it
describes what Miller and Powers' \[71\] note as [the **restoring of
wellbeing**]{.underline}, [whereby the activation of a just future
fosters a **meaning** or **purpose** in life in relationship with others
who share that dream]{.underline}. The anticipation of a just, free, and
liberatory life suggests that [healing happens when hope in action is
activated and supported]{.underline}.

[Recent scholarship purports that developing a **radical vision** of
possibilities is **necessary** for restoring individual and community
wellbeing]{.underline} \[72\]. Relatedly, [having a **practical vision**
of possibilities can help]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} it
[can]{.underline} even [**increase collective action** among communities
of color]{.underline}. To support the political action of youth,
Ginwright \[25,27,73\] asserts that settings must align their values or
practices with healing. Furthermore, issues of power, history, identity,
culture, and collective memory must be integrated into settings where
learning, health, and wellness can and must be fostered. [Freedom
dreaming involves]{.underline} looking back and forward while [attending
to the urgencies of **redressing** systems of power and oppression that
produce inequities in the present and infusing political action with
radical hope]{.underline}. As Kelley \[65\] describes, [freedom dreaming
is]{.underline} "[**making visible** a new society]{.underline}, a
peaceful, cooperative, loving world without poverty and oppression" (p.
196), one that is unbounded and limitless to imagination.

6\. Conclusions

YCO supports political action among young organizers by providing them
with opportunities to develop, experience, engage, and practice values
associated with wellbeing. We describe these as healing-oriented values
of collective care, spiritual activism, and [freedom
dreaming]{.underline}. We demonstrate how these values provided young
organizers with meaningful moments for reflection on their concerns and
needs, alongside opportunities [for political action]{.underline}. The
facilitated opportunities for shared reflection, dialogue, and action
within YCO [can help cultivate]{.underline} young organizers' leadership
and [capacities for **sustained sociopolitical wellbeing**]{.underline}.
The three healing-oriented values we illustrated via our data show how
YCO practices can support youth sociopolitical wellbeing in relation to
[political action for liberation]{.underline}. We believe that the
pursuit of liberation is, or [can be]{.underline}, in and of itself, [a
pathway toward **healing**]{.underline}. Justice-centered work is
founded upon building a better world for ourselves, others, and those to
come, as well as the environment---a point that Kendall's vignette made
clear and several other organizers also expressed. In effect, Kendall
underscores the importance of belonging, specifically of [building
**solidarity** and connecting with others for the **greater
good**]{.underline}. In turn, this experience, or interconnected
practices of collective care, spiritual activism, and freedom dreaming,
[held implications for]{.underline} their [developing sociopolitical
wellbeing and]{.underline} ongoing process of [healing]{.underline}.

Personal and collective wellbeing for youth can be fostered through
varied activities and sources of support. Participation in YCO, however,
is not to be understood as a substitute for forms of mental health
support, such as therapy or medication, which may be right for a youth
in need of resources. What the examples from our study show is how
[organizing can be a **support** for]{.underline} young organizers'
[wellbeing]{.underline}, specifically [by exercising **agency** to
address institutional and systemic harms]{.underline}. In providing
opportunities for experiencing and enacting collectivized care,
spiritual activism, and [freedom dreaming]{.underline}, YCO [settings
became important spaces to build **learning communities** for the
development of sociopolitical wellbeing]{.underline},
[which]{.underline} is essential to sustain action for just change. YCO
[offered **scaffolding** to less experienced]{.underline} young
[organizers]{.underline}, [which enabled them to
participate]{.underline}, even if they were initially nervous or shy.
Because of the culture of care, the acknowledgement of the work as
rooted in something greater than the individual---freedom dreaming---and
the deep desire for change or an otherwise more just world, which is in
resonance with spiritual activism, young organizers' sociopolitical
wellbeing was fostered. When young organizers take the mic, as speakers
at a rally or stakeholder meeting, for example, they often use a script
or notecards, and over time they develop their confidence in public
speaking and leverage their experiences, emotions, and support system to
sustain their political action alongside their wellbeing. [These links
are **key** to nourishing]{.underline} a relational process of healing
and the transformation of oppressive conditions of inequity and violence
\[2\].

YCO settings that practice healing-oriented values can help youth
overcome the challenges that come with activism and movement-building.
When provided with support, youth can lead [**organizing efforts** and
**campaigns** that]{.underline} further [reinforce **collectivized
care**]{.underline}, **[spiritual activism]{.underline}**, [and
**freedom dreaming**]{.underline}. Participating YCO groups demonstrated
relational practices that affirmed the dignity and humanity of oppressed
youth and their communities. Trust, intergenerational relationships, and
mutual support contributed to sustained youth participation over time.
[These qualities provided a safe]{.underline}, [intellectual space
for]{.underline} dialogue on ideas, **[debate]{.underline}**, and
reflection. One common practice was to try to apply a more egalitarian
or democratic set of norms related to power differences based on age. As
young organizers, they tended to be intentional about the relevance of
age as a social category and how age should be considered for
organizational decision-making. YCO used terms such as
intergenerational, youth-adult partnership-oriented, or youth-centered,
each with slightly different meanings, to characterize their approach.
Young organizers' experience of voice and power often contrasted with
their experiences in school, a point that Kendall expressed in the
opening vignette. These examples, along with the healing-oriented values
we observed, were anchored in a YCO culture of belonging, forming
friendships and family-like relationships, self and collective love, and
an affirmation that what they experienced was shared among other members
of their organizing group or movement. Most importantly, within YCO
settings, freedom dreams of radical hopes for change, sustained by
collectivized care, and spiritual activism were intertwined.

Participating in YCO offered learning environments [that enabled young
organizers to **translate** reflection]{.underline},
[research]{.underline}, [and dialogue into **political
action**]{.underline}. Young [organizers]{.underline} like Kendall,
among others whose voices we featured engaged in YCO, [developed a
**critical understanding** and **analysis** of their social context and
**core skills** for leadership]{.underline}, [community
engagement]{.underline}, [and democratic participation]{.underline}.
[These findings are **consistent with prior research**]{.underline} on
the impact of community organizing and civic engagement experiences
\[36,74,75,76\]. Additionally, there was diversity in strategies for
engaging and mobilizing youth at varied sites, particularly across
nations. [The **international scope** of our research study]{.underline}
was a strength, and it [attests to the value of this work]{.underline}.
This diversity is important because it can contribute to new strategies
for YCO to use. To further explain, certain educational practices in EE
that helped young organizers like Kendall learn about their connection
to historical struggles and building a national movement may be of
interest to youth and YCO outside of South Africa. Similarly, [the
ability]{.underline} of YCOs such as PJU and Coleman Advocates [to
achieve **new**]{.underline} state and city
**[legislation]{.underline}** dismantling the school-to-prison pipeline
[is **important** for groups that wish to make **institutional impact**
and **systemic policy change**]{.underline} \[77\].

[Our study's findings underscore the importance of]{.underline}
healing-oriented values in YCO [settings that]{.underline} help foster
opportunities to [facilitate youth **political action**]{.underline},
**[activism]{.underline}**, [and **voice**]{.underline}, alongside
sociopolitical wellbeing. [Participation]{.underline} in movements for
justice, rights, and dignity, while certainly presenting challenges to
young organizers, like burnout, [can]{.underline} also
[offer]{.underline} meaningful [opportunities]{.underline} for them [to
develop the capacities]{.underline}, [skills]{.underline},
interpersonal/introspective [insights]{.underline}, [and relational
networks to **prevent burnout**]{.underline}---[or at the very
least]{.underline}, [**recognize** it and seek out **support** before it
compromises one's health]{.underline}. We have offered this paper as an
invitation to ongoing dialogue and theoretical and empirical research on
the intersections of youth sociopolitical development, YCO, and
wellbeing. The three interconnected healing-centered values we offered,
which young organizers put into practice, show how young organizers
engage these to support political action. Moreover, these values also
help cultivate young organizers' socioemotional awareness, which is
needed for long-term engagement in justice-centered work.

### Fiat Good---Speculation---2AC

#### Fiat is a [precondition]{.underline}\-\--imagining alternative futures is a necessary precursor to enacting them and generating survival strategies. 

**Rusca et al. 24** (Maria Rusca, Global Development Institute,
University of Manchester, Manchester, UK. Dylan M. Harris, Department of
Geography & Environmental Studies, University of Colorado, Colorado
Springs (UCCS), Colorado Springs, Colorado, USA. Dan Santos, Australian
National Centre for the Public Awareness of Science, Australian National
University, Canberra, Australia. 12-5-2024, \"Experimental and
speculative political ecologies for an age of crisis, hope, and
action,\" Sage Journals,
https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/27539687241298719, //St.
Marks Adam)

A second [approach to]{.underline} [make room for emerging and future
socioeconomic alternatives]{.underline} [involves analyzing narratives
and discourses of the future and their potential to enable or disable
possibilities for just outcomes]{.underline}. Andrew S. Mathews and
Jessica Barnes (2016) argue that [the future is a site of
struggle]{.underline}, [one that is constantly being made in the present
through policy making and political action]{.underline}. Haarstad,
Sareen, and Wanvik (2023) explore [the potential of aspirational goals,
such as Norway\'s Zero Growth Objective, to shape and articulate
present-day climate politics. While some goals may seem lofty or even
impossible, they argue **that a vision of an ideal future can help
prefigure more just outcomes**]{.underline}. However, as Kasia Paprocki
(2019) points out, narratives of ruined futures---of climate-related
threats, such as sea-level rise along the rural coasts of
Bangladesh---are powerful tools used by elites seeking to dispossess
farmers of their lands. [Implicit in Paprocki\'s work, we argue, is the
affirmation that narratives of possible futures]{.underline}, [where
lives can flourish despite climate impacts, is equally as important for
what we see as the promise of speculative political
ecology]{.underline}. Along these lines, we are [also inspired by the
work of Black Geographies, and of abolitionist geographies
specifically]{.underline}, which, [through their critique of the
carceral system and the prison-industrial complex that drives it,
presents visions of a future that exists outside of the confines of the
violence of racial capitalism]{.underline} (Gilmore 2007, 2022).
Further, this scholarship revisits historical injustices---of land
stolen from Black farming families, for example (Heynen 2016)---[and
aims to create conditions for more equitable, reparative pathways for
communities impacted by destructive socioecological
relations]{.underline} (Heynen and Ybarra 2021; Lewis 2023).

In sum, we argue that [**leaning into speculation** is a generative
means of theorizing and analyzing how past and present conditions shape
the contours of the future. Here, we want to acknowledge, and reiterate,
that much of political ecology\'s strength is in its grounded approach
to empirical knowledge production]{.underline}. We [do not see
speculation as dismissive of or lacking empirical analysis]{.underline}.
[Rather, many speculative approaches described here depart from rich
empirical analyses]{.underline} [of past and present socioecological
relations that shape the current environmental and climate crises and
its uneven outcomes, as well as from lived experiments of more desirable
socioecological configurations]{.underline}. Moreover[, drawing from
fields like queer theory and speculative fiction, we see speculation as
**a means of creating empirical analysis**,]{.underline} [similar to how
climate model predictions are treated as empirical evidence of future
climate scenarios]{.underline}. **[Speculation]{.underline} [is a tool
to engage]{.underline}** [with "important **questions of potential that
are often dismissed** because they are not as empirically legible as
typical political ecology scholarship"]{.underline} that tends to focus
on histories of the present (Harris and Santos 2023, 3). The work of
engaging directly in the politics of imagination is a critical first
step toward a more critical version of speculation.

Conclusions---doing speculative and experimental political ecologies:
reparative, proactive, and creative knowledges

In this paper, we have explored the potential of a political ecology
that engages socioecological relations more experimentally and
speculatively. We have shown that complementary theoretical
frameworks---from Diverse Economies to Feminist analyses of Science,
Science Fiction, Black Geographies, Queer Theory, and Multispecies
Ethnographies---[open up **intellectual spaces** to experiment with new
modes of critique **within political ecology scholarship**.]{.underline}
Crucially, [these **theoretical perspectives**]{.underline} have the
potential to **[generate new registers of consciousness that make room
for hope, possibility, survival, resistance, and
creativity]{.underline}**. Concurrently, we have argued that [some of
these intellectual stands are already informing experimental and
speculative political ecology.]{.underline}

To conclude, we highlight what we see as the potential of a political
ecology that engages with experimentation and speculation by returning
to our initial question on the performativity of political-ecological
knowledge. Following Sedgwick\'s and Latour\'s "critique of critique,"
we ask [what knowledge generated through speculative and experimental
approaches does]{.underline}. Specifically, [we consider what these
approaches add to understandings of socioecological relations and to
political-ecological praxis.]{.underline} [First]{.underline},
[experimental and speculative approaches]{.underline} **[are
critical]{.underline}** [for laying the foundations of a future-oriented
and reparative mode of critique that **illuminates emerging and future
possibilities** of alternative worlds]{.underline}. Steven Cork et al.
(2023, 26) claim that **[actualizing fundamental
transformations]{.underline}** in socionatural relations [**requires**
enhanced abilities]{.underline} [to "imagine pathways toward
alternative, preferable futures."]{.underline} Yet, they note that
[contemporary societies]{.underline} are sometimes unequipped with the
cognitive and psychological skills needed to imagine different futures,
particularly when it comes to "possibilities not encountered
before---and the possible pathways toward achieving them." Experimental
and [speculative]{.underline} [approaches leverage the power
of]{.underline} [stories]{.underline} and storytelling,
[imagination]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} [creativity]{.underline}
[to "engage directly in the politics]{.underline} **[of expanding
imaginative]{.underline}, [perspectival, and political
capacity"]{.underline}** (Harris 2022; Harris and Santos 2023, 524).

[Second]{.underline}, the quest for reparative critique is entangled
with its [potential to transform and carry material change. Engaging
with questions about the possible, alternative,]{.underline} [and
desirable futures contributes to questioning assumptions about the
inevitability]{.underline} [of]{.underline} [current and future
socionatural configurations]{.underline}. Concurrently, centering
research on desired futures can strengthen commitments to these futures
by provoking stakeholders' reflections on how to achieve them and,
potentially, reconfigure decision-making around these aspirations (Smith
and Vasudevan 2017; Heynen and Ybarra 2021; Pelling et al. 2023;
Willemin and Backhaus 2021).

[Third]{.underline}, while maintaining a strong focus on generating
theory from rigorous empirical work, [speculative and experimental
approaches]{.underline} [reconfigure research and praxis within
political ecology]{.underline}. For instance, speculative political
ecology scholarship proposes that [the act of speculation]{.underline}
[is a form of research both because it is often grounded in rich
empirical analyses of past and present socioecological configurations
and because it **generates data on desired futures**]{.underline}.
Speculative [political ecologies]{.underline}, [therefore]{.underline},
[allow for new ways of pluralizing knowledge that center on desired
socioecological futures, rather than "just" on past and present
configurations]{.underline}. Similarly, [performative
epistemologies]{.underline} are [grounded on a research process that
also involves enacting socioecological interventions]{.underline}.
[Questions regarding how to navigate the relationship between critique
and activism have always been at the center of political ecology debates
(Blaikie 2012; Blomley 2008; Loftus 2015). The performative
epistemologies discussed in this paper offer a range of approaches to
"**think critically** **but to also act experimentally**"]{.underline}
(Harris 2021, 332).

#### RETAG\-\--mapping good

Marjo **Lindroth et al. 22**, D.Soc.Sc., University Researcher (critical
studies in Arctic politics) at the University of Lapland, with 34

[Community Archaeology in the Arctic]{.underline} and Subarctic

[Issues of participation, collaboration, community-based and
communityinitiated archaeology have been of central importance, and are
of great current concern, in many parts of the Arctic]{.underline}.
These issues have been discussed in particular in relation to
archaeology in the North American Arctic (see e.g. Lyons, 2013, 2016).
In Arctic Russia and Siberia, there has thus far been little explicit
discussion of the concepts of Indigenous archaeology and community
archaeology---and few projects framed in these terms---within the field
of archaeology, although some scholars have addressed these topics
recently (see e.g. Nomokonova, 2021; Shnirelman, 2020). In Siberian
Arctic archaeology, there is also a strong tradition of
ethnoarchaeological approaches, which connect archaeology and
traditional knowledge of the Indigenous Arctic populations.

[The archaeological excavations at the Nunalleq site in Alaska serve as
an example of collaborative archaeology projects.]{.underline} The
excavations, conducted by scholars from the University of Aberdeen in
collaboration with the local Yup'ik community of Quinhagak, have
revealed extremely rich finds from a pre-contact Yup'ik settlement site,
settled for the most part in the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries.
The collaborative project set out to rescue a unique archaeological
heritage threatened by coastal erosion due to climate change as well as
to revitalize local Yup'ik knowledge and traditions (Hillerdal, 2017;
Hillerdal et al., 2019).

[The excavations at the site have uncovered exceptionally rich and
diverse finds, especially organic material, which have greatly increased
the knowledge about Yup'ik pre-contact history]{.underline}. Among the
extensive amount of organic material, one could mention the many
spectacular finds of wooden masks (Mossolova & Knecht, 2019). [Within
the Nunalleq project, educational programmes for schoolchildren have
been developed in collaboration with the local community, the aim being
to reconnect younger generations with Yup'ik cultural traditions through
the archaeological finds and to stimulate creative engagement with the
local past]{.underline} (Watterson & Hillerdal, 2020).

[The Nunalleq site illustrates that climate change]{.underline}---one of
the greatest challenges facing Arctic communities---[is also severely
affecting the history and heritage of local Arctic communities, leading
to a large-scale loss of knowledge about past times]{.underline}.
Large-scale [erosion along coastlines, as well as the melting of
permafrost and glaciers]{.underline} (see e.g. Hollesen et al., 2018),
[has deteriorated and destroyed enormous amounts of archaeological
remains, especially organic artefacts, in the Arctic
regions]{.underline}. This damage will continue in the coming years,
prompting more archaeological monitoring, surveys and rescue excavations
in the affected areas. [The Nunalleq project illustrates the urgent need
for archaeological investigations to examine historical sites in the
Arctic and Subarctic threatened by environmental and climate
change]{.underline}. The project [also underlines the importance of
local community engagement and involvement for the monitoring and
protection of archaeological heritage threatened by climate
change.]{.underline}

[Community-based archaeology and heritage initiatives are becoming
increasingly debated in Sámi contexts as well]{.underline}. In Sweden
and Norway, South Sámi archaeology has been an especially controversial
field of archaeology, with discussions on the possibilities and problems
of ethnic interpretations of prehistoric archaeological remains,
especially from the Iron Age (Ojala, 2009: 141--164; Zachrisson et al.,
1997). As mentioned earlier, there have been several court cases in the
South Sámi region, in which the rights of Sámi villages to reindeer
grazing lands have been contested. Archaeological arguments have also
been used in the court proceedings. As the deep history of the South
Sámi population in their present-day territory has been questioned, the
interest in archaeology as a way of exploring and engaging with one's
own history has increased. [Several collaborative projects have been
carried out involving archaeologists, museums and local communities,
such as local Sámi villages]{.underline} (the organizational unit for
reindeer herding in Sweden). [These projects have focused on mapping
cultural landscapes and documenting traditions, land use, significant
places and resources, place-names as well as archaeological
remains]{.underline} (Ljungdahl & Norberg, 2012; Norberg & Fossum, 2011;
Norberg & Winka, 2014).

Another example of a community-based heritage initiative is Árrangádde
at Ájtte---the Swedish Sámi and Mountain Museum in Jåhkåmåhkke/Jokkmokk,
northern Sweden. It was a project that sought to generate discussions
and exchange of knowledge and experiences across generations centring
around Sámi sites with hearths (árran in North Sámi). The work aimed to
connect Sámi traditional knowledge with young people's everyday life and
featured archaeological expertise on the archaeological remains (Nutti &
Kuoljok, 2014). The hearth sites are not only archaeological resources
for investigating past times, but also part of living traditions. In
this way, the initiative opened up new perspectives on the hearth, one
of the most common ancient remains in Sápmi and the northern parts of
Fennoscandia (see further about hearth sites in Sápmi in Hedman, 2003;
Halinen & Olsen, 2019).

[Archaeological mapping projects using Participatory GIS (Geographical
Information Systems) have also been developed as a tool for local
communities to map and visualize landscape resources and significant
places, for instance, in Sámi communities]{.underline} in northernmost
Norway (Barlindhaug, 2013). [This can be seen as part of a movement of
alternative mapping, or counter-mapping, of histories and resources, one
providing more dynamic and complex landscape perspectives, in contrast
to the long tradition of colonial mapping and map-making in
Sápmi]{.underline} (cf. Ojala & Nordin, 2019).

In a study of Swedish contract archaeology in Sápmi (which takes place
in connection with construction or other land development affecting
archaeological sites), Charina Knutson has examined how the
international debates on collaborative and community-based archaeology,
as well as international agreements such as the United Nations
Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples mentioned above, have
been received and incorporated in Swedish archaeology in Sápmi (Knutson,
2021). The results of her study show that the international debates and
the UNDRIP have affected contract, or rescue, archaeology in the Swedish
part of Sápmi to a very limited extent---much less than research-driven
archaeology. She argues that more discussion on this topic is needed
among archaeologists and heritage authorities and institutions in
Sweden.

According to this narrative, [the political agency of the Global Arctic

#### AViewing the arctic as strictly "indigenous land" is [dangerous]{.underline}

Graham **Huggan**\* and Lars **Jensen**\*\* **16**, \*PhD in comparative

For some commentators (see, e.g., Brody 1976), [colonialism in the
Arctic is synonymous with continuing mistreatment of the region's

#### Foreign actors can be [coopted]{.underline} via refusing demands to symbolically affirm action. Effective demands for concrete action break free from colonial frames of understanding.

31.1 Against Neocolonialism

[We have become **increasingly attuned** to the **necessity of asserting
our rights** when dealing with]{.underline} transnational corporations
and [powerful state actors,]{.underline} and such [**actors** have in
turn become **increasingly aware** of the **necessity** of taking our
rights into account]{.underline} (Wilson 2016; Papillon and Rodon 2017).
[**Foreign capital** and **expertise** is **not in itself
bad**]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} it is [**vital** to ensure that
foreign-led and financed economic activities in the Arctic **serve the
needs of Arctic peoples** as well in order to prevent becoming caught up
in neocolonial processe]{.underline}s. From mining companies and fishing
interests to government authorities based in distant seats of power, [we
**must seek** to simultaneously be open to the world and **avoid
becoming** a **passive** cog in the great machine of global capital and
realpolitik.]{.underline}

The globalisation of corporate power is accompanied by a globalisation
of risk and of concern for Indigenous peoples and the future of the
Arctic. [As the world awakens to the dangers of climate
change]{.underline}, increasing attention is being paid to scientific
research into the Arctic, [research]{.underline} that -- though **[often
well-meaning]{.underline}** -- is also often [embedded in metropolitan
mindsets]{.underline}, which ultimately [reinforce]{.underline} existing
power [structures]{.underline}, priorities, and dependencies. Each new
scientific report produced from the metropole highlights new challenges
for Arctic peoples while advancing ever-more technocratic discourses and
globalist solutions. People the world over are at last becoming aware of
how important the Arctic is to them, yet this [growing
recognition]{.underline} is [not guaranteed]{.underline} to [result in
greater influence for Arctic peoples]{.underline}; it might just as
easily result in renewed metropolitan efforts to control developments in
the region to suit state or globalist interests -- perhaps at the
expense of those who make the Arctic their home. Indeed, [international
focus on climate change may]{.underline} in some cases [be politically
convenient for powerful actors]{.underline} rather than ideally suited
for improving the lives of those in the affected regions (Kelman et al.
2015; Baldacchino and Kelman 2014).

[We **require** **knowledge**, **research**, and
**resources**]{.underline} [from]{.underline} [outside the
region]{.underline} if we are [to undertake]{.underline} sustainable
[development]{.underline} and **[take advantage]{.underline}** of the
**[changing conditions]{.underline}**. However, [care must]{.underline}
be [taken]{.underline} to [avoid buying into]{.underline} or submitting
to a [neocolonialisation]{.underline} of the Arctic. Images of melting
glaciers and starving polar bears may galvanise the wider public and
power holders to action in a manner that is not beneficial to Arctic
peoples.

The peoples of the Arctic are not responsible for having produced global
environmental threats, yet **[metropolitan society]{.underline}** [calls
upon us to forego]{.underline} economic [development]{.underline} and
extractive industries, [take]{.underline} **[symbolic action against
global challenges,]{.underline}** and **[stand at the
vanguard]{.underline}** [of a new era of 'conspicuous
sustainability']{.underline} (Grydehøj and Kelman 2016). [Such efforts
to persuade us to turn our lands and seas into nature preserves, to
transform our traditional territories into a form of World Heritage and
present the Arctic as a treasure belonging equally to the world as a
whole]{.underline}, are ultimately requests that we who have been so
long exploited and so thoroughly dispossessed further sacrifice
ourselves for the continued wellbeing of those who have proven unwilling
to make sacrifices. [**Effective efforts** to **preserve** Arctic
**environments** and **revitalise Arctic livelihoods** should take
**precedence over symbolic** **action** and 'conspicuous sustainability'
that provides little real benefit]{.underline} (Grydehøj and Kelman
2016). **[We do not ask to live from the charity of metropolitan
benefactors]{.underline}**. [We do not wish]{.underline} [to be the
realisation]{.underline} [of]{.underline} metropolitan visions of happy,
[hardy Northerners]{.underline} in need of patronage from the forces of
global capital. **[The last thing we want is to be a canary in the
coalmine, a cautionary tale]{.underline}** (Farbotko 2010).

#### Arctic policymaking is key. Collective Arctic action that engages with state actors allows for a ruptural break from neocolonialism and guides the stolen capital of the West back into Arctic control, especially over contingent issues like climate crisis.

Ulunnguaq **Markussen** 6-16-20**17**. Principal at the Social
Pedagogical Seminary at the University of Greenland. \"Towards an Arctic
Awakening: Neocolonalism, Sustainable Development, Emancipatory

https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-57532-2_31. 苗

31.2 A New Era of Arctic Cooperation

[If we are to guide change in the Arctic]{.underline} rather than merely
become victims of it [or take on roles designed to appeal to
metropolitan sensibilities]{.underline}, it is [necessary to **accept**
that **change** is occurring and then **commit to managing
it.**]{.underline} The Arctic is home to many peoples, who possess
diverse sets of needs. Yet [we are **united** by a number of shared --
if not **universal** -- interests]{.underline}, which can be supported
through **[collective action]{.underline}**, whether [through **formal
bodies** that can **engage with sovereign** **states** or through
looser, more flexible coalitions of actors]{.underline}. What is clear
is that [Arctic sustainability depends on long-term
governance]{.underline} across national borders
[transcending]{.underline} [single]{.underline} [state,
corporate]{.underline}, or even [ethnic interests]{.underline}, in light
of the **[remarkable interdependence]{.underline}** of Arctic peoples
and environments surrounding their shared sea.

[As Arctic peoples]{.underline}, [we]{.underline} [must]{.underline}
[identify what we really want]{.underline} so that we can begin
**[setting common targets]{.underline}**, planning for and
**[implementing strategic actions]{.underline}**, and **[taking on
strategic responsibilities]{.underline}**. By [creating a true Arctic
community]{.underline}, one that works at [multiple levels of
**formality** and through multiple channels]{.underline}, we can at last
truly gain a voice (Plaut 2012). [An openness to informal
political]{.underline} [organisation]{.underline} [must]{.underline}
[complement a **dedication** to constructing **strong** institutional
foundations for **Arctic policymaking** that reflects shared values and
priorities]{.underline}. As we work to define our own ethnic and
national identities, [we should seek to locate these within a wider
Arctic identity]{.underline}, one that can [enrich]{.underline} --
rather than overshadow -- our [localised values]{.underline}. Such a
multifaceted, **[pan-Arctic]{.underline}**
**[policymaking]{.underline}** platform could [strengthen our ability to
engage with non-Arctic actors]{.underline} ([including]{.underline}
metropolitan interests within **[states]{.underline}** [that possess
Arctic territory]{.underline}), which are both [affecting and affected
by changes taking place within the Arctic]{.underline}. This contrasts
with current regional bodies and institutions, which often take a broad
state-centric perspective (for example, the Arctic Council and the
Barents Euro-Arctic Council) or a narrow focus on Indigenous interests
(for example, the Inuit Circumpolar Council and the Indigenous Peoples'
Secretariat): Both such perspectives are necessary and important, but
the former places agency in the hands of the metropole while the latter
risks creating false divisions between Arctic communities, erroneously
grouping the interests of Arctic non-Indigenous peoples with those of
the metropole.

Arctic economic, societal, cultural, and political development must be
led by Arctic peoples themselves, working to develop peaceful and
constructive relationships at the community, state, and other levels. A
sense of a shared stake in the Arctic can even help foster better
relationships between Indigenous peoples and former colonisers,
supporting a decolonial Arctic politics that regards all Arctic peoples
as equal -- understanding the consequences of historic injustice without
perpetuating ethnic and political animosity. Understanding today's
Arctic and envisioning a future Arctic requires acknowledgment that,
across much of the North, "the institutional and ideological
interference of the colonial period directly or indirectly, subtly or
overtly, continues to influence political thought and economic agendas"
(Nadarajah and Grydehøj 2016: 442; see also Grydehøj 2016).

[These **must not**, however, be **decisions made by a local
elite**]{.underline}. It is [important]{.underline} that the
[entire]{.underline} [population]{.underline} of the Arctic be [involved
in the move toward sustainable]{.underline} regional
[development]{.underline} and come to [understand the benefits to
**collective action** and **collective development.**]{.underline} At
the same time, [desired development]{.underline} outcomes [must be
localised]{.underline}; [no longer]{.underline} can it be
[acceptable]{.underline} for Arctic actors to [pursue]{.underline}
[competitive]{.underline}, **[zero-sum games against one
another]{.underline}**. At the same time, as [we recognise that shared
development may require certain shared sacrifices]{.underline}, we must
draw the line at sacrificing some communities solely in favour of
others.

31.3 Arctic Research and Education

This kind of [regional awareness and bottom-up policymaking]{.underline}
[requires]{.underline} enhanced knowledge on the part of Arctic peoples
and an **[improved connection between science and
policymaking]{.underline}**. [Education]{.underline} is
[key]{.underline} to this, with actors such as UArctic working to [build
local capacity through education]{.underline}. Yet it is important to be
conscious of neocolonial forces within education systems and to prevent
the development of an Arctic cultural elite that is distanced from
knowledge of ordinary communities across the region. Development
planning is increasingly driven by systemitisation, data collection, and
technocratic discourses, which often combine to privilege metropolitan
interests and expertise, leading to local suspicion of development and
planning in general (Pugh 2013, 2016).

Without denying the importance of 'evidence-based policymaking', it is
necessary to recognise that, in today's world, not all kinds of evidence
are treated equally. In particular, the experiences of Arctic Indigenous
peoples, who have centuries or millennia of experience living in close
interaction with nature, can contribute lessons in flexible livelihoods
and adaptability to harsh and changing environments (e.g. Pearce et al.
2015). [**Climate change**, for example, will have serious and possibly
even disastrous repercussions in some parts of the world, but this
change will need to be **dealt with**, and its effects on Arctic
environments and peoples may not be straightforwardly
negative]{.underline}. It is vital that Arctic policy breaks free from
the crippling strictures of neoliberal risk, vulnerability, and
resilience discourse (Evans and Reid 2014). [**Scientific research** and
**evidence-based policymaking** that is sensitive to Arctic conditions
may reach quite different conclusions than research and **policymaking**
with a metropolitan orientation]{.underline}. The scientifically and
technocratically motivated drive toward 'modernisation' asks Arctic
Indigenous peoples to submit to a developmental model that has proved
environmentally calamitous on the global scale and that has been applied
elsewhere with such poor results in earlier periods of decolonisation.
Involvement of Arctic peoples in Arctic research is a necessary but
insufficient condition for truly locally oriented research: The
framework, expectations, and design of the research must itself be
decolonised.

[Arctic peoples require an emancipatory and decolonial system of
research and education]{.underline}, which [neither turns its back on
knowledge and cutting-edge scholarship nor find itself trapped in
preconceived notions regarding the creation of colonial]{.underline} (or
postcolonial) [subjects]{.underline}. **[Scientific]{.underline}**
**[research]{.underline}** itself -- from the humanities, social
sciences, and natural sciences -- [should be undertaken by or in close
engagement with people from a diverse range of communities]{.underline},
who can both contribute their own perspectives to the wider scientific
world and better communicate findings to their own communities (Mulligan
and Nadarajah 2008). [**Greater investment** in Arctic science is
**critical for achieving Arctic sustainability** -- but only if this
science has firm roots in Arctic communities.]{.underline}

Arctic peoples share a number of cultural values but also possess
cultural distinctions from one another, which produces a rich seam of
traditional and Indigenous knowledge that can be drawn upon in
developing Arctic research and education. [**Truly Arctic research** and
**education does not** **involve** **rejecting science** or **Western
learning**;]{.underline} it instead [**involves** the **investment** of
such knowledge with local values in order to better serve local
needs.]{.underline} To paraphrase Grant McCall (1994), Arctic studies
must consider Arctic peoples on their own terms, not on those of
metropolitan well-wishers, development workers, or political
authorities.

31.4 Towards an Arctic Awakening

Global change is producing new challenges for peoples of the Arctic, but
it is also bringing us closer together. We cannot simply draw the line
at the individual community, ethnic group, nation, or even state: It is
vital for us all to get engaged with the shared Arctic project. By
grasping the necessity of change, we can enter a new era of regional
stability, shared purpose, and political power.

**[Change is coming.]{.underline}** We may find it daunting, yet [it is
up to us, the peoples of the Arctic, to say:]{.underline} [**We will
no** **longer be passive observers** of our development but **will take
charge of our futures.**]{.underline}

#### Writing off legal frameworks as products of colonialism prevents their usage to solve real world problems now. The EU can be used to protect the Sámi people. 

Anja MáRjá Nystø **Keskitalo** **and** Jacqueline **Wingens**

Potential of an EU internal Indigenous Peoples Policy
