authorities.24)

#### Indigenous communities [benefit]{.underline} from policy action that works towards the green transition.

**Bjørst et al '23** PhD and Associate Professor, Dept. of Culture &
Learning, Aalborg University (Denmark) Sigríður Kristjánsdóttir, Ph.D in
Environmental Sciences (Iceland) Christopher Clarke-McQueen, Architect
and PhD fellow at McGill University (Canada) Jaime DeSimone, Curator of
Contemporary Art (USA) Anna Krook-Riekkola, Associate Professor, Energy
Engineering, Luleå University of Technology (Sweden) Andrea Kraj, PhD
and Microgrid & Energy Transition Advisor (Canada) ("Arctic
Infrastructure: Considerations in the Green Transition", *Fullbright
Arctic Initiative III,* xx-xx-2023,
<https://fulbrightscholars.org/sites/default/files/documents/Arctic_Infrastructure_in_a_Changing_Environment_Paper.pdf>,
DOA: 7/2/2025)//LFS---LMH

[Position: Green transition as a driver of change The Arctic has the
potential to play an important role in the green transition, as
illustrated in the four fingerprints. Both as an important provider of
resources (material and energy), and as assistance to meet the climate
goals agreed upon in the 2015 Paris Agreement by making it easier to
replace fossil fuels with renewables as well as providing carbon
storage.]{.underline} Meanwhile, changes also bring opportunities and
green transition can be a driver of change in the Arctic -- to improve
quality of life, well-being, and support the local livelihoods in Arctic
communities. For the peoples of the Arctic, there is a need for both
sustaining the good life while being part of a global green transition
moving away from fossil fuels dependency. [Green transitions, if managed
well, can be of particular importance to the continuation and
proliferation of Indigenous cultures, as we learned from NWT. However,
other settings we have identified include how the concept can offer a
"promise" about being green and transformative without being binding or
committing to local needs or agendas.]{.underline} As FAI scholars, we
would like to encourage a green transition informed by the knowledge and
expertise that already exists within the local communities, also make
sure to connect local communities across the Arctic regions as to be
inspired by each other (instead of flying consultants up from the
south). [There are many good examples across the Arctics - like our
fingerprints - that can be beneficial for building multipurpose and
multiuser infrastructure fit for Arctic citizens. Takeaways from our
field trips, observations, and document analysis conducted in various
places in the Arctic on the current position is: 1. The Arctic has a new
strategic role because of the green transition, 2. Arctic communities
lack physical as well as policy infrastructure for a successful
transition, 3. Green transition is not "a one size fits all" in the
Arctic; different communities have different opportunities as well as
requirements when it comes to green transition, 4. There is a knowledge
gap both in terms of what Arctic communities need from a transition, and
how these needs best could be met, and 5. Green transition can become an
important driver of change in the Arctic.]{.underline}

### AT: Biopolitics

#### Biopolitics don't actually exist and the concept is flawed

**Solis & Moya 20**, Angel Octavio Alvarez Solis is professor of
philosophy at the university Iberoamericana in Mexico City. Moya holds
an M.A. in philosophy from the same university. "Against Biopolitics:
Compensation and Confinement: Entombing the Academic Left", September 25
2020,, https://publicseminar.org/essays/against-biopolitics/

[Perhaps due to its successful discursive dissemination, there are few
thinkers --the less judicious or the most reactionary ones-- who have
questioned Michel Foucault's late ideas. These disputes about the
"truth" of biopolitics culminate in such paroxysm that critique has
become a continuous theological diatribe. Everyone is engaged in an
amendment or a commentary on the grand biopolitical text: Paul B.
Preciado disputes Roberto Esposito; Esposito corrects Jean-Luc Nancy;
Nancy revises Giorgio Agamben; Agamben adjusts Foucualt, and so on,
until any of us --any average reader-- finds oneself accepting that the
virus is a product of biopower.]{.underline} However, if we pause the
time of the event, [if we take a step back from the totalizing demand of
biopolitics, we may envisage that the **biopolitical vocabulary disables
any outside and all exits**. As a good compensatory theory, biopolitics
is an anthropological diagnosis. As a good theology, biopolitics
substantiates life and kidnaps its own subject matter: life, whether it
is one's own, the life of others, or life in its constitutive
fragility.]{.underline}

I am probably exaggerating and not all biopolitics amounts to
confinement. The demand for confinement is probably compatible with
ungovernable life. Indeed, maybe the intensification of absolute control
over life is just beginning to constitute a new world order. Every
crisis has its own doubts, exaggerations, and prophecies. But crises are
also measures of time, indicators, and thresholds. The pandemic is
undoubtedly a threshold. A threshold that doesn't open up a future
because the future is the mode of time currently in jeopardy, so we have
no options or decisions to make about it. [Therefore, the problem is
that if biopolitics is defined as the sovereign administration of life,
it is precisely because, ultimately, life cannot be administered. The
sovereignty of the body is a liberal illusion. Life is always
ungovernable. As Ariadne's thread, biopolitics weaves its sovereign
arguments to construct the garment of confinement.]{.underline}

[**Due to the latter, biopolitics sets up subjectivity in a permanent
state of war:** a subjectivity articulated as a phármakon that is
capable of carrying within it both evil and salvation.]{.underline} A
gnostic subjectivity. A subjectivity that cures and poisons. A
subjectivity with friends and enemies. [A subjectivity which is at war
with itself and with the world because its isolation "saves lives" and,
even if "contaminated," has no other choice but survival]{.underline}.
That the possibility of a virus lives within each of us implies that
everyone bears the accelerated death of others and oneself. Biopolitics
transforms us into Poe's red masque or into Melville's Bartleby: forms
of anguish that cover the death drive. Biopolitics is always
necropolitics.

Biopolitics occludes the right to death. Biopolitics ensures that life
--just as death-- is a political act, that burying our dead is a
political activity and, in such a total capture of existence, we find
one of the greatest obstacles to live again. Not even the dead are safe
from the exercises of sovereignty. The dead deserve to be outside
statistical expropriation. This is why Giambattista Vico, the
philosopher of the Italian plague of 1656, was not wrong when he claimed
that, no matter how barbaric or civilized, every human form of life
possesses religion, marriage and burial. The loss of the burial rite is
the victory of biopolitics. The number trumps the name. The right to
bury our dead is the first and last locus of resistance against
biopolitical closure. The tomb is the last testimony of life, of our
life. [**Therefore, biopolitics cannot be used to exit biopolitics**. We
must avoid the biopolitical temptation of Antigone's oblivion because,
as María Zambrano reminds us, "Antigone's tomb is our own obscured
consciousness."]{.underline}

[Biopolitics forecloses the possibility of learning to live amongst
specters, amongst the "non-living," amongst the "dead" or the "nor
living nor dead,"]{.underline} because its aim of governing all life
penetrates the fantasy that life is more important than death: the death
of anyone, the death of our beloved, the death of the unknown.
[Biopolitics is not interested in tombs but in morgues, it does not
imagine cemeteries but death pits, it does not project lives but counts
deaths.]{.underline} Finally, [as a psychic structure, biopolitics is
not capable of governing life nor generating a sense for
survival]{.underline}. As a theory, [biopolitics does not explain how
the life of the dead is possible, nor does it consider the importance of
burial for life itself]{.underline}. Maybe [it can only envision the
politics of death as a statistical extension of
populations]{.underline}. [**Biopolitics does not conceive of an outside
where not all life is political and not all death is a political
issue**. **Biopolitics is, ultimately, academia's mode of confinement**.
For this reason, slightly fragile and untimely, **biopolitics is the
compensation of the academic Left in order to avoid contemplating its
own tomb** and realizing that, for a long time, it has carried the virus
of sad passions within itself.]{.underline}

## AT: Alts

### 2AC\--Consequentialism

#### Refusing to consider [consequences]{.underline} makes them [complicit]{.underline} in the structures they critique.

**Zembylas, 25**---Professor of Educational Theory and Curriculum
Studies at the Open University of Cyprus (Michalinos, "Decolonial
pathways in education: Walter Mignolo, epistemic delinking, and the
risks of ethno-essentialism," Globalisation, Societies and Education,
February 3, 2025, DOI: 10.1080/14767724.2025.2459110, dml)

In his critical analysis of intellectual decolonisation, Moosavi
(Citation2020) cautions that while decolonisation efforts are essential,
they come with notable limitations. In particular, he identifies five
such **[limitations]{.underline}** that [can **undermine** the
effectiveness of decolonisation efforts]{.underline}: [the tendency to
**oversimplify** decolonisation as a **straightforward**
task]{.underline}; [the risk of **essentialising** or **appropriating**
the Global South]{.underline}; [the failure to recognise the **varied**
and **complex** nature of marginalisation within academia]{.underline};
[the pitfalls of **nativism**]{.underline}, which may elevate certain
cultural knowledge uncritically; [and **tokenism**]{.underline}, [which
can **reduce** decolonisation to **superficial gestures** rather than
**meaningful change**]{.underline}. Moosavi argues that [identifying
these pitfalls is **critical**]{.underline}, [as certain approaches to
intellectual decolonisation may **inadvertently perpetuate** the **very
coloniality they aim to dismantle**]{.underline}, thereby
[**reinforcing** power hierarchies and marginalisation rather than
**subverting** them]{.underline}. His insights serve as a reminder that
decolonisation must be approached thoughtfully and critically, ensuring
it is grounded in genuine inclusivity and avoids replicating reductive
or exclusionary practices.

Similarly, Naicker (Citation2023) warns that decolonial theory's
'[exclusively and moralistically exclusionary anti-Westernism begins
**mirroring** the rightist tendencies it sought to locate **solely**
within a **fabricated Western essence**]{.underline}' (237).Footnote1
Although such a sweeping critique risks oversimplifying decolonial
theory, it raises important concerns about the potential pitfalls of an
epistemic approach to decolonisation, particularly regarding its
reliance on identity politics. [When decolonial discourse **primarily**
focuses on **rejecting** Western frameworks **without** self-reflection
on the **consequences** of such move]{.underline}, [it risks becoming
**entangled** in the **same exclusionary impulses** it seeks to
challenge]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} critique points to a crucial
tension in decolonial thought: how can scholars engage in meaningful
anticolonial work without reducing the complexities of colonial power
relations to a binary opposition between 'the West' and 'the non-West'?

One reason I prefer the term 'anticolonial' (Sefa Dei and Lordan
Citation2016; Zembylas Citation2024) over 'decolonial' is because it
[underscores the need to embed **historical awareness** and **political
engagement** into decolonial work]{.underline}. [This]{.underline}
approach [challenges scholars to go **beyond** a strictly epistemic
focus]{.underline}, [asking]{.underline} instead [whether
our]{.underline} decolonisation [efforts address **material** realities
and power structures that perpetuate colonial legacies]{.underline}.
[While epistemic decolonisation **critiques** Western knowledge
hierarchies]{.underline}, [it often **stops short** of engaging with the
**political** and **material mechanisms** that reproduce these
hierarchies]{.underline}. [This raises an important
question]{.underline} for decolonial scholarship in education: [Are
scholars at risk of **inadvertently reinforcing** essentialist or
exclusionary narratives that may align with nationalist or authoritarian
agendas?]{.underline}

Future investigations into anticolonial thought in education should
therefore consider several critical questions. How can scholars ensure
that decolonial work does not become co-opted to support
ethno-essentialist or exclusionary politics, as seen when nationalist
discourses appropriate decolonial language to advance their own
hegemonic ends? How can decolonial scholarship in education remain open
to plural voices and multiple narratives without devolving into
uncritical nativism? Additionally, how might we address the complex
interplay between local and global forms of oppression without reducing
anticolonial thought to a simplistic dichotomy between 'the West' and
'the non-West'? [Addressing these questions may allow education scholars
to develop anticolonial approaches that not only **critique** epistemic
colonialism]{.underline}, [but also **actively challenge** political and
material structures that **hinder** collective liberation and social
justice]{.underline} through educational policies and practices [in
**different** political contexts]{.underline}.

### 2AC\-\--Crackdown

#### The government will crack down on indigenous resurgence in instances that threaten government projects -- empirics.

**Dhillon & Parrish '19** Dhillon is an anti-colonial scholar and
organizer who grew up on Treaty Six Cree Territory in Saskatchewan,
Canada. She is associate professor of global studies and anthropology at
The New School in New York City, and Parrish is an investigative
journalist focused on government repression, militarism, and popular
responses to the global climate crisis. (Jaskiran & Will, "Exclusive:
Canada police prepared to shoot Indigenous activists, documents show",
*The Guardian,* 12-20-2019,
<https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/dec/20/canada-indigenous-land-defenders-police-documents>,
DOA: 7/3/25)//LFS---LMH

[Canadian police were prepared to shoot Indigenous land defenders
blockading construction of a natural gas pipeline in northern British
Columbia, according to documents seen by the Guardian.]{.underline}

[Notes from a strategy session for ]{.underline}[a militarized raid on
ancestral lands of the Wet'suwet'en
nation](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/jan/08/canada-14-arrested-indigenous-anti-pipeline-protest-camp)[ show
that commanders of Canada's national police force, the Royal Canadian
Mounted Police (RCMP), argued that "lethal overwatch is req'd"
-- ]{.underline}[a term for deploying an officer who is prepared to use
lethal
force](https://www.crcc-ccetp.gc.ca/pdf/chrisKlim-rcmpR-eng.pdf)[.]{.underline}

[The RCMP commanders also instructed officers to "use as much violence
toward the gate as you want" ahead of the operation to remove a
roadblock which had been erected by Wet'suwet'en people to control
access to their territories and stop construction of the proposed 670km
(416-mile) ]{.underline}[Coastal GasLink
pipeline](https://www.tcenergy.com/operations/natural-gas/coastal-gaslink)[ (CGL).]{.underline}

[In a separate document, an RCMP officer states that arrests would be
necessary for "sterilizing \[the\] site".]{.underline}

[Wet'suwet'en people and their supporters set up the Gidimt'en
checkpoint in December 2018 to block construction of the pipeline
through this region of mountains and pine forests 750 miles north of
Vancouver.]{.underline}

[On 7 January, RCMP officers -- dressed in military-green fatigues and
armed with assault rifles -- ]{.underline}[descended on the
checkpoint](https://thetyee.ca/Analysis/2019/01/08/LNG-Pipeline-Unistoten-Blockade/)[,
dismantling the gate and arresting 14 people.]{.underline}

[The checkpoint lies 22km east of a camp operated by a house group of
the Wet'suwet'en called
the ]{.underline}[Unist'ot'en](https://unistoten.camp/)[, which has been
at the center of the struggle against the pipeline.]{.underline}

[The camp is one of several instances where Indigenous people in British
Columbia have reinhabited ancestral territory that falls outside of
demarcated reservations, in what they refer to as
"reoccupation".]{.underline}

[Unist'ot'en spokesperson Freda Huson (Howilhkat) said that the RCMP's
militarized posture during the raid was consistent with a long history
of colonial violence.]{.underline}

["In our experience, since first contact, RCMP have been created by the
federal government to dispossess ]{.underline}[Indigenous
peoples](https://www.theguardian.com/world/indigenous-peoples)[ of their
lands," Huson said. "They have proven \[that\] through their harassment
of my people to support Coastal GasLink in invading our
territories."]{.underline}

[Police records seen by the Guardian include transcripts from police
strategy sessions, reports filed after the raid and audio and video
files.]{.underline}

[One document noted that the Wet'suwet'en possessed "firearms for
hunting/sustenance" but police intelligence indicated that there was "no
single threat indicating that \[land defenders\] will use
firearms".]{.underline}

[An RCMP spokesperson declined to comment on the specific content of the
documents, saying they were merely carrying out a December 2018
injunction against people who interfere with the CGL
pipeline.]{.underline}

["During the planning for the enforcement of the court-ordered
injunction, the RCMP took the remote location of the Morice River Bridge
into account and ensured that enough police officers were present in the
area to keep the peace," the spokesperson said. "We also took into
consideration the unpredictable nature of what we could face in the
remote area, and so we moved additional police resources including
members of the tactical and emergency response teams to provide
support."]{.underline}

[The revelations come as the Wet'suwet'en camps brace for a provincial
supreme court ]{.underline}[ruling on an
injunction](https://yellowheadinstitute.org/2018/12/12/an-injunction-against-the-unistoten-camp/)[ applied
for by the pipeline builder TC Energy (formerly TransCanada), which
seeks to permanently restrict the Wet'suwet'en from blocking access to
pipeline work sites.]{.underline}

### 2AC\-\--AT: Refusal alt

#### Rejecting maps fails.

Terrence W. **Haverluk '14** \[(Academy Scholars Program, United States
Air Force Academy, USAFA, CO, USA) \"The Three Critical Flaws of
Critical Geopolitics: Towards a Neo-Classical Geopolitics,\" Taylor &
Francis,
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/14650045.2013.803192,
1-21-2014\]

This [anti-map stance]{.underline} can be [traced back to classic
geopolitics and]{.underline} especially Mackinder's agenda to use [maps
to "geo-graph" the world]{.underline}, to "write territory" for the
imperialist agenda of the British Empire. In mapping the empire the
British created Ireland, created British East Africa and many other
places by focusing the British "scientific imperial eye" on its
territories.22 By elevating the visual, scientific agenda through maps,
Mackinder's geopolitics became a practice of "visualizing and surveying
global space with a view that writes."23 [Since critical geopolitics has
a radical, anti-imperial agenda,]{.underline} the [use of maps could be
misconstrued as condoning a Western imperialist agenda]{.underline}.
[Maps are an important tool in geopolitics]{.underline} and essential
when attempting to explain global conflict, [but they are studiously
avoided in critical geopolitics]{.underline}. Let's look again at
Roberts, Secor, and Sparke's critique of Thomas Barnett's "Pentagon's
New Map." According to Barnett the areas within the lines in Figure 1
represent parts of the world that are "disconnected," i.e., they lack
the rule of law, a robust judiciary, functioning institutions, a working
infrastructure and high speed internet connections. It is here where the
US is fighting terrorists, pirates, drug smugglers, gun runners, and
human traffickers. These are also the places where the US military has
deployed most often since 1990 (Figure 1, Appendix A). Barnett calls
this part of the world the "Gap." The "Core" in Figure 1 consists of
those states outside the "Gap." This core constitutes most of the Group
of 20 states (G-20). Barnett, unknowingly, identified the G-20 as
constituting the global "Core," and, in fact, the G-20 is the core of
the global economy. The G-20 constitutes 90% of global GDP, 85% of
global trade, and 75% of global population.24 Barnett argues that the
disconnect in the Gap fosters terrorism and repressive regimes. His
advice to the Pentagon is that by shrinking the Gap, the Core could
increase their security and reduce terrorism. Barnett specifically
states that neo-liberalism has the potential to shrink the gap -- which
is anti-anti-geopolitics. Roberts, Secor, and Sparke argue that
Barnett's neo-liberal, pro-globalisation argument, like modernity and
development before it (and let's not forget Marxism), is a totalising
discourse that presents the false idea that free trade is the path to
world peace and prosperity. To Roberts, Secor, and Sparke, "The map is
both that which is to be explained and the explanation itself,
descriptive of the recent past and predictive of future action. For
Barnett, the map reveals an indisputable pattern from which geopolitical
knowledge can be read."25 It is "binary" and Barnett seems intent to
create the Gap as a continuous area. There is no "Gap in the Core and no
Core in the Gap; no details that might disrupt his Mackinderesque bands
of homogenized planetary difference."26 [Again the few maps employed by
critical geo-politicians are the ones they critique]{.underline} -- in
the Geopolitical Reader it was Mackinder's map of 1904, and now it's
Barnett's map of 2003. [Political scientists and economists don't seem
to have any problems analysing conflict at a global scale, i.e.,
"Mackinderesque," but in critical geopolitics any attempt to map the
world at the global scale is usually dismissed as "Mackinderesque" and
therefore imperialist, racist, sexist, and deterministic]{.underline}.27
[According to critical geopolitics, after one hundred years, geographers
are still getting their maps wrong]{.underline}. Roberts, Secor, and
Sparke go on to say that the "Gap thus represents a (f)rigid and torrid
zone, both wretched and resistant." They continue by stating the
audience for Barnett's map is just "one more incarnation of the
masculine authority figure, the hetero-patriarchical savior \... (read
Mackinder)."28 [Even if their critique is correct, it is tautological
because just as the map "is that which is to be explained and the
explanation itself" the critique of the map is that which is to be
critiqued and the critique itself]{.underline}. In their conclusion
there is no alternative geopolitics provided, but simply a critique of
how the map was purportedly used to persuade people in power to invade
Iraq and advance a neo-liberal, Western imperial agenda. [Rather than
simply critiquing the 2,000-year-old Greek latitudinal zonation model,
critical geopolitics could include more nuanced environmental hypotheses
such as]{.underline} those presented in Jeffrey Sach's [European
Mortality Index]{.underline}; Jared Diamond's [founder crop
hypothesis]{.underline}; Fareed [Zakaria's explanation of the rise of
Europe; or]{.underline} Jim Lee's [eco-regional approach to global
conflict]{.underline}.29 [Yes, the Greek model was simplistic and
(f)rigid, but there have been real advances in physical and economic
geography over the last 2,000 years]{.underline}, which brings us to our
third critic -- radical geopolitics is anti-environmental.

**The alternative cannot solve it so long as it requires the judge's
affirmation.**

Ilan **Kapoor 24**, Professor of Critical Development Studies at the
York University Faculty of Environmental and Urban Change, Ph.D. in

Psychoanalysis adds another significant dimension here: the reason why
[intersectional identity politics appeals]{.underline} in today's
multicultural world is **[because of the subject's
enjoyment]{.underline}** (jouissance) **[of it]{.underline}** --
enjoyment in Lacanese denoting the excessive pleasure derived from
something to the point of irrationality (Lacan, 1998: 183--4). 4
Accordingly, under a multicultural regime, [the subject is **seduced by
the prospect of** social and political **recognition and
recovery**]{.underline} (Thakur, 2020: xx--xxi; Žižek, 2006: 309).
[Intersectionality's libidinal reward can be seen in]{.underline} a
range of current socio-political occurrences: the (liberal) benevolence
of granting rights and [**rescuing** and rehabilitating **oppressed
voices**; the]{.underline} corresponding **[thrill of being recognized
by claiming identity]{.underline}** or minority rights; **[the self-
righteousness of asserting]{.underline}** one's oppression and
'**[victimhood]{.underline}**'; [and]{.underline}/or **[the narcissism
of acknowledging one's]{.underline}** personal guilt and
**[complicity]{.underline}** in social injustices. Of particular note is
'privilege theory' (Applebaum, 2011; Kimmel and Ferber, 2016), which,
while [bringing attention to privileged subject positions and
complicity]{.underline} in homophobia, patriarchy, or racism,
[can]{.underline} sometimes [create an atmosphere of **surveillance**
and **disciplining**]{.underline} ('privilege checking') [of both self
and other]{.underline}. As Zalloua (2020) notes, [there is]{.underline}
'[a kind of illicit satis- faction]{.underline} -- an unconscious
enjoyment -- not only [in]{.underline} exposing the blind spots \[and\]
. . . **[rhetorical disciplining]{.underline}** of others, but in your
own self-discipline, in your perceived suffering. . . . At its base
level, [privilege theory provides you]{.underline} with [the **fantasy
of intervention and action**; it offers you criticism]{.underline}
([attention to subjective violence]{.underline}) **[without
critique]{.underline}** ([occluding]{.underline} objective \[i.e.
[systemic]{.underline}\] [violence]{.underline})' (pp. 36, 18). [So
invested can people become in identity]{.underline} (their own and that
of others) [that they can get **bogged down in battles over recognition
and self-policing**]{.underline}.

[The libidinal complement]{.underline} to intersectional identity
politics thus [reinforces the culturalization of liberal democratic
politics. **Real social problems**]{.underline} (discrimination,
marginalization, racialization, inequality) **[are pressingly raised,
but]{.underline}** only **[superficially]{.underline}** or partially
[**acted upon**. People enjoy and publicize their critical
self-awareness, their empathy with the oppressed, and their]{.underline}
seemingly [progressive]{.underline} and humanitarian
[politics]{.underline}, but **[without taking any meaningful
action]{.underline}** (**[confronting]{.underline}** the **[systemic
barriers to]{.underline}** inequalities, **[or demanding collective and
material rights]{.underline}**, not simply individual civil and cultural
rights). [We feel good about naming our complicity in global
inequality]{.underline}, or **[we get off on surveilling others' lack of
'political correctness']{.underline}**, but [this only ends up
**reinforcing** the particularity, fragmentation, and depoliticization
that capitalist liberal democracies count on]{.underline}.

**The alt's refusal of humanism poisons its potential.**

**Boelens et al. 24**, Richard Boelens, Special Professor of the
Political Ecology of Water at the University of Amsterdam Centre for
Latin American Research and Documentation, Personal Professor of Water
Governance and Social Justice at Wageningen University, Ph.D. from
Wageningen University; Lena Hommes, Lecturer and Postdoctoral Researcher
in the Department Geography and the Institute of the Environment at the
University of Girona, Ph.D. from Wageningen University; Jaime
Hoogesteger, Associate Professor of Water Commons and Collectives at
Wageningen University, Ph.D. in Water Governance from Wageningen
University, "Ontological Politics in River Defence Debates: Unpacking
Fields of Contention in Eco-Centric and Non-Human Turns," Water
Alternatives, Vol. 17, No. 3, 2024, accessed 06-28-2025,
https://www.water-alternatives.org/index.php/alldoc/articles/vol17/v17issue3/762-a17-3-4/file,
DG

Catton and Dunlap (1978), among others, have challenged the social
sciences to engage in the urgent task of dismantling the \'human
exceptionalism paradigm\'. Since then, myriad bodies of
transdisciplinary sciences and activism have constructed important
alternatives to the Enlightenment ontology of nature as primordial,
autonomous, and mechanistic, open to human extractivism (cf. Goldman and
Schurman, 2000). In [the non-human turn]{.underline}, this [is often
translated into the need to \'de-centre the human\']{.underline}. We
strongly identify with movements fighting against states' and elites'
exceptionalist claims to having the right to destroy the planet and
accumulate \'natural resources\'. At the same time, however, we warn
activists and academics of the need for caution regarding how
popularised non-human and RoN/RoR policy debates ontologically and
generically tend to de-centre the human. One main reason is that
[human]{.underline} (elite) [groups certainly are exceptional in how
they have radically transformed the human/non-human milieux]{.underline}
(Grusin, 2015; Büscher, 2021). [This demands that we address the **roots
and impacts of this exceptionality, instead of \'ontologising it
away\'**]{.underline}. It also asks for political contestation and the
devising of strategies to make particular classes responsible.
[De-centring the human in policy and activist currents may]{.underline}
tend to [**create binaries** between \'generalised humans\' and
\'generalised non-humans\'. These]{.underline} binaries may [blind us
not just to the exceptionally destructive capacities of some humans and
their inventions, but also to the exceptional suffering of
others]{.underline}. At the same time, it might blind us from
recognising the exceptionally creative capacities that humans have for
reversing the transformation, not just of their own environments, but of
the planetary system as a whole.

[In]{.underline} mainstream [water policies]{.underline}, for instance,
[de-centring humans' exceptional agency has led to **overlooking** the
**complex lived realities of riverine families, livelihoods, meanings,
values, identities, and rights systems**. These policies]{.underline}
have [constructed their own river dwellers and water users, with
identities that conveniently fit the models and policies]{.underline}
(Roa-García, 2014; Hoogesteger, 2015; Hidalgo-Bastidas et al., 2018). In
this way, [they justify **dramatic interventions**]{.underline} (e.g.
Nixon, 2009; Sanchis-Ibor et al., 2017; Lynch, 2019; Aubriot, 2022;
Duarte-Abadía, 2023; Heikkinen, 2024). The [mainstream nature-science-
policy nexus **takes exceptional agency away from \'targeted human
beneficiaries\' and simplifies their ontological diversity in line with
expert notions**. It]{.underline} thereby [produces policy models that
**depoliticise their deeply political choices while dehumanising the
people they affect**]{.underline}. In fact, [they perceive and construct
abstract \'equals\' rather than recognising highly differentiated and
plural realities]{.underline}.

Brackel et al. (2023) provide a common illustration. Communities
affected by the Manila Bay Sustainable Development Master Plan
(elaborated by Dutch and Pilipino \'experts\') were reduced to \"people
in PowerPoint pixels\". These people, who would soon be displaced,
disappeared from view through the \'upscaling perspective\' of
hydro-territorial transformation programs (cf. Melsen et al., 2018).
[Actively de-centring the human, and]{.underline} thereby [constructing
ignorance, helps to \'rationally\' intervene in the complex water world.
This **de-humanises water reality**. Rather than revealing]{.underline}
the [on-the-ground realities, **only governable subjects and objects
appear: an empty space for constructing and ordering rivers, water
users, and identities**]{.underline}.

[This picture is the contrary of what the posthuman]{.underline} and
non-human [turns intend to accomplish]{.underline}. Nevertheless, [in
de-centring the human]{.underline}, many dam removal
[campaigns]{.underline} in North America and Europe tend to
[misrecognise]{.underline} the peasant [communities]{.underline} who
build their livelihoods on the small and medium- sized dam systems --
dams whose removal they project will \'enliven rivers\' and re-centre
nature (for critical reflections, see Sneddon et al., 2017; Drapier et
al., 2023; Harrington and Cantor, 2024). Hommes (2022: 604) shows how
communities along Spain's Mijares river fiercely contest dam removal
that would dry out a local irrigation canal: \"Destroying the Toranes
Dam is damaging this natural, territorial, and social ecosystem in which
we live, it is destroying an organised community \[in reference to the
irrigation community\]\". Another illustration is how RoN policy
advocates may unwittingly collaborate in new enclosures of riverine
commons (Duarte-Abadía et al., 2023). For instance, in the Andean
wetlands of Tungurahua, Ecuador, campesino-indigenous communities deploy
territory-situated conservation practices, but international
conservation agencies threaten them with RoN-based court cases that
could take away their ancestral governance rights if they fail to comply
with PES-based conservation schemes (pers. com. Juan Hidalgo-Bastidas,
10/04/2024). In parallel, RoN and RoR policies de-centring the human may
invisibilise powerful interests. Kinkaid (2019: 561), for instance,
shows how RoN imposition on the Hindu-religious rivers Ganges and Yamuna
by India's right-wing Hindu-nationalist government may lead to the
violent expulsion of Muslim and Dalit river dwellers (labelled
\'unclean\' tannery polluters of the sacred rivers) while leaving the
overall environmental status quo unchallenged.

In general, Büscher posits that, [for the purpose of understanding how
capitalism has made humans alienate themselves from
non-humans]{.underline}, \"\[...\] [it **makes little sense to decentre
the human**]{.underline}\" (2021: 7). [Because we live in increasingly
alienated interrelationships, we have to **re-emphasise
our**]{.underline} (**[human]{.underline}**) [**bonds with and
dependence on the rest of life**. This pays **genuine attention to the
agency, life worlds, and exceptional potential of highly divergent water
commons and collectives**]{.underline}. This [re-centring
would]{.underline} also [challenge any de-contextualised and detached
prescription of the diverse identities and nature of human and non-human
collectives]{.underline}.

### 2AC\-\--AT: anti-state alts

**The state will [inevitably assert itself]{.underline}, and [direct
action]{.underline} techniques are [insufficient]{.underline} to
resist.**

Michael **Murphy 19**, Ph.D. Political Science, McGill University; M.A.
Political Science, University of Western Ontario; Professor, Faculty of
Indigenous Studies, Social Sciences and Humanities, University of
Northern British Columbia, 2019, "Indigenous Peoples and the Struggle
for Self-Determination: A Relational Strategy," *Canadian Journal of
Human Rights*, 8(1), pp. 67-102,
https://cjhr.ca/indigenous-peoples-and-the-struggle-for-self-determination-a-relational-strategy/

Additionally, [not all **Indigenous peoples**]{.underline} are [opposed
to participating in **state institutions**]{.underline}. Some are, but
many others maintain that participation is a crucial means of shaping or
influencing state policies that impact their rights, interests and
well-being.21 Beyond this purely functional rationale for Indigenous
participation, [**evidence** from **multiple
jurisdictions**]{.underline}, including **[Australia]{.underline}**,
**[New Zealand]{.underline}**, [Canada]{.underline}, the
**[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates and [Latin American
countries sugges]{.underline}ts that [many Indigenous people
value]{.underline} their [engagement with]{.underline} the political,
social, and educational [institutions of the **wider
society**]{.underline}. [These **Indigenous peoples**
embrace]{.underline} their [status as **state citizens**
and]{.underline} as [citizens of their own]{.underline} self-determining
Indigenous [communities]{.underline}. Similarly, there is no consensus
among Indigenous peoples regarding the compatibility of Indigeneity and
the pursuit of capitalist modes of economic development. Many Indigenous
communities have adopted and modified capitalist economic development.
As a result, they have reaped significant economic benefits, but also
augmented their capacity to advance some of their key social, cultural
and political objectives in the process.22

Perhaps [the biggest weakness of a **resurgence approach** lies
in]{.underline} its [response to the **power imbalance** that profoundly
shapes **Indigenous-state relationships** worldwide]{.underline}. [While
this]{.underline} approach [accurately diagnoses]{.underline} the
severity of this **[power imbalance]{.underline}** -- what Coulthard
refers to as the "sheer magnitude of discursive and nondiscursive power
we find ourselves up against in our struggles" -- ultimately it fails to
offer a convincing strategy for counteracting its effects, and for
advancing an extraordinarily ambitious program of Indigenous economic
and political independence.23 A [strategic withdrawal from the
state]{.underline} may very well [aid the process of
**self-decolonization**]{.underline}, but [it will not stop state
judicial and legislative bodies from asserting their jurisdiction over
**Indigenous territories**]{.underline}, governments and economies. [Nor
will it stop state policy **governing activities**]{.underline} (e.g.
**[resource extraction]{.underline}**, industrial development,
**[environmental review]{.underline}**) [on lands]{.underline} adjacent
to Indigenous communities from continuing to impact Indigenous
territories, environments and well-being. Finally, [it will not change
the fact that many Indigenous communities will continue to be
**dependent on the state**]{.underline} for economic support, programs
and services and the building and maintenance of public
infrastructure.24

Indeed, [a wholesale retreat]{.underline} from state institutions [runs
the risk of increasing the vulnerability of **Indigenous
communities**]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} would [occur by ensuring
that **Indigenous people** have **no voice**]{.underline}, and thus, no
influence in those very same institutions (e.g. courts, legislatures,
constitutional forums, resource consent and co-management bodies) that
significantly impact their rights, interests and well-being. [Although
direct action can be an effective tool for advancing or defending
**Indigenous interests**]{.underline}, [past experience suggests that it
is **no magic bullet** in the struggle for
**self-determination**]{.underline}. Direct action campaigns are
difficult to sustain over longer periods of time because they sometimes
aggravate internal community divisions and alienate outside sources of
support and solidarity, frequently achieve only limited objectives and
in many cases, fail outright.25 The [limitations of direct action are
even more apparent for **smaller Indigenous communities** that lack
financial and **organizational resources**]{.underline} as well as
external allies. However, even in countries like Bolivia and Ecuador
where very large, well-organized and resourced Indigenous social
movements have made significant advances towards self-determination,
there is a perception that direct action on its own is inadequate to
achieve the desired scale of reform. This [perception led Indigenous
activists to pursue parallel and complementary **institutional routes**
to advance their goals]{.underline} -- most notably, **[electoral
politics]{.underline}**.26 Therefore, it is [crucial to understand both
the **advantages and limitations** of direct action]{.underline}, and to
acknowledge that this approach is [more effective as]{.underline} part
of a **[broader]{.underline}**, [multi-pronged strategy of]{.underline}
advancing **[Indigenous self-determination]{.underline}**.

#### Anti-statism performs an [ethical]{.underline} and [existential failure]{.underline} by abandoning the [ethical pursuit of revolutionary power]{.underline} through the [nation-state]{.underline}. 

**Dale, 23**---Lecturer in English at the Australian National University
(Amelia, "Nothing's More Precious Than a Hole in the Ground," Australian
Humanities Review 71 (May 2023), dml) \[language modifications denoted
by brackets\]

A Western turn away from internationalism and towards critiques of power
cannot be traced back to a single point: one cannot place it simply as a
result of, say, the New Left insurgencies, Foucault or indeed fractures
within or around the Soviet or Sino-Soviet projects. In our time [a
**great contradiction** has arisen between ethical
minoritarianism]{.underline}, in itself not a bad thing, [and any notion
of commanding the **machinery of state** through which
some]{.underline}, but not all, [of these ethical conundrums can be
resolved]{.underline}. [To frame]{.underline} the climate [crisis
through the lens of **individual** or even **generational** guilt and
argue it should be addressed through an **ethics of care** risks
**obscuring** both its material and historical **foundations** and its
potential **remedies**]{.underline}. John Frow, writing on the 'agents'
that hold some power over the 'transmission of a world' laments that
they are 'nation states, political parties, ... and only in the last
instance a citizenry with a stake in a future world' (26). Yet [it is
**vital** at this juncture that we **do not abandon** the idea of a
**nation** with a responsibility]{.underline} for its citizenry, or a
political party with responsibility [for the **future**]{.underline}.
[The globe is **not solely constituted** by]{.underline} multi-party
[democracies held hostage to fossil capital with a disenfranchised and

[other nations]{.underline}---marginalised and blacklisted as they might
be by Northern centres of capital and imperial power---[there are
**models worth considering**]{.underline}.

A [hostility to states and nations]{.underline}, while understandable in
the specific Australian sub-imperialist context, [**does not hold up**
to theoretical scrutiny if our horizon is equality]{.underline}: global,
intergenerational and otherwise. Samir Amin, for instance, notes how
[an]{.underline} '"**[anti-state]{.underline}**" [strategy]{.underline}'
[unites **perfectly** with the capitalist's strategy to **reduce**
actions by the state to **redistribute wealth** and **regulate
corporations**]{.underline}, [and instead **reduce** the state's role to
its **policing** functions]{.underline} (27). At this late stage of
ecological catastrophe, [the **mass extinctions** and **depredations**
of our **planet**]{.underline} under capitalism [demand]{.underline},
[as an **ethical imperative**]{.underline}, [**large-scale changes**
that can be **only** enacted on the level of the **state** and
**international blocs** with sufficient **organisational capacity** and
**drive** to bring such changes to fruition]{.underline}. Likewise, [a
total **antagonism** to]{.underline} '**[nation]{.underline}**' **[does
not get us anywhere]{.underline}**. Expressions of Australian

[informed by the **histories** of anti-imperialism and decolonisation in

[is to be aware of the **crucial role** of national liberation movements

**revolutionary**]{.underline}, [such as the national liberation fronts
in **Southern Africa**]{.underline}, [nations can be
**Indigenous**]{.underline}, [as with **First Nations**]{.underline}. [A

[it is on the level of **nation** that military and economic imperialist

resistance**]{.underline}. [Being unequivocally against

the role of the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates [as **military superpower** and **world

Unfortunately, the fall of the USSR and [historical
mistakes]{.underline} of socialism [have encouraged]{.underline},
generally speaking, a [drawing inward]{.underline} among Western
leftists, [towards **individual ethics** and away from the **ethical
pursuit of power**]{.underline}. The turn of the left away from

\[**[ignore]{.underline}**\] [geopolitical material forces and

#### We're anti-anti-state.

**Gordon, 24**---Associate Professor of Political Science and Africana
Studies at University of Connecticut (Jane Anna, "Creolising the
State?," Theoria, Volume 71: Issue 181 (Dec 2024), dml)

In Statelessness and Contemporary Enslavement (2020), I offered a brief
defence of the state in what I preliminarily termed an
**[anti-anti-statism]{.underline}**. I formulated it this way to
[acknowledge that]{.underline}, [given the **active complicity** of
states in the production of **statelessness** and]{.underline}
conditions that foster [contemporary **enslavement**]{.underline}, [it
is **not hard** to recognise the appeal of **anarchistic**
approaches]{.underline} amongst some of the most politically committed.
As was true in the first quarter of the twentieth century, contemporary
[states appear as primarily **predatory** and
**kleptocratic**]{.underline}, [either **unable** or **unwilling** to
counteract]{.underline} the [precariousness]{.underline} created by
current political economic developments and their violently xenophobic
expressions.

Still, I argued, as indispensable as [these insights]{.underline} are,
they [must be mobilised to formulate a **viable
anti-anti-statism**]{.underline}. After all, [conceiving global
responses to developments that have made vulnerability highly lucrative
**must involve governments** and **political
institutions**]{.underline}. What is more, in most versions
[anti-statism rests on a **logical circularity** in which political
institutions that are **not liked** are synonymous with **states** while
responsive]{.underline}, [legitimate ones are called **something
else**]{.underline}. [Rather than letting the exploitative
instantiations of the state or instances of state capture **monopolise**
and **exhaust** the term]{.underline}, [the state must also include the
deeply imperfect **New Deal** government]{.underline} of Franklin D.
Roosevelt [and experiments in **Caribbean socialism** that
produced]{.underline}, amongst other outcomes, the free medical training
offered by [the **Cuban healthcare system**]{.underline}. Surely
'**[states]{.underline}**' [include neo-fascist varieties]{.underline},
[political institutions that expand human freedom]{.underline}, [and
everything in between]{.underline}. If so, [the question is **not**
whether we are **for** or **against** states but about the **kinds** of
political institutions we need and **how** they are
constructed]{.underline}. As Alix Olson and Alex Zamalin write: 'If
democracy is intended to be enacted by the people, then the state itself
is ours to reimagine and those desires are to be collectively acted
upon' (2024: 151).

This observation informs a second pragmatic one. [We **cannot cede**
existing states to those with **no aversions** to most of the globe
living in unsustainable conditions of unfreedom]{.underline}. [This
refusal is not achieved only through interacting with states through
**established channels**]{.underline}. [It also entails engaging in
activities that make it **politically dangerous** for such bodies **not
to respond** and that gives **leverage** to allies on the
**inside**]{.underline} who already accept a conception of sovereignty
as realised across porous borders. Crucial to such an approach is A.
Naomi Paik\'s observation that the activism of the rightless proceeds
from understanding that [the state]{.underline} '[is **not** a
**singular entity** but an]{.underline} intricate, multidimensional
[**assemblage** of forces]{.underline}.' The resulting 'cacophony
marshals immense force, and yet, [the work of these many moving parts is
inevitably **incongruous**]{.underline}, [sometimes even in **direct

13).

#### Nation-states [inevitable]{.underline}.

**Malešević, 23**---Full Professor and Chair of Sociology at the
University College, Dublin (Siniša, "The Many Faces of Nationalism,"
Nationalities Papers, First View, doi:10.1017/nps.2022.114, dml)

Only [**twenty years** ago]{.underline}, many scholars interpreted
nationalism as an ideology experiencing gradual and inevitable decline.
Social and political [theorists]{.underline} from Ulrich Beck and
Anthony Giddens to David Held and Zygmunt Bauman [were **adamant** that
globalization had **dissolved** the borders of nation-states and
that]{.underline} "old style [nationalism]{.underline}" [had become
**obsolete**]{.underline} (Giddens Reference Giddens2002, 18). Even as
an astute thinker as Hartmut Rosa (Reference Rosa2013, 203--4) could not
escape this type of reasoning, as he wrote about the ever increasing
"denationalization" of societies and the "loss of the national state's
function in the age of globalization." Today, the focus has shifted in
the opposite direction and many scholars speak about the resurgence,
revival, return, renewal, or re-emergence of nationalism (Tamir
Reference Tamir2019; Hazony Reference Hazony2018; Judis Reference
Judis2018). For example, Tamir (Reference Tamir2019, 3) writes how "the
reemergence of nationalism has taken the world by surprise" while Hazony
(Reference Hazony2018) points to the "return to nationalism" that is
exemplified by "the British vote for independence and a nationalist
revival in the United States." The 2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine has
further intensified this view of nationalism's "re-emergence."

However, there is nothing new to these types of pronouncements, as
[intellectuals have been **writing off** nationalism since its inception
and have]{.underline} then [just as often been]{.underline} [**regularly
surprised** by its]{.underline} "sudden" and "unexpected"
[periodic]{.underline} "**[revival]{.underline}**." From Rosa Luxemburg
and Karl Popper to Friedrich Hayek, political [theorists have
**habitually dismissed** nationalism as a temporary anomaly bound to
disappear soon]{.underline} (Malešević Reference Malešević2019, 1--5).
As [this **does not happen**]{.underline}, many intellectuals express
astonishment every time nationalism becomes highly visible in public
discourses. Hence the focus usually shifts towards finding the immediate
causes of something that is in fact a longue durée phenomenon and, as
such, requires structural and historical explanations.

The classical scholarship on nationalism demonstrated a long time ago
that [such dismissive proclamations have **no foundation in historical
reality**]{.underline} (Gellner Reference Gellner1983; Anderson
Reference Anderson1983; Smith Reference Smith1986). Nationalism
developed quite late as a sociological phenomenon, but [once it took
hold of state power its growth was **largely continuous**]{.underline}.
[Nationalist doctrine has managed to **replace** competing ideological
discourses of state legitimacy]{.underline}, [including the divine
origins of monarchs]{.underline}, [imperial creeds]{.underline}, [and
the]{.underline} notion of a [civilizing mission]{.underline}, among
many others. From the end of WWII, [nationalism has gradually attained
many **hegemonic features** and has become the **dominant form** of
political legitimacy]{.underline} in the contemporary world. [In
addition to **acquiring state power** and **enveloping official
institutions** and **non-state organizations**]{.underline}, [this
ideology and social practice has **penetrated civil society**
and]{.underline} the inter-personal networks of **[everyday
life]{.underline}**. The [scholarship]{.underline} on everyday
nationalism [clearly indicates how **embedded** nation-centric thinking
is]{.underline} our daily practices (Fox and Iddriss, Reference Fox and
Miller-Idriss2008, Skey and Antonsich Reference Skey and Antonsich2018,
Knott Reference Knott2016). Hence [nationalism **never** experienced a
significant decline]{.underline}. Instead, [over the last **two hundred
years** one could witness an **incessant proliferation** of nationalism
across the globe and within different social strata]{.underline} of
individual societies. As I have argued before, [the historical
trajectory of nationalism has been shaped by **nearly
continuous**]{.underline} organizational, ideological, and
micro-interactional [grounding **across** time and space]{.underline}
(Malešević Reference Malešević, Höhne and Meireis2020, Reference
Malešević2019, Reference Malešević2013). [The **relative invisibility**
of this grounding]{.underline} and the lack of aggressive language in
the public discourse [have prevented many]{.underline} commentators
[from **adequately assessing** the actual strength of nationalist
penetration in everyday life]{.underline}. Thus, as [nationalism **never
went away**]{.underline}, [**nor** it was in **decline**]{.underline},
it could not experience a "re-emergence" or "return." [The recent
proliferation of aggressive nationalist discourses does not indicate
its]{.underline} "**[revival]{.underline}**," [it only makes ongoing
nationalist practices **more visible**]{.underline} in the public space.
In this sense, nationalism resembles the proverbial iceberg: many pay
attention to its tip when there is a threat of direct collision, even
though the iceberg has been there before and much of it remains
invisible to the human eye.

One of the reasons why intellectuals tend to regularly downplay the
significance of [nationalism]{.underline} is that this phenomenon [has
**many faces**]{.underline}. Nationalism has protean features
[that]{.underline}, like a chameleon, [can **easily adapt** to its
surroundings]{.underline}. Scholars have traditionally invoked the image
of the Roman god Janus to pinpoint the two contradictory faces of
nationalism - as a force of collective solidarity, but also as a
mechanism of group aggression against those that do not belong to the
nation (Nairn Reference Nairn2005). However nationalism has many faces
-- [it is a **multifaceted** political ideology]{.underline}, [a
**habitual form** of everyday practice]{.underline}, [and a **very
plastic** type of modern subjectivity]{.underline}.

[Nationalism is]{.underline} a **[flexible]{.underline}** discourse
[and]{.underline} social practice that [can coalesce with far right and
far left ideologies but can also accommodate moderate positions **across
the political spectrum**]{.underline}. Nationalist ideas are the
cornerstone of such ideologically diverse movements as the far right
Reconquete! in France and Finns Party in Finland, and far-left groups
such as the Catalan Candidatura d'Unitat Popular or the United Socialist
Party of Venezuela. Similarly nationalist principles feature prominently
in center right parties such as the Conservatives in the UK, Forza
Italia, or the Croatian Democratic Union, and in the center left
parties, such as Scottish National Party, the Bulgarian Socialist Party,
or the Danish Social Democrats.

Even more importantly, [nationalism is not just a **political
ideology**]{.underline}, [it is also a **social practice** that is
**integral** to many activities and processes present in a **variety**
of social organizations and outlets of everyday life]{.underline}. For
example, [nationalist practices are often promoted by **religious
organizations**]{.underline} (i.e. Serbian Orthodox Church, evangelical
Christian movements in the US, or the Jewish Home organization in
Israel.) Nationalist ideas can also underpin **[civil society
activities]{.underline}** (from ethnic minority NGOs and cultural
heritage associations, to national sport societies such as the GAA in
Ireland and Basque pelota clubs), **[private corporations]{.underline}**
(from selling distinct national products such as BMW and Guinness, to
promoting national tourist destinations such as the Taj Mahal or Hagia
Sophia), [and different **social institutions**]{.underline} (such as
masonic lodges, veterans' associations, or even nursing homes.)

[Nation-centric practices are also **integral** to everyday life and
habitual interactions]{.underline} between friends, family members,
neighbors, peers, and clan and kinship-based groups. For example,
wedding celebrations in the Balkans are often accompanied by patriotic
songs and the waving of national flags, while in Denmark birthday
parties regularly feature Danish national symbols, such as the
Danneborg. The intimacy of friendship is also often interwoven with the
shared experience of fervent cheering for one's national teams in
various sporting competitions. In this sense, [nationalism has become a
meta-ideological doctrine and social practice that
**infuses**]{.underline} many aspects of [everyday life]{.underline} in
the contemporary world. Hence to better understand this complex
phenomenon, it is necessary to explore these many faces of nationalism.

#### RETAG\-\--Native development is possible through the USFG

Kathryn C. **Lavelle 25**, PhD, the Ellen and Dixon Long Professor of
Political Science at Case Western Reserve University and the author
of *The Challenges of Multilateralism*. She has been a congressional
fellow and a residential fellow at the Woodrow Wilson International

Economic and political development in Alaska were the initial areas to

century[. Yet as development progressed, the contradictions be-tween the
promise of American democracy and its reality became painfully apparent
with the treatment of Native Alaskans. Alaska's towns and harbors
expanded in the years leading up to and follow-ing World War
I.]{.underline} Fairbanks was established in 1901. In 1914, An-chorage
was initially a landing site for supplies to build the Alaska Railroad.
Completed between 1915 and 1923, the new railroad linked the port of
Seward on the south coast with Fairbanks, more than three hundred miles
north.20 [As more settlers moved to Alaska, they came into contact with
the majority of the Indigenous population.]{.underline} By the end of
World War I, many of the legal categori-zations that had existed
previously, and had protected some people, were no longer recognized.
Children attended schools operated by the Bureau of Indian Affairs or
churches run on its behalf, and many schools exhibited an intolerance
for Native cultures and be-liefs, including abusive tendencies, as
existed in the rest of the United States. The Nelson Act of 1905 allowed
for exclusively white schools. [After World War I ended, the anti-Native
prejudice prevailed.21At the turn of the century, efforts at
organization and advocacy emerged within the Indigenous community,
although politics were not the original intent of the most prominent
among them. Formed in 1912 by Tlingit and Haida Indians, the Alaska
Native Brotherhood (ANB) and its auxiliary, the Alaska Native Sisterhood
(ANS), sought to educate and acculturate Alaska Natives as well as
advance the social and political interests of the territory's Eskimos,
Aleuts, and other Native Americans.]{.underline}22 The early leadership
of the organizations made advocacy for Native rights and legitimacy
their priority. In the interwar period, the Alaska Native Brotherhood
targeted issues connected to the fish trap as a unifying issue for the
pursuit of Native interests. [In 1921, their leader, William Paul, went
to Washington to testify on the issue and was elected to the territorial
legislature in 1924]{.underline}---the year all Native Americans were
granted citizenship. [The advance of the Alaska Native Brother-hood's
work in the 1920s and Paul's leadership is significant be-cause it
marked the first time that a Native Alaskan made their own case, on
their own behalf, to the federal government.23]{.underline}In the years
that followed, [the groups faced considerable opposition---particularly
with respect to extending citizenship and the franchise to Indians in
Alaska]{.underline}.24 [They also faced obstacles re-lated to the
original terms of the Alaska accession.]{.underline} For example, in
1934, [Congress passed the Indian Reorganization Act, which rec-ognized

had been no treaties or reservations there]{.underline}.[25The Alaska

to fashion legislation that would bring the act to Alaska and remedy the
discrepancy.]{.underline} In 1936, the Franklin D. Roosevelt
administration passed the [Alaska Reorganization Act]{.underline}, which
did so, albeit with some modifications.26
